Transcript Crump 202A-B										Narrator: Maxine Crump 4700.0117 Interviewer: Pamela Dean Session I Transcriber: Barrett Campbell 8 July 1992 [Begin Tape 202, Side A. Continues to Side B. Entire Session I.] DEAN: [006] This is Pamela Dean. It's the eighth of July, 1992. I'm going to be interviewing Maxine Crump in her office. We're going to be talking about her life as the first black student to live in a dormitory at LSU. Let's go ahead and do it. If you'd start off just telling me, for the record, your name, when and where you were born, parents' names, and what they did for a living. Crump: Okay. DEAN: And then we'll go into the more general recollections. CRUMP: I'm Maxine Crump. I was born in Maringouin, Louisiana, in 1946. My parents are Pauline and Emmanuel Crump. My father worked for himself. He did various things. He was a barber; he owned a nightclub; he was a school bus driver; he was a politician; he was a landowner. My mother worked at home, a housewife. And I'm from a family of seven, and there were six of us growing up . . . the seventh child was born when I went to college. The earliest recollection I have of my life was between three and five years old. And I . . . it's a scene of me walking along a canal . . . in the canal . . . in the fall, just to hear dry leaves crunching under my feet. And I knew my family didn't know where I was, but I knew I was safe. It was a dry canal. It was near the house. It seemed far to me, but as I've gone back and looked at it now I realize I wasn't very far away from the house. But I liked being alone in the woods. I liked hearing the sticks and the leaves crack under my feet, and I liked taking long walks. I've done it for years. I still do it. We lived with my grandmother and my aunt. My grandfather was dead. DEAN: Is that on your mother's or your father's side? CRUMP: On my father's side -- my father's mother. I had a brother one year older than me, Eugene. DEAN: So you were the second? CRUMP: The second DEAN: And the first girl. CRUMP: Then the first girl. My grandmother was a slim woman who was . . . you could almost call her . . . What is that expression they use for people who are her kind of slim? She wasn't slim like you think of slim women. She was almost leathery . . . just very . . .  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A no fat on her. That stood out to me because she was so slim like that. She had long hair, mixed grey, even though she was, I guess, in her seventies. Her hair was not totally grey yet. My aunt was, in my opinion, in those days kind of fat . . . because my mother was slim, so my aunt seemed fat to them. My mother was very slim. And my dad was always busy. He was in and out. My brother and I played together very well when he let me play with him. Mostly he had his own thing. He liked to play with a little boy up the road -- there were no streets, there were roads in the country. He played with a little boy up the road who was his age. That was our cousin, and his sister was older than me so, at that time, I didn't play with her, because I was too little for her, I guess. But my mother commented how my brother and I played well together. We never fought or anything like that. DEAN: Well, good. Crump: [52] We moved from my grandmother's house when I was five. We moved to a three bedroom house, and just before we moved, my sister was born, that was two years younger than me. Then the twins were born, and I was sent away when the babies were born. I was sent away one other time when my youngest brother was born. DEAN: Where did you go when you were sent away? CRUMP: To my grandmother's. DEAN: To your grandmother's. CRUMP: Which was a happy time for me because my grandmother was very quiet and gentle, and she took me to visit. My mother didn't visit anyone, probably because she had so many children. So I loved visiting. I loved to hear people talk. I loved to hear old people talk, and in those days, they seemed to just sort of say things like, "Yeah, you know." That's how the conversation sounded to me as a child. "You don't say." "Well, I never!" [Crump and Dean laugh] I loved it. It was very expressive because by the tones with which they spoke I seemed to get the message of the conversation. Sometimes they would say things they would say, "Oh, watch," and it was like I thought I had intruded on something I wasn't supposed to hear, which I had no idea what that was. But they said I looked like I knew. DEAN: Oh. CRUMP: I had a knowing look. I used to hear that. I remember hearing people say I was smart. That started registering to me very early that I was smart and that I was finelooking -- not pretty, or not cute, but fine-looking. DEAN: What did that mean?  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A CRUMP: To me, it meant that I had a solid look that wasn't pretty, because I . . . it finally registered that pretty was what my sister was . . . dimples and curly hair and brown skin. I was sandy and light-eyed, which was unusual for blacks. So it was sort of pointed at like, "Well, her eyes . . . " It was just "her eyes". It wasn't really said what they were. Sometimes people would say, "Her eyes are pretty," but mostly it was a reaction to the color of my eyes being light, golden. They were lighter than they are now. So I would remember hearing being called "sandy" and that sort of thing. And . . . DEAN: Was this good or bad? Or just different? CRUMP: It was just different because no one said it really negatively. So I took it as just different, and I decided to like it. [Crump and Dean laugh] I was aware, though, that sometimes I wished that it would get me what my sister got me . . . picked up more. She got picked up and swung around and played more, and I felt like, I must look heavier and solid because I always had bigger feet and big legs. So I didn't look like I had the cute curly feet like little girls normally have and the slender little legs. I always had big legs for a little girl and big feet and hands. So I just felt like that didn't look like someone you pick up. Since then, I've been picked up many times and played . . . [Crump and Dean laugh] But I guess it was because something I wanted and finally met men who weren't afraid to pick up women with big legs. Maybe because I never really got heavy. I grew up to my legs. DEAN: You grew into them. CRUMP: That's it, I grew into my legs. I can remember the first year . . . Well, in the three room house . . . DEAN: Where was this you moved to? CRUMP: It was about a mile away from my grandmother, up the road, which was considered in the town. My grandmother was in the country; we moved to town . . . still on the bayou. And . . . DEAN: [101] Why was it you had made this move? Had your father taken on another job or something of this nature? CRUMP: Oh, good question. My mother really got sick, and her cousins who were twins and both nurses came to visit. They gave her a lot of books on health and raising children, and she did everything in the books. This was in the fifties when the . . . when the . . . DEAN: Right. CRUMP: . . . war, well, yes, late forties. World War II was over. They had all of these new ideas about how to take care of children. She went germ-free crazy. All the books  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A said, "boil everything", and my mother did all of this stuff perfectly and got very ill because she tried to de-germ everything in my grandmother's house for her babies. My grandmother wasn't raised in that germ-free era. Although my grandmother was clean, she didn't understand. "What is all of this boiling about?" My dad's cousin came to visit from Plaquemine, and she told my dad, she said, "Your wife is getting very, very ill, and if you want her you better take her away from here because it's killing her here." She didn't like my grandmother or my aunt, she said they were mean, thoughtless, and didn't help her, and didn't appreciate what she did. So Dad moved her. I think he had been trying to save enough money to build a house, but he decided to move into a house that the family owned already. Since it was just he and his sister and he was in charge of everything, he sort of took care of his mother and his sister. So that's when we moved. My mother . . . well, when we moved in the three room house, it was a . . . two bedrooms and a kitchen. So my sister and I slept in the room with my parents in a foldaway bed. It folded into a couch during the day. My brother . . . when the baby was born, he slept in a baby bed in that same room by the window. The twins slept in a roll-away bed in the back room with my oldest brother. Children can adjust to anything. DEAN: Yes. Yes. CRUMP: With the washing machine! DEAN: Oh, yes. Well. CRUMP: There was a hole in the floor, and a pipe was put through the floor to drain the water out. There was no actual plumbing in the house. There was plumbing in the kitchen for water, to the sink. And that was it. We had an outdoor toilet. When we took a bath, the water was drawn in the bathtub, and we all took a bath in that tub. DEAN: What was the order of bathing? Do you remember? CRUMP: I think from the oldest to the youngest. I think . . . I really . . . it didn't stand out to me. It was just always the way it was so I didn't notice. We took a bath in the kitchen. And then the water was, I think . . . when I got old enough, I would help my brother carry the tub out and dump it. It was what they called the number three tub. The number two tub was a foot tub, and the number three tub was a bathtub. Then there was the water bucket with the dipper, and that was for drinking. Then we had a refrigerator. In 1950, we got a refrigerator -- a General Electric. The radio was on top of the refrigerator. [Phone rings] DEAN: Do you want to get the phone? Do you want me to turn it off? CRUMP: Yes. [Break in Tape]  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A CRUMP: Our outdoor toilet, we would whitewash it with lime. It kept it from having a smell. It was what prideful people did. They whitewashed it. Because it looked nicer, and it showed something about quality . . . the image I picked up. DEAN: And it was more sanitary. CRUMP: It was. It was more sanitary. DEAN: Which your mother must have . . . CRUMP: She was Miss Germ-Free! [Crump laughs] DEAN: Hard to do without lots of running water and plumbing and so forth. CRUMP: She sacrificed greatly for it. We would . . . [coughs] At this time, my dad had a night club. And, so he would be gone about five o'clock in the evening. So we saw him late mornings because he slept late because he was up late. And then he was gone, because he also had gardens, and he was always gardening and that sort of thing. So he was in and out. We started collecting lumber to build our new house, and it looked like that went on for years. He was collecting the old rafters, so that it could be built with the most solid wood, that you no longer could buy, and beams and things like that. We would polish the furniture with lamp oil. We had to sweep everyday, and sometimes several times a day. My mother got us all involved in housecleaning. We even brought flowers in. We would pick wild flowers to put in . . . She would always say, "Now see, doesn't that look nice? Don't you like the way that looks?" I soon learned to wash dishes by standing on a chair. [Crump and Dean laugh] At the time, the road in front of our house was gravel, and they . . . somewhere in along there they started paving the road. Fortunately, they started in the summer so I could watch it every day. It was like my mission to get up and watch the road being paved. The school that I went to when I started school . . . I remember when my parents took me to school the first day. I got out the car and didn't look back. I just ran to see who I could go find to play with. That was my experience my first day of school. No separation anxiety whatsoever. I liked school very much. DEAN: Describe . . . How large a school was this? CRUMP: [184] It was a four-room tin building with a kitchen. Not a dining room, a kitchen. The desks, in the first grade, were wooden desks with the desktop. It wasn't movable -- the tops weren't movable. They were built almost like a shield that came out and around where you slid into your desk on the side, and then you had your table built in. But, I know they have now desks that you can open and move down. It wasn't  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A anything like it was made like that. You just had to slide in. My teachers, . . . Well, the rooms there was the first grade, the second grade, the third and fourth grade, which were in the same room. The fifth grade was at a community hall down the street, and the sixth grade was in a church up the street, a mile away. They would walk to lunch. Lunch was off the back of the first grade classroom -- how miserable. The first graders had to smell the lunch cooking, but we did get served first. DEAN: Well, there's something there. How many students were there in your class? How large do you think it was? CRUMP: I think twenty. DEAN: And this was a segregated school? CRUMP: Yes, all-black elementary school. The whites went to an elaborate, it looked elaborate to me, brick school, that started first grade and went through the twelfth grade. DEAN: Are we surprised? CRUMP: Big campus, huge trees, school buses. We didn't have school buses at that time. DEAN: And how far was this from your home? CRUMP: A mile. When I was in the first grade my parents drove us to school. I think first and second grade, my dad took us to school everyday, and after that we walked. I don't remember . . . the road was gravel when I first started walking, so the pavement wasn't up yet. Then when they did put the pavement it was only halfway. So once we left the pavement, we were on gravel again. My first grade teacher was very old. She wore a hairnet and cotton stockings and lace-up shoes and black dresses, mostly, with tiny little flowers in it, down nearly to her ankles. I think she had false teeth. They looked like false teeth. Her name was Miss Baptiste, and she wore spectacles. She was the original image of a teacher. She liked to drink RC Colas. DEAN: [Crump and Dean laugh] You painted a very vivid picture there. I can see it, starting from the feet up. CRUMP: [228] She taught the basics. She was very much for repetition and rote. I got my first image of my reading ability from her. She read a story to the class one day, and she said, "Who would like to tell me the story I just read?" I raised my hand. I came up, and I told the story. She was so proud. I had no idea I had done anything outstanding. She held me by my shoulders and stood behind me as if, "This is my prize. Now class, do you see how well she did that?"  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A Dean: Very nice. Crump: All of a sudden I thought, "Oh, I like this." I had a cousin who taught school. My cousin visited us one day, and my mother said . . . I suppose I told my mother about this. My mother said to show cousin Leona how well I could read. So I got my book, and I was reading for her. And my cousin Leona said something that just echoed in my head and imprinted as believability to me, because it felt good. When I finished reading she says, "Oh, my little cousin can really read!" I must have fluffed like a peacock. So I started getting the image that I read well. And, my, when I got . . . when, when . . . in the first grade . . . my brother had failed the first grade, or was . . . or that was the second . . . or she taught the second grade, too. I don't remember. I think he had failed the first grade, but he wasn't in my second grade . . . but he was in my class for a while. I'm vague on that. My second grade teacher appeared to be tall and slim. Bony almost, not skinny, though. She had something looked strong about her. She dressed a lot in beige three-quarter length dresses with belt of the same fabric as the dress. She had very pretty hair. It was always shiny. Sometimes she would pull it back into a bun, but she didn't look like an old teacher. She looked young with it back like that. Other times she would wear it wavy in the front but usually close to her head and smooth. She wore very nice shoes, high heeled pumps, and they would be reptiles and things like that. It's amazing that those teachers dressed so well in a four-room tin building. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: Because a lot of them did, and there was no air conditioning. DEAN: That was my next question. It must have been very warm in there, too. CRUMP: Yes, and I notice that teachers now dress very casual, especially if they're going to be in any hot situation. But her clothes were dress clothes, I could tell. They weren't flashy, but they were professional clothes. She wore powder, and her blush appeared almost burgundy. Her lipstick too, was very dark, but very becoming on her. She had small lips, and she spoke very well. Everything was articulated perfectly. And she didn't have very much trouble disciplining her class because she was very strict. If she walked in and we were hanging off the rafters, she would simply say, "Sit down, and shut up." And everybody did. [Crump and Dean laugh] DEAN: What happened if you didn't? CRUMP: Everybody did! DEAN: The question never arose! CRUMP: That never came up. She did give whippings, in the hand. She didn't yell and scream because she didn't have to. She just said it very firmly and everyone followed. But  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A we got whippings for not doing our work. I remember getting a whipping once, and it was . . . I got a whipping with a string of kids. It hurt my feelings because I did not want to disappoint her, more than I cared about the pain in my hand. It was that I had met the discipline of what I knew better than to do. I followed the crowd. One other time I didn't do my homework because I had lost my pencil when I was playing at home one night. And my other brothers and sisters weren't in school. We had a three room house, and so I wanted to play. She asked me why didn't I do my homework. I had started and didn't finish . . . she said, "Why didn't you finish?" I said, "I lost my pencil." She said, "That is not an excuse." I had somehow knew she was right. It wasn't a good excuse. I was hoping she would buy it, but somehow I knew that not to ever sell that one to anybody. So I never tried anything like that again. [304] When I got to the third grade . . . Oh, in the second grade, I remembered, they gave us all . . . they gave us all blood tests for something they thought was going around. Somebody had something at the school, and I don't remember what it was, hepatitis or something, and they gave us all of the blood tests. They really did it awfully. I know that it was probably, probably the best they could do at that school. They set up the stations on the grounds, and it was like a series of tables, about four tables, and the kids were lined up in front of the tables to get the vaccinations. None of us could remember having had blood tests before. So we were all afraid. They described, "It's going to be . . ." They tried to be real . . . I guess . . . DEAN: Reassuring? CRUMP: Reassuring about it, thank you. Describing to us what it was going to feel like. It didn't help. It made it worse. So they were all crying and screaming, and one little boy, he had reared [up] and cried so they had actually scarred his arm with the needle. I saw him with marks on his arm, and I then became frightened. I wasn't frightened until I saw that. By the time they got to me, I was crying too. I remember the man said, "Are you going to stand right here in my ugly face and cry?" And I agreed, he was ugly. His teeth looked like they were decayed and had coffee stains. DEAN: Oh, dear. CRUMP: But now I realize they weren't decayed, they just were positioned badly in his mouth. It did distract me, though, because the blood test didn't hurt at all. DEAN: Good. CRUMP: I was really never afraid of shots before, but a blood test was different. I hadn't had one of those. I wasn't the type who cried when I got a shot. I thought to myself, "Why am I crying? Because everybody else is crying. This must be bad!" We used to get our . . . to get our . . . that's when they were giving measles shots and vaccinations and all that started coming in. My mother used to walk with us to the clinic, which was just a few blocks up the road, to get all of those shots, and I never cried. You know, kids would be falling out, and I was like,"It doesn't hurt."  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A In the third grade, I met a teacher who was twenty-four years old. She didn't look young to me. She had gold teeth. She had several gold crowns in the front of her mouth, and she grinned a lot. She didn't smile; she grinned. Because she was usually making fun of people, and she made fun of the kids a lot. She would walk in, if we were clowning in class, she would say, "Heathens! Heathens!" It may have quieted us, but it shamed us, too, and embarrassed us. At least that's how I felt. She taught the third and the fourth grade. So I had her for two years, and I was not someone she liked. It was the beginning of me losing my spirit, to me. I felt I had to defend my life from her. And I would tell my mother about it, and my mother didn't do anything about it. She didn't . . . She says, "Well, I'm not going to go down there and have them talking about me and saying that I'm causing trouble at the school." And I, that, that became very vague in my mind as to why my mother didn't come and stand up for me. I had no one to stand up for me. I thought that she was always on me. [357] She said I looked like death on a soda cracker. I don't know what that had to do with anything I did. I know I did nothing to get that name, but that's what she said to me one day. She whipped me all of the time for talking. The kids found out she liked whipping me. She would assign one of us to take names, so they always put my name down whether I talked or not. Once I discovered they were going to put my name down anyway, I talked all the time, and she whipped me all the time, and I had never cried. She said I took it like a soldier. But I just wasn't going to give her the pleasure of crying. Then she did the ultimate. They were going to have a school play. I didn't know what a school play was, but it sounded like fun to me. They described it. It was going to be Snow White. There were two girls in the class that were light-skinned, me and my cousin. My cousin was in the class ahead of me. So they wanted a girl that was light-skinned to play Snow White. They asked for me, and she said, "Oh no, she doesn't speak loud enough." I knew I could do it, and I wanted to say, "But I know I can do this." So they put my cousin Betty in the play, who really was more Snow White, she even had the black long hair. [Crump and Dean laugh] So I didn't get that part, and I knew she was wrong about me. It's like it stayed in my mind, that determination, because I knew I could do that. It wasn't until they built the new school, which was when I was going into the fifth grade . . . when I was in the third grade, it seemed, the fourth grade, they started building a new school. By the time I finished the fourth grade, I was so glad, because in my mind, somewhere along the line, I had wished I'd never have to go to the hall or the church. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: That didn't seem like school to me. Something about that would have done something to me. I didn't ever want to do that. By the time I got ready to go into the fifth grade, they had the new school built. It was a brick school with corridors. It wasn't indoor halls, like the white school, but they were a much older school. We were built on a modern style, where you had the corridors outside. DEAN: So this must have been about what, '54? '55? Someplace in that?  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A CRUMP: Fifty-five. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: So, in the fifth grade, I was in a new school, all new desks, everything was new but the books. There were no new books. I didn't know that there was such a thing as getting new school books. I didn't know that until I was in college. New school books? Our books were always recycled, always, always. My fifth grade teacher was a tall, slim, pretty woman, who wore skirts and blouses because that was popular in the fifties. The full skirts and blouses, and that's how she dressed. Very neat. It looked like she ironed every morning because she wore crisp cotton. It looked like she stayed fresh as a flower all day. And she wore the nicest cologne! I wish I could find it today. I've never smelled anything like she used to wear. I'm sure it was her chemistry that made it smell nice. DEAN: Probably even if you found it, it wouldn't smell the same as you remember it. CRUMP: That's true. DEAN: Nothing, never, ever comes close like that (?). CRUMP: Another thing that was fascinating about the school, the four-room school, that the principal that was in the four-room school decided not to remain as principal when we moved to the other school. DEAN: Was this a man or a woman? CRUMP: It was a woman. She was the most interesting woman. Her name was Geneva Mildred Williams, and we used to call her Mrs. G.M. She was short and foxy. She was curvy, and she had a curvy behind and curvy legs. She wore slip-in shoes . . . bare- back . . . sometimes they would have, she had some that . . . she wore a lot of black. Black knit. Knit dresses must have been rather expensive then because it wasn't a common fabric, but she wore knit dresses; fitting, scoop neck, low back, with cap sleeves, and big loop earrings that would almost touch her shoulders because she was short, and she had a short neck. She had very short red hair. She walked with her shoulders back, and she carried her head back. She wore orange lipstick, and she spoke extremely well, very articulate. All of the kids respected her. We were afraid of her, especially when she put her glasses on and put them down on her nose. It's not that she did very much whipping, she would just hit her strap on the wall and speak strongly, and everybody would just . . . but I, they would say, "Ooh, Miss Williams is tough." I was thinking, "Oh my God!" If I had to be in Miss Williams's class, I'd be afraid, but for a little kid, she frightened us a lot. But she laughed a lot when she would say mean things . . . but we still took her seriously. She was not nearly as mean as she sounded, but we thought she was kind of mean. Yet we liked her. It was a funny mix because if we would walk by, we would just walk by and say, "Hey Miss Williams," or "Hey Miss G.M.," and she'd always say, "Hey, baby." I  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A thought she was pretty. They used to wear the stockings with the seam, and hers were always straight. Those are the things that I noticed. DEAN: [451] Yes. So you were talking about moving to the new school, she didn't? CRUMP: She did not move on as principal. They brought in a principal . . . a man . . . a tall man who carried one shoulder up and one down. He always wore a suit, and he sort of walked to one side. He had big feet and big hands, and I can remember seeing that. I liked my fifth grade teacher. She seemed to be smart. She taught us a lot of things. She taught geography and reading, and she always put interesting things on the wall. This was new. We didn't have this the first four years. We had bulletin boards, but they didn't stand out as much because the rooms were not "stand-outs," but we had real things, and we would help put up the bulletin boards. I was very active in school. DEAN: Was she a new teacher? CRUMP: She was a new teacher. All of the teachers were new except the one that had taught me in the third and fourth. She was still there. Miss Baptiste had retired, and Miss Bell, the second grade teacher, moved on to the new school . . . still teaching second grade. The rest of the teachers were all new. DEAN: Do you have any sense of what their educational background was? CRUMP: Yes. They all had completed college. The substitute teachers . . . many of them had not. They couldn't find any. You know, they didn't have enough people. If you had had some college, you could do substitute teaching. The rumor was that Miss G.M. really didn't have a degree . . . that she had forged it. She had a shady lifestyle, which, which, which I, I, I am grateful for because she gave us a realistic image of life. She didn't have to be perfect to teach us. She was a good teacher. She really was very thorough. People used to . . . people today say they learned so much from her. She's dead now. They're all dead now, except Miss Hall, the one that I was telling you about in the fifth grade. Miss Hall's son was in my class, and he was a very cute boy. It used to hurt my feelings because she would whip him if he didn't do well. I felt like she whipped him because he embarrassed her because he wasn't smart and her daughter was. So I always felt sorry for Jerome. DEAN: When you say whip, precisely what do you mean? CRUMP: She whipped him in his hand . . . with a strap. DEAN: With a strap, okay. CRUMP: There were a couple of teachers who would hit kids about the shoulders, but they were considered the bad kids, and it was not a pretty scene. It felt like a lynching to me, when I saw kids getting whipped that way. I didn't like watching kids get whipped.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A DEAN: This was done in front of the class? CRUMP: [504] Yes. Sometimes outside, in front of the whole school, because kids would gather around. Mr. Williams, the principal, was that kind of man. He was crazy. The man should have been put away. No question. I remember one day a boy broke the fire alarm. He called the whole school out and made us stand in the heat, while he stood in the shade. He had two baseball bats in his hand, he said, "If I find out who did this, I'll bat their heads." We all knew who did it, but no one was going to tell because we weren't going to have the boy's head batted. Well, he wasn't going to do that, I'm sure, but he was . . . he would threaten us in those ways. But I saw him whip children in ways that you don't do. He should have been arrested. He had a teacher's meeting, and he held it a long time. And we had the most interesting boys in the class. They could get up and entertain us better than any stand-up comedians that I can think of. They were so funny. I would come home and tell my mother and father, and they would laugh. That's how funny these boys were. So they would get up and entertain us if the teachers were gone a long time. He came back, and they had windows on the outside that were really big windows, but then on the corridor side, there were windows up high, and they could pull in a draft across the room, which helped a lot. Well, these were open, and he could hear when he came down the hall this comedy show going on. So he listened for who all he thought it was, and then he walked in on them. He picked them up by their shirt and their pants, and he threw them outside. He pitched them. He pitched them like animals, which wouldn't be a good idea to do to animals . . . like sacks, he pitched them outside. Then he told them, he said, "I want . . . ", this is unbelievable . . . I can not believe he did this! He told them, "I want you to crawl from one end of the corridor to the other, and I mean crawl. Don't stop until I tell you!" While they were crawling, he was hitting them. It's, it's cruelty, abuse, battering. He did it, and the teachers stood by and nobody reported it. I would tell my parents, and they didn't do anything. Nobody did anything. This guy continued there until he started stealing, and they found out he was stealing. Then they got rid of him . . . he had finally broken a rule. You can beat the kids, but you can't steal. DEAN: Goodness. CRUMP: Yes, goodness. DEAN: Such a message. CRUMP: We were heathens. We were mosquito-bitten bayou heathens because the teachers were from the city. Only Miss Bell lived in the country, the second grade teacher, she lived up the street from us. Miss Hall moved out there for a while. DEAN: Now, were they from that area? CRUMP: No, they were all from the city. DEAN: They had all come, and . . .  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A CRUMP: None of them were from that area. DEAN: Being from the city means being from Baton Rouge? CRUMP: Baton Rouge. DEAN: So they really were urban, and they were outsiders? CRUMP: [569] Yes. They considered they had been sent to the country to save the heathens -- the mosquito-bitten heathens. Those from our area that had gone on and completed college, which I had a half-sister who had, had moved away. Yes, she taught in California. So no one had been educated there. In fact, my sister was the first graduate of the high school. What year did she graduate in? Fifty-something. The first graduate of the first black high school in my town graduated in the fifties. DEAN: I believe it. CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: I believe it. CRUMP: So the teachers had to come from somewhere else because there was no one there who had an education. Those who had been sent away to school to get an education stayed away. They didn't come back. What was there to come back to? Because we were taught to look down on the country. The country was the place to be embarrassed to be from. You always loved to look forward to your cousins who came to visit from the North or from California because they were the only ones who were anything. We weren't considered anything. We were . . . we were ashamed of ourselves, being from the country. DEAN: Those with, with get-up-and-go had gotten up and gone long before. CRUMP: [595] Yes. Gotten up and gone, yes. So my parents were determined that their children were going to "make something out of themselves," which was the country expression. "Yes, she sure is making something out of herself." So, yes the teachers were all from Baton Rouge and considered themselves well above us. When we didn't do our work, they would say . . . if we were making noise and they were writing assignments on the board or something, they'd say, "Look, let me tell you something. If you don't want to do your work, okay, because I have mine. I have my education. You've got yours to get." It wasn't very inspirational to me, though. Somehow that didn't make me feel -- I didn't connect with that. It just sounded like an insult. It didn't sound like a motivation. I remember they would do things like have what they called the "dumb row" which I didn't see it motivating kids, either. I saw it embarrassing them and shaming them. No one ever got off the dumb row. So it couldn't have been very motivational.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side A DEAN: No. CRUMP: It was just meant to punish and shame them, so forth. In the fifth grade, they started a school chorus, and I tried out for it. Only two of us in the fifth grade got selected for the school chorus. So I felt that there was some validation for me that the fourth grade teacher had said I couldn't do. It wasn't until the seventh grade when Miss Williams, who had stepped down as principal, was teaching the seventh grade still . . . the one that we were afraid to go to. By the time I got her in the seventh grade, she was no longer frightening to me. I wasn't a first grade kid thinking she was mean. She did tryouts for a play, for a Christmas play. The first time, because you see, until then, all of the kids were the same kids every year, and all the teachers were the same. They would just ask the next teacher, "Who was good in the last school play?", and they would just follow along. So you never had a chance, you had, your, your role was set. Well, Miss Williams did auditions, and I auditioned and got the part -- not the lead, but the next part. I went for the lead because I knew I could do that, but since I had never been in anything, I got the next part. DEAN: [654] Let me stop just a minute, I need to turn this.  [End Tape 202, Side A. Begin Tape 202, Side B.] CRUMP: [031] Okay. You want more coffee while I'm up? DEAN: No, I'm fine, thanks. It's very good coffee. CRUMP: I made it. DEAN: You did darn good. CRUMP: Thanks. So I was so excited about getting the part in the play . . . I did terrible in rehearsal, though. And when I was getting ready for the play . . . I was going to play a magistrate in the Christmas play, and I had to wear a towel on my head, but I had my mother to do my hair anyway. The towel was supposed to be the magistrate's headdress or part of the robe look. But the night of the play, I made not one mistake. I spoke loudly enough -- we had no microphones -- and I got laughs and applause. DEAN: Wow. CRUMP: And I helped the lead character with her lines. DEAN: Trying . . .  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: I was on my way. No one was ever going to say I couldn't do this again. I got put in plays after that, and I got asked to do readings after that. I was just known for that. I had proven, proven myself to me -- and everyone else. DEAN: Terrific. CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: So that overcame some of the negative kinds of messages you'd been getting in that context. CRUMP: Yes. Yes. I came out, I came out of flatness then. I started having friends. It seemed like in the third and fourth grade I was always battling for a place in line or something. Someone was always knocking me out of line or something. It was a big deal to get first in line after recess and that sort of thing. Someone was always elbowing me out of the line, and I was seen to be just this little ball that was trying to find my way. I was always getting at the end of the pop-tail, which was a game we used to play. The one at the end got whipped up the most, you know, got thrown a lot. Kept missing in jump rope, kept, kept getting my legs swatted with the rope when we would do hot-cuts. Until I got to the fifth grade, and all of a sudden it was like . . . I could do anything anybody else could do, except play softball. I was never any good at that. I couldn't catch, so I got selected last on the team all of the time as a result, which made me only a worse player. I play softball just fine now. But I started having friends, and we got into talking about records and movie stars and lipstick and things like that . . . I was good at all of that. And tv shows and who was cute and boys and books and things like that, and . . . DEAN: You had a television? CRUMP: [062] We had a television when I was in the sixth grade. In the fifth grade, we would watch television at my cousins' house. The reason we had a television is because my dad had a nightclub, and he had a television. So we got one at the house when I was eleven. That was fun. It was really, as I look back, it was great that we didn't have one before then because we were very creative in our play. We didn't have a lot of toys. We got toys at Christmas, and when they were gone, they were gone. We made up toys the rest of . . . we played hopscotch. We rolled tires. We shot marbles. We rode . . . we did have a bicycle until we broke it, but we'd fix it and ride it again. And we cooked grass. We made up ways to cook outside. We built houses from the lumber and the tin Dad collected to build the new house when we were in the old house. We would set up playhouses with that and use bricks. We did the nine yards, and we played in the bayou. Sometimes it would dry up, and we would play in the middle. It would create an island, and we would play in it. We'd wade through the mud. They had snakes in there, but we didn't notice them. DEAN: Woo!  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: Yes, I know, "Woo!" Cottonmouth moccasins were in that bayou. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: My mother told us not to go in it, but we went anyway. She didn't know we were there. DEAN: Nobody ever got bit. CRUMP: No, no, not one even got their foot cut in the bayou. We got lots of injuries. I jumped up to get some elderberries once, and jumped down on a nail. Went into my heel, all the way in. I didn't go to the doctor. They put turpentine on it. Healed it. Oh I got more cuts and bruises, and scrapes and scars . . . it was just part of it to me. I would get my foot cut. I'd come in and get it treated and go back outside. No one ever got stitches. No one ever had an arm or leg broken. [088] My youngest brother had spinal meningitis when he was a baby, and we all had to take medicine because it was contagious. I remember we prayed every night for his recovery, by candlelight. The twins were three, and they were with us, too. I used to have to take my medicine to school. The teachers would give it to me. This is when I was . . . before the fifth grade . . . this was, I was still in the first when this happened. In those days, it was very rare that you recovered from spinal meningitis completely, but he did. He recovered completely, and the doctors said it was a miracle. I remember, my mother didn't go to the hospital with him . . . my dad . . . they went to the hospital in New Orleans because there was no Charity in our hometown and in Baton Rouge. It was only in New Orleans. It was a storm that night. It was the first storm I can remember. It was a storm brewing. It hadn't quite hit yet. And my dad and my uncle drove my brother to the hospital. There were no ambulances. The doctor had said, "Get him to a hospital right now." They drove so fast, they said they know the car left the ground several times . . . with the storm, and with the breeze, and all that. DEAN: Wow! CRUMP: My dad stayed with him, and my mother said she needed to stay with us. DEAN: Right. CRUMP: I think it needed to be the reverse, but they didn't think so. They gave him so many shots, he had a sore on his butt when they brought him home. DEAN: But it was a miracle. CRUMP: It was a miracle. He was fine. He went on to be a star football player. Now he has his own children, so yes . . . he did fine. He had no nothing, no deformities, no retardation, nothing. DEAN: Amazing.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: It was amazing. DEAN: [113] Was religion an important part of life? CRUMP: It was indeed. I remember making my first communion, and it was a big deal. In the summer, they had summer school for the Catholics. My brother was going off to summer school the first summer, and I wanted to go too, but I wasn't old enough to go yet. I went anyway because I insisted. I had heard my mother talking about the nuns so much, and they put me in a class with a lay teacher. I didn't want her. She couldn't know anything. I wanted a nun. But they said the nun was teaching the first graders, the second graders, because they were going to make their first communion. I insisted, and I cried. So they said, "We can't do anything with her. Sister, you come see." The nun came over, she says, "Well, let me see." So she asked me some questions from the catechism, and I knew all the answers. So she said, "Okay, come on in." She put me in the second grade class. DEAN: How had you learned the answers? CRUMP: I knew them from listening to my brother when he studied and my mother would teach him. When they gave out the first communion certificates, they called my name too because my name was on the list. My mother said, "Just go get it and give it back to them after church." So I was in the back of the church, and I remember I had on a hat. I walked from the back of the church to the front to get it. Priests never knew anything, they never knew kids' names. Nuns did, but to priests, children were just there. You didn't count. Maxine Crump . . . You've never seen me in the first communion line, but it didn't matter to them. They just did things by rote. I went up and got it and gave it back to him afterward. Yes, religion was very important. We had to say our prayers every night before we went to bed and every morning when we got up and grace before every meal. After we made our first communion, we went to church every Sunday, every Holy Day. We fasted on Friday. There was no meat on Friday. During Lent, it was one full meal a day. You could eat light meals the other two. We did all of that. DEAN: Was this an integrated church? CRUMP: Yes. It was integrated/segregated. It was the only Catholic church in town. The blacks sat on one side, and whites sat on the other side. DEAN: Was there a large Black-Catholic community? Or was this . . . CRUMP: Yes, it was large. It was just as large as the white community because in the town, there were more blacks than whites. DEAN: Were there Methodist, Baptist churches . . .  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: . . . that other blacks went to? CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: Was that a larger percentage of the black community? CRUMP: Yes. Yes, the larger percentage of the black community was Protestant, but there were quite a few Catholics. Yes, I would say it was just as many black Catholics as there were white. We sat on one side, and for a while, we had a third of the church. So it was a lot of standing, jammed up situations. Finally, it was moved to half. It wasn't integrated until I went to college, and it was integrated because I sat where I wanted to when I came home. Whites didn't sit on the side where blacks sat, but I sat over there. So did my whole family. They just didn't bother me. It just didn't make any sense to me. It still somewhat . . . DEAN: I sense you didn't have a lot to do with that kind of pattern. CRUMP: Didn't have a lot to do with it, yes, didn't. They still sit rather segregated in the church. It's kind of integrated, but it's still kind of segregated in the '90s. They all going to get to Heaven, I'm sure. They say, "If God would have meant us to be together, he'd have put us together." DEAN: Looks to me like he did! CRUMP: [168] Where did he put us? I remember asking my mother one day, "Mother, why don't white people like black people?" She said, "It's because they're ignorant." That's all she said. We lived next door to a Methodist church, and the family of the minister lived in the house next door to us. As the minister changed, then we got new neighbors. We always got along with them. We always liked them, but I always knew that they were Protestant which wasn't as good as Catholic . . . that God was going to let them in Heaven in spite of themselves, not because of themselves. I understood that. But we were nice to them, even if they were less than us. They were very nice Protestants. We lived on a corner, so the people across the street from us were our cousins. Cousin Corrine lived in an old Acadian- style house high up off the ground, high ceiling. We would love to go to her house, and she would always fix us Kool-Aid and cake or ice cream and cake. She had a tv before we did and a telephone. Not that we used it, we just liked to hear it ring. [Crump and Dean laugh] She was just real sweet to us, just real sweet. She was raising her brother's daughter. She was a little older than us, but she was very nice to us. So we just liked to go visit her and sit in the swing on the porch. It was great. Oh, I could swing all day. Then next to them was Mr. Buster, who had just moved there from the plantation. He had paid off his plantation dues, and finally was moving up to the city. He had sharecropped  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B until his debt was paid, and he had moved up to the city from the plantation. He brought his whole family, and we used to say they still lived plantation-style. They would walk to town with no shoes on, walking on the back of their shoes, slips hanging, hair not combed, and we used to think, "Oh, how awful." But it wasn't long before they figured out that that wasn't the way we lived in the city, and they changed. They started combing their hair, wearing shoes, and nice clothes. They had very pretty skin . . . smooth skin, all of them. And Mr. Buster started keeping his yard up immaculately. He was the janitor at the school, and he kept our school so clean. Our school was so clean. He was such a good janitor. He kept the schoolyard cut so beautifully. I never knew to appreciate him then, just took it for granted, but they were very nice neighbors. Next to him was a white family, and the man would speak to my dad. My dad said, "He speaks to me because he wants me to vote for him," because landowners could vote, and my dad was a landowner. Everybody couldn't vote, but landowners could. So Dad said, "He speaks to me because he wants my vote." He said,"But I don't vote for him because he hates black people, and I know it, and his family does." Well, they passed our . . . DEAN: [211] What was the position that he would have been running for? CRUMP: Alderman. DEAN: Alderman. CRUMP: City Council. His whole family -- he had two sons and a daughter -- and they passed our house every day, and did not even look in our yard. Did not even look in our yard. Mother said, "You think they'd look if the house was on fire?" We used to laugh and say, "No, they wouldn't." The oldest son was throwing rocks at my oldest brother one day, and my mother went out and stopped him, and he said, "He threw at us first." And Mother said, "He didn't. He wouldn't do that." She said, "Well, just don't do that again," and they didn't. So that was the only conversation I can remember between the rest of the family. But they never looked at us. The daughter was my age. Next to them was another white family, and they were very nice. They had moved there. They were not from there, and they would always say hello to us. Next to the church was another white family. They were very friendly. Behind us was a white family, they were considered poor whites. They walked around with dresses that didn't fit on the waist, bare-footed, kept a dirty house. I think the husband drank. Mother used to say, "You know they have nothing, and they are considered nothing among whites, but they know they're better than us." So I lived in an integrated community in a way. I had the experiences of whites in a way, and across the bayou was an affluent white family, and the man owned a store, and we used to go to his store because it was right up the street from us. So whenever we wanted . . . and he was very nice to us, and he always said we looked real nice. Occasionally, he would give us candy or something as we went there. He always said we were very nice, said, "You-all are very nice. You dress so nice." His wife had a drinking problem, but she would go on drinking bouts. She didn't drink all  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B of the time because she was a teacher. When she would go on drinking bouts, she would just take off sick leave and drink the whole time. Then she would get over it and go back to work. A woman worked for her, and she was young, a little younger than my mother, but she was single, which was rare. She was early thirties but single. Didn't happen in Maringouin. That was considered "Can't get anybody" Well, she wasn't considered attractive, so I suppose that was it. She lived in a little house on the premise. Since she was right across from us and she walked to work . . . or she would walk where she went otherwise because she didn't have a car. She and my mother visited a lot and they became very good friends. It was the first time my mother really started to recover. She had been sick a lot of years, and she became very happy. Those were happy days for me, and I'm convinced that when parents are happy, children are happy. Children really do feel their parents at a certain age. We just remember having very good times in the '50s. Times got very affluent. People bought cars. People bought new clothes for holidays. It just seemed to be a bustling time. [259] My dad had his nightclub. His business was going very well. He had a speaker on the outside of his place, and he would put money in the jukebox and keep it playing and people would think it was a lot of people there because they could hear the music, and they would come. So it worked . . . until they passed an ordinance that you couldn't have outside loud noises like that, but that was a long time. I remember we would sit outside at night. We would hear his music, sort of, just the background of it. Like now, when people drive down the street and you hear the boom boxes -- that's the way it sounded coming from his nightclub. DEAN: Bass. CRUMP: Yes. We would sit outside and build a smoke to fight the mosquitoes so that we could be outside. We smelled like smoke afterwards, but we could survive the mosquitoes. It was better than being in the house. But I remember the '50s as a lot of fun, a lot of good times. When every year the cars were totally different and my brother and I used to go up to the Chevrolet places to see what the new cars were like as soon as they came out. "Let's go see the new cars. The new cars are in today." DEAN: They kept getting longer, and . . . CRUMP: Yes, with more chrome, heavier bumpers. DEAN: Yes. Yes. CRUMP: So . . . DEAN: I need to take a short break and visit the restroom. CRUMP: Sure.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B DEAN: That's what coffee . . . [Break in tape] CRUMP: [279] My dad had a seventh grade education, and my mother had a fifth grade education, but they were very interested in education. My mother could have gone as far as she wanted in school. She could have even gone to college had she wanted to. UNKNOWN MAN: Thanks a lot, ma'am. CRUMP: You're welcome. She became ill, she said. She had to have her adenoids removed and things like that. She said she had dizzy spells, but the story doesn't wash with my sister and I. We believe she had all that, but we believe she didn't want to go to school because her eyes were crossed. We think that that was it, that she got teased and she never wanted to admit that that was it. She created the rest of this to get out of going to school . . . that was our belief. She has since had her eyes fixed, and they really weren't crossed that bad. She had what you call, I guess, a lazy eye or a wandering eye . . . wasn't exactly cross-eyed. DEAN: Right. Yes. Yes. Would there have been easily accessible high school for her? CRUMP: Oh, no, it wouldn't have been easily accessible, but her parents would have made sure she got there because her cousin who was her same age did get to high school and as a result, had lots of opportunities. If you were black and went to high school then, your opportunities were great . . . in the black community. DEAN: But you had to really be motivated. CRUMP: You had to be really motivated, and she was not. Teachers even lived at her home, and they could have taught her. They always rented rooms to teachers, so surely they could have helped. But she and my dad were always fascinated by people with educations. People would often ask them if they were teachers or ministers because with my dad owning his own business and being able to go places during the day, during the week. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: Well, if you were a black man, and you were dressed, and you're speaking well during the day, you must be a teacher or a preacher. So they always would say, "Oh, well, are you a teacher or are you a preacher?" Always, always, always. He would get so tickled at that because every other able-bodied black man was working and he was not -where he could walk in town, especially to Baton Rouge. DEAN: Right.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: There were no others in town during the time when we would go. Sometimes I would get to go with my dad or something in the summer, and you just didn't see them. They were working. DEAN: Yes. Yes. CRUMP: So they were always fascinated by education and by teachers, and there was an encouragement to get an education. It was always there. It was always that as blacks you were going to need an education. I always knew that. Then they started saying that they didn't want us to just be teachers because everybody was a teacher. Surely there was something else we could be. That started coming in in the '50s, everybody thinks that you ought to be a teacher. Well, I knew by then I wasn't going to be a teacher because it seemed to be losing its credibility somehow. DEAN: Well that really had been the one option, really, for educated blacks. CRUMP: It had been. Yes. And my mother used to say "I wish you could . . . " DEAN: For women, for that matter. CRUMP: And for women that's exactly right. My mother used to say, "I wish that you could get a job like those ladies in the offices . . . one of those office jobs." Which there weren't, then. DEAN: Right. CRUMP: But I just remember her saying, "That looks like that would be a nice job . . . in one of those offices." My dad was always going in business offices or something. My mother would say, "Oh, that was such a nice looking lady. She had her makeup on and her hair done and her nails done. I mean she gets to go to work dressed up everyday." I was like, golly, sounds nice to me. So I remember hearing a lot of talk about people who were getting an education, who was getting to go to college . . . that got to be real big because then there was Southern . . . and I don't remember when Southern was there, I just remember hearing the talk of "going to Southern." People started getting an education, graduating from college, and this seemed to sort of get in there. By the time I was thirteen, I knew I was going to college. It was like I knew that . . . I was sure of it. DEAN: And the assumption at that point was that it would be Southern. CRUMP: Yes, there was no other alternative. DEAN: Right. Right. CRUMP: That assumption was all the way until I graduated from high school. It was assumed that I was going to Southern.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B DEAN: Right, right. Shall we move on to that moment of decision? CRUMP: It was a very simple moment. [Crump and Dean laugh] That's a very simple moment. DEAN: You graduated in the spring of `64, and it was in 1964 that a number of people had brought suit against Louisiana State University to have them admit undergraduates. Now they had been admitting, under court order only, a few blacks since the mid- fifties in the graduate program. CRUMP: In the graduate program, exactly. DEAN: And every step of the way had been resisted by the university, by the Board of Supervisors. Were you aware of any of this that was going on? CRUMP: None of it. I was unaware of all of it. I was aware of the civil rights movement, but I didn't know about LSU in particular. DEAN: [364] Well, let's stop a minute and talk about that. What about the civil rights movement were you aware of? CRUMP: I was aware that my dad had always talked about civil rights. It was just a natural part of what I knew. Men would come to the house and talk about it to my dad, and I would hear them. They were just always trying to get rights, just trying to get just ordinary rights. I remember seeing drinking fountains and restrooms, and my dad kept me away from all of that. We never went to anything that said "Colored Only". Never. If we couldn't use the one that said "White," we didn't use any. We never stopped anywhere that wouldn't serve blacks. We never went to the side window that was designated for blacks. We were not going to do that. That was out. I remember one day, we all wanted a hamburger, and Dad stopped the whole car. He says, "If they'll serve you at the front window, you can get it. If not, you get back in the car." I was designated, somehow. I was voted out among the backseat to go to the window. I knew why they voted me because my sister was brown, and she would have been recognized first. It was like, you know, among us kids it was like, who would have a better chance. It's night, and they might not know, and they'll serve at the front window. We'll get this hamburger, you know? All we wanted was a hamburger. We did not want to integrate, you understand. We did not want to make a civil rights statement here. We just wanted a hamburger. So, I went up to the window, and I was looking at the whites in the line, it was like, "Oh, please don't recognize that I'm black and start a mess out here." Because there were two windows at the front, so I went to the window that was behind one other person. At the side window, I was hoping that the blacks didn't see me and think I was causing trouble and making trouble for them or better than them. Oh, I was just in a dilemma. I just wanted a hamburger. DEAN: How old were you?  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: I think about nine or ten. So we got the burgers and got back in the car. DEAN: You did it. You did it. CRUMP: Yes. They just couldn't see that I was black because those Dairy Queen windows were . . . they had paper with all of the ice cream sales on the front, and they just raised the window and look out there. It was night, and I just kind of got away with them not knowing. But my dad felt triumphant that they had served us at the window. DEAN: That's a pretty heavy thing to put on a nine-year-old. CRUMP: Yes, yes . . . but he thought it was even heavier to make us go stand at the side window there . . . that it was more demeaning. DEAN: How did you feel at the time? Did you think he was right? CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: Or all you wanted was the hamburger? CRUMP: I really thought it was right. I think I'd have felt terrible standing at that side window. I really do. I knew I didn't want to do that either. I just didn't want any trouble at the front window. DEAN: How did you feel about how much that must have limited the things you could do if you couldn't acquiesce? CRUMP: I remember feeling the limit, and I felt the limit most of all when I got a tv, and I could see what was possible in the world. I wanted those possibilities, and I didn't like the limits. The older I got, going past from age eleven to twelve, the limits really started to bother me. I was limited by the fact that I couldn't buy hosiery to match my skin. That there was hosiery for black people, and the rest of the shades were white people. I wasn't the color that the stockings they had for black people. There was makeup for black people, and I wasn't that color. So it was "What is this?" DEAN: So you couldn't be wholly in either community? CRUMP: No. DEAN: You really were, by virtue of your skin color, in the middle. CRUMP: Yes, yes. The limits were there, but I didn't know whether there was going to be a change. I don't remember thinking of the possibilities for those limits, but I somehow wished for them . . . wished for more. DEAN: There were no civil rights demonstrations, sit-ins around that you were . . .  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B CRUMP: Not directly in the town at that time. Not until the sixties when we boycotted a store that had blacks only bagging groceries. The day of the demonstration, the day of the boycott, they put the bag boy on the register, and it was that way ever since. Now, the store manager is black. That same store. DEAN: When was this? When was this? CRUMP: [443] This was in sixty- . . . It may have not been until '65 because it seems like I wasn't living there at the time. It was after I went to LSU. Yes. The thing that my dad did, a lot of work was going on in Plaquemine, and that was still the same parish. The Civil Rights Act, Voting Rights Act passed, and that's when he started the campaign of just getting people to go vote. DEAN: He did? CRUMP: Yes. He did that every day. He spent every day talking to people about voting. They didn't want to. They resisted. They were saying, "What good is one vote going to do?" and, "Are they going to give me a test?" He says, "No, you don't have to take a test anymore. Just sign your name." Some of them couldn't sign their names. They didn't want to admit they couldn't sign their names. He would say, "You just put an X." It was a very, very hard task. It was very difficult for them to understand. A lot of people were very resistant, a lot of black people. DEAN: Was there any overt white effort to suppress that? CRUMP: Not that I was aware of. DEAN: No threats? I mean, he owned his own business. So he was less vulnerable than a lot of other people would have been. CRUMP: Yes. That's right, right. He and his cousin, who also worked for himself, themselves, were the major people in it. There were two other men in town, who also owned their owned their own business, and they were a paramount in it. It turns out, it ended up being a faction anyway, because there were these two blacks who were pushing for voters on one side, who were going to elect the white people they wanted, and Dad was pushing voters on his side to elect the white people he wanted. It wasn't to elect black people. It was to get the white people you wanted, the ones that were going to be more favorable. The two men that Dad felt were opposing him wanted the white people who were going to make sure they got kickback on money. Dad had wanted the white people who were going to do more for black people. So that was the push there. And that was the major . . . DEAN: One, one faction wanted to play politics Louisiana- style. CRUMP: [Crump and Dean laugh] And the other were real interested in civil rights. The one that the civil rights interest wasn't the money pay off. Because I remember a man  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B came down to the house one day and wanted to talk to Dad, and Dad said "You-all go inside." I thought, "I don't know why he wants me to go inside. I want to hear this." So I just went back to the window and listened to it. The man offered Dad some money to vote, and Dad said, "If you'll make out the check to the Community League ," he said, "then I'll accept it, but I will accept no money for me. And I will talk to them about endorsing you," (They had formed a community voting league.) "only if you will make these promises." When Dad finally did run on his own, and anyone questioned his integrity, I knew his integrity from having heard it when he didn't know I was listening. To this day he doesn't know that, I never told him. But I remember because I wanted to know what it was that Dad didn't want me to hear. I did ask him, finally, what didn't he want me to hear? He said, "Oh, sometimes those men use bad language." I'd think, "Well, what do you mean? I go to school, I hear all this all the time." It was simple as that, he didn't want us to hear bad language. But, you know, growing up in a town where the police are white. Everyone of any position was white. The mail carrier is white. The postal workers were white. The store owners were white. Everybody was white but the undertaker because whites didn't want to handle black bodies. And the nightclub owners, because they did not want to socialize with them. Other than that . . . and the hairdressers were black because they didn't want to do our hair. That's it. You could only have the jobs they didn't want. I remember my brother asserted his civil rights, he went into a store, and they didn't wait on him, and mother had sent him for something. He came back into the house, and he sat down. He did the defiance stance, sit, that I found out lately . . . He threw his leg over the arm of the couch, which is rebellion, and I didn't know that at the time . . . he was sitting like that. Mother said, "Where is what I sent you for?", whatever she said. He said, "I didn't get it." He was a little kid. He may have been seven. She says, "Why didn't you get it?" He said, "Because they waited on somebody ahead of me." She said . . . I don't know whether he said he told them and nothing happened, or whatever, but that's why he left because he was there first. [Crump and Dean laugh] He came home. So those are the kinds of stands we used to make sometimes. We were always afraid that they were going to pull that one on us because it used to get pulled on kids all the time. We knew that we could not come home and tell my dad that we had stood for that and did nothing. We somehow knew we were to stand up for ourselves. We were just hoping no one would pull it, so we wouldn't have to say, "I was next." DEAN: Did you ever have to do that? CRUMP: No, I didn't. I used to position myself to make sure I didn't have to. Just stand real close to the counter and look in their faces. They would have to say, "And what do you want, little girl?" [Crump and Dean laugh] "I'll take that, just don't skip me." But, the, the actual, the civil rights was just like that. I remember there were the Clouatres. They were white, and they used to sit on the black side, on the colored side, then, it was. They took a stand, and they would sit over there. We were in the choir, and so they joined the choir because the choir was all black. The  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B priest was black at the time, and so the altar boys had to be black and the choir was black because whites were not going to serve him. They took communion from him somehow though. DEAN: Sure. Well, he was a priest. CRUMP: He was a priest. Yes, he was a priest, but they were not going to have their children be altar boys of his, and they were not going to join his choir. He looked white. You would not have known he was black unless someone told you. DEAN: It was known. CRUMP: But they knew he was black. Yes. Occasionally they would have him in their homes. They really would. So it was . . . DEAN: For dinner? CRUMP: Yes, for dinner. They did. Oh, he was the priest. Some of them would; some of them would. DEAN: That's really a major factor, thing, isn't it? CRUMP: It was, yes. That's right. DEAN: Yes. CRUMP: I had never been in but one white person's home in that town . . . DEAN: Really? CRUMP: . . . the front of it. That was Miss Smith, who lived across the street who had the woman that worked for her. The reason I was in the front was when she was drunk, she would send for us to come in the front. She even let us play the piano when she was drunk. [Crump and Dean laugh] Other than that I could come in through the back and go in the kitchen. DEAN: Right. CRUMP: So that was a big deal, you know, that you went to the back. Now I don't go to the back of anybody's house. Some people say, "Oh, I thought you were ringing the back doorbell." Blacks, too, will say that. I say, "No, I thought maybe you'd be busy in the kitchen. I came to the front." I just don't like going to the back anymore. They don't understand they're just going to have to unlatch that front door because I'm coming to the front.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B DEAN: Of course, where I'm from in Maine, nobody uses the front door. CRUMP: Really? DEAN: Everybody goes in the back door, you just . . . CRUMP: It's considered sociable, right? DEAN: Frequently, frequently. Sure, I mean, frequently the front door sort of hangs there in midair without really stairs or steps to let you in that way. CRUMP: And really . . . that's why people use the back now, it's just informal. DEAN: It's just, I mean, everybody does that. CRUMP: Yes. Yes. I just don't have the old . . . DEAN: I'd think you were rather strange if you came to the front door. CRUMP: Yes, well, they do think I'm rather strange. DEAN: The significance there is different. It's quite different. CRUMP: Yes. Yes. Well it's, you know, it is now, and people think I'm still a little strange coming to the front door. "Oh, gee, I heard the bell and thought you were at the back." "Well, no, I thought maybe you were busy in the back." I'm out of coffee. [611] But the actual decision to go to LSU just came one day when I was outside, and my dad says, "well, you know LSU's integrated now, and would you be interested in going to LSU?" I said, "Yeah, I would." Just like that. DEAN: Now you had gone to segregated schools all the way through high school? CRUMP: Yes, yes. One of the things that had happened, though, that made the decision that simple for me, is my mother used to hassle me, at least it seemed to me, I would hear her talking about girls who had gone to college and came home pregnant and how wild she had heard it was at Southern. She had heard that some of the teachers weren't competent, and that your education really wasn't valued once you got out of there. That had started happening in the '60s. It wasn't valued by white companies, and people were starting to try to apply to white companies. So those were images that I had heard about Southern. I felt that if I was going there I was going to get less than a valued education. That had been in there, sort of sitting, but I didn't have another option. So when he said LSU, I knew the credibility it had. So it was a sort of easy decision for me. The desegregation part of it, I felt like, "What could be any harder than what I had already gone through in the church, in the town, going to back doors? How could LSU be any harder than that?"  Crump, Maxine - Tape 202, Side B DEAN: Despite the fact that you knew you were going to be one of a very, very small minority in . . . CRUMP: Yes. Yes. I thought, "So what? I've been a minority all my life!" It was nothing. I didn't see where it was going to be anything hard about it for me. As far as there being all white -- that hardly bothered me. I had been treated black all my life. I had been treated less than. If I could get a good education out of it, that's all that mattered to me. I didn't care about the fact they weren't going to like me. I decided I'd like them. I never disliked white people, no matter what they did. I didn't see where they had done anything for me to dislike. I just saw them making ignorant decisions. My mother told me it was ignorant early on. So I didn't know what there wasn't to like. I didn't like what they did, but I didn't dislike them. My mother had told me that my heritage was white, that my great-grandfathers on both sides were white. It made it kind of hard for me to dislike them. DEAN: Let's put a new tape in here. CRUMP: [665] Okay.  [End Tape 202, Side B. Begin Tape 203, Side A.] DEAN: [005] Alright. We're back again. CRUMP: My dad do and say things like when he had his nightclub, he would have his deliveries made at the house. Because a lot of the nightclubs in town were also stores, and so they were open all day. So they made deliveries there. Dad's was only a straight nightclub, and it was open only in the evenings. So his deliveries would be made at the house. We would sign for products and things like that, and all of us were designated to do that. Dad would tell us how to behave with them, you know that we were to be polite, we and we were not to accept any mistreatment. Well, none of them mistreated us. They were all very nice to us. But I remember they would say things to him, like they would call him "Hoss". He would just call them "Hoss" back. I never understood what the "Hoss" was, but he said it was something white men did to black men is call you "Hoss". So he called them "Hoss" back. I remember there was a man next door that was knocking on the neighbor's door. He asked Dad, "Do you know if Willie Hall is home?" And Dad says, "No, but his wife is there." He said, "But I don't know her name." And Dad said, "Her name is Mrs. Hall." Those are the kinds of things he used to have to think to do. The man looked really baffled. He's like, "I'm not going to call this black woman Mrs. Hall, excuse me." So he just left. He just walked away. My mother never told them her first name, so they would just say good evening. She wasn't going to have them calling her Pauline. Anyone else, but not . . . it was for the reason that they wanted to use her first name. DEAN: Right, right.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 203, Side A CRUMP: So those are the kinds of little things I used to see my parents having to do, and Dad never said "Mr." anyone. He just say, "I don't 'Mister' no white man." Because it was required to say, "Yes sir" and "Mister," and he wasn't going to do those things. So he didn't "Mister" anybody. Everybody was whatever their first name was. Of course, they first named him, but he first named them, too. Unless they called him "Hoss," then he called them "Hoss", too. [Crump and Dean laugh] It's only funny now. It's really terribly stupid, you know, that that kind of oppression was even leveed on anyone for whatever reason. So after living through all of that and seeing all of the things that I saw, what could LSU do to me? DEAN: You didn't think it would be more fun to be in a group of . . . CRUMP: Black kids? No. DEAN: Black kids like yourself where you . . . CRUMP: [044] No. No, because my mother was probably going to send me away with so many restrictions. They weren't going to let me live on campus at Southern because they didn't trust the dormitories there. At LSU, they were going to let me live on campus. I was looking at the advantages. I'm going to be away from home, be living on campus. At Southern, I'd have to commute. The thought of commuting just . . . I could imagine myself being tired because I was anemic. So tired was something that I got very easily. The thought of traveling back and forth in the car to school everyday was just tiring. To think of coming home and studying college work in the midst of my brothers and sisters who were in high school and elementary school . . . DEAN: Had your father gotten the house built by then? CRUMP: Yes, the house was built in '57. DEAN: So you did at least have a little more room? CRUMP: Yes, I had more room, but still not enough for college work. I just felt like college needed to be totally separate. Something in me . . . DEAN: Not with six kids in the family. CRUMP: [052] Yes, yes, I wanted to be away from there. I had had it. I was done with that. My brothers were wild. The fact that I was going . . . When Dad took me to LSU and I saw the campus and I saw how pretty it was, I wanted to go there. I didn't think about how I was going to be treated because that . . . I saw what I was going to like about it. I saw a pretty campus with good buildings, and I saw the dormitories. They looked good and clean and solid, and I was going to have my own room. That's all I thought about. I was going to be going to a school that's credible and one that where my mother trusted me to live on campus without the restrictions. When I was in high school, I didn't get to hang out with my friends anyway, except at school.  Crump, Maxine - Tape 203, Side A DEAN: Oh really? CRUMP: Yes, so I wasn't used to a big social life anyway. DEAN: [065] Had you done any dating in high school? CRUMP: Very little. Very, very little. There, I think boys had come to the house, and I had gone to the prom. I'd get to go to some games and see them there. No one had picked me up and took me to the game. Once, in [my] senior year, when our class gave a supper, I got to go for a ride in the car with my boyfriend and my girlfriend's boyfriend, and we had more fun! Just fun, you know, it was like, "What does my mother think is so bad about this?" [Crump and Dean laugh] And I got to go on the senior class trip but not with my boyfriend, I had to ride the bus with the rest of the kids while he drove in the car because my mother didn't trust me to ride with him. DEAN: Where was the trip to? CRUMP: A beach not very far from us. It was a day's bus ride. It was nothing. It wasn't even a nice beach. It was a made up beach. DEAN: Well, there aren't any beaches in Louisiana. CRUMP: Yes. It was the only beach the blacks could go to. We couldn't go to the other beaches in Louisiana that were also made up. This one was even worse made up, but we enjoyed it. DEAN: Well, we've been going for quite a while now. Do you want to stop now and set up another time? CRUMP: Sure, we can do that. DEAN: To, to go on with the LSU, per se? CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: Is that all right with you? CRUMP: Yes, that would be fine. DEAN: [083] Good. Good. [End Tape 203, Side A.
