Transcript Crump 203A										Narrator: Maxine Crump 4700.0117 Interviewer: Pamela Dean Session I Transcriber: Barrett Campbell 8 July 1992 Maxine Crump 4700.0117 Tape 203 Side A DEAN: [005] Alright. We're back again. CRUMP: My dad do and say things like when he had his nightclub, he would have his deliveries made at the house. Because a lot of the nightclubs in town were also stores, and so they were open all day. So they made deliveries there. Dad's was only a straight nightclub, and it was open only in the evenings. So his deliveries would be made at the house. We would sign for products and things like that, and all of us were designated to do that. Dad would tell us how to behave with them, you know that we were to be polite, we and we were not to accept any mistreatment. Well, none of them mistreated us. They were all very nice to us. But I remember they would say things to him, like they would call him "Hoss". He would just call them "Hoss" back. I never understood what the "Hoss" was, but he said it was something white men did to black men is call you "Hoss". So he called them "Hoss" back. I remember there was a man next door that was knocking on the neighbor's door. He asked Dad, "Do you know if Willie Hall is home?" And Dad says, "No, but his wife is there." He said, "But I don't know her name." And Dad said, "Her name is Mrs. Hall." Those are the kinds of things he used to have to think to do. The man looked really baffled. He's like, "I'm not going to call this black woman Mrs. Hall, excuse me." So he just left. He just walked away. My mother never told them her first name, so they would just say good evening. She wasn't going to have them calling her Pauline. Anyone else, but not . . . it was for the reason that they wanted to use her first name. DEAN: Right, right. CRUMP: So those are the kinds of little things I used to see my parents having to do, and Dad never said "Mr." anyone. He just say, "I don't 'Mister' no white man." Because it was required to say, "Yes sir" and "Mister," and he wasn't going to do those things. So he didn't "Mister" anybody. Everybody was whatever their first name was. Of course, they first named him, but he first named them, too. Unless they called him "Hoss," then he called them "Hoss", too. [Crump and Dean laugh] It's only funny now. It's really terribly stupid, you know, that that kind of oppression was even leveed on anyone for whatever reason. So after living through all of that and seeing all of the things that I saw, what could LSU do to me?  Crump, Maxine - Tape 203, Side A DEAN: You didn't think it would be more fun to be in a group of . . . CRUMP: Black kids? No. DEAN: Black kids like yourself where you . . . CRUMP: [044] No. No, because my mother was probably going to send me away with so many restrictions. They weren't going to let me live on campus at Southern because they didn't trust the dormitories there. At LSU, they were going to let me live on campus. I was looking at the advantages. I'm going to be away from home, be living on campus. At Southern, I'd have to commute. The thought of commuting just . . . I could imagine myself being tired because I was anemic. So tired was something that I got very easily. The thought of traveling back and forth in the car to school everyday was just tiring. To think of coming home and studying college work in the midst of my brothers and sisters who were in high school and elementary school . . . DEAN: Had your father gotten the house built by then? CRUMP: Yes, the house was built in '57. DEAN: So you did at least have a little more room? CRUMP: Yes, I had more room, but still not enough for college work. I just felt like college needed to be totally separate. Something in me . . . DEAN: Not with six kids in the family. CRUMP: [052] Yes, yes, I wanted to be away from there. I had had it. I was done with that. My brothers were wild. The fact that I was going . . . When Dad took me to LSU and I saw the campus and I saw how pretty it was, I wanted to go there. I didn't think about how I was going to be treated because that . . . I saw what I was going to like about it. I saw a pretty campus with good buildings, and I saw the dormitories. They looked good and clean and solid, and I was going to have my own room. That's all I thought about. I was going to be going to a school that's credible and one that where my mother trusted me to live on campus without the restrictions. When I was in high school, I didn't get to hang out with my friends anyway, except at school. DEAN: Oh really? CRUMP: Yes, so I wasn't used to a big social life anyway. DEAN: [065] Had you done any dating in high school? CRUMP: Very little. Very, very little. There, I think boys had come to the house, and I had gone to the prom. I'd get to go to some games and see them there. No one had picked me up and took me to the game. Once, in [my] senior year, when our class gave a supper, 2  Crump, Maxine - Tape 203, Side A I got to go for a ride in the car with my boyfriend and my girlfriend's boyfriend, and we had more fun! Just fun, you know, it was like, "What does my mother think is so bad about this?" [Crump and Dean laugh] And I got to go on the senior class trip but not with my boyfriend, I had to ride the bus with the rest of the kids while he drove in the car because my mother didn't trust me to ride with him. DEAN: Where was the trip to? CRUMP: A beach not very far from us. It was a day's bus ride. It was nothing. It wasn't even a nice beach. It was a made up beach. DEAN: Well, there aren't any beaches in Louisiana. CRUMP: Yes. It was the only beach the blacks could go to. We couldn't go to the other beaches in Louisiana that were also made up. This one was even worse made up, but we enjoyed it. DEAN: Well, we've been going for quite a while now. Do you want to stop now and set up another time? CRUMP: Sure, we can do that. DEAN: To, to go on with the LSU, per se? CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: Is that all right with you? CRUMP: Yes, that would be fine. DEAN: [083] Good. Good. [End Tape 203, Side A. End Session I.]  3	Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections			Louisiana State University Libraries, Special Collections ( http://www.lib.lsu.edu/special )									Physical rights are retained by the LSU Libraries. Transcript Crump 204A-B										Narrator: Maxine Crump 4700.0117 Interviewer: Pamela Dean Session II Transcriber: Barrett Campbell 14 July 1992 [Begin Tape 204, Side A. Begin Session II.] DEAN: All right. We had gotten you to the point last week of your father had suggested to you you might want to go to LSU rather than Southern, and you thought it sounded like a good idea. Now I know that the university and the board and the state legislature had all been consistently resisting integration and only doing it under court order. This process had been going on over the last eight to ten years before you arrived there. And undergraduate enrollment had been opened up under court order again. You, as you told me, were the only woman who was not from Baton Rouge who came that year. Is that right? CRUMP: No, I was the only one who lived on campus. DEAN: The only one who lived on campus. Okay. I know when the first black student came to register that the president of the university and somebody from the state police, I think, accompanied him. CRUMP: He came in the summer. DEAN: In the summer and registered. How did you go about registering? CRUMP: By the fall, that had changed because I met some other black students when I came there, and they talked to me more about the university than about what it was like being black there. They just were asking questions about what classes to take and that sort of thing. I was almost oblivious, at that point, of what my presence there was meaning as far as the university. I had a hard time keeping focus on being black because having gone to an all black high school, you didn't have to think about being black all the time. Fortunately, I was still in that mode. I mean, I thought of myself as Maxine, a freshman going to college. I saw white people all around me, but since I didn't see a contrast -- I saw very few blacks--I didn't think of myself . . . it's not that I didn't know I was black, but it wasn't any more conscious of me that I was black than it was of the white students that they were white. I was just Maxine. So registration just was normal. I didn't notice anything. Noticed nothing. DEAN: No. You didn't hear any expression of resentment that you were there? No sense that anybody was trying to make this difficult? CRUMP: Nothing. DEAN: Suggesting you shouldn't be here?  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A CRUMP: [25] That's right, and my parents never spoke to me that I might encounter anything like that. I talked with J. K. Haynes who was instrumental in helping me get a small scholarship to go there, and I think he was also instrumental in helping the other students go there. DEAN: Now, who was he? CRUMP: J. K. Haynes, at the time, was head of Urban League [Louisiana Education Association], I believe . . . I think, I'm not sure. He was some civil rights activist at the very sophisticated level. He's a very classy, sophisticated man till the day. He's just one of these designers of the culture. Going to his office, he treated me with a lot of quality, and he treated me like I was special, and, and like I, I was . . . it was an honor for me to be able to go to LSU. I heard all of that and it felt nice, but I didn't feel any burden with it. DEAN: No sense that you were a pioneer, breaking new ground and had a mission? CRUMP: It was like I knew it. Right. It was like I knew it, but I had no perspective on what that really meant. I couldn't relate it to anything. I just heard it and took it as it was and just did what I did. Felt good with it, didn't quite know what it meant, but was willing to participate in it. Wasn't frightened, because I didn't know what to be frightened of. No one told me what to be frightened of. I think that was very fortunate that no one warned me about LSU -- not a single soul. If they did, I never heard them. I heard no warnings. I think, perhaps, my Dad protected me from it because he told me not to tell anyone until I actually got there, until we were actually going. I think that may have been his way of protecting me from hearing any negatives, so I had no negatives going in. Registration was just as tough as registration could be for me, coming from a small town. DEAN: Was it one of these, you know, you have to go to the gym and stand in long lines, and go through all of that sort of thing? CRUMP: Oh, yes, it was horrendous, yes. As far as it was for me, I seemed to be going to the wrong places, and oh I was just miserable. People would say, "Ha! I got registered in two hours." It was like, "How? It took me all day!" I was just in pain at the end of the day, but very willing, very willing. I had moved on campus, of course, I think a week ahead and had the orientation and all that. DEAN: Let's go back and talk about that . . . Let's talk about arriving on campus, and your impression of the place, and moving into the dorm and so forth. CRUMP: [51] Oh, I thought it was the most beautiful world I had stepped into. The main thing I noticed was the tower and the trees. I thought they were the most beautiful trees I had ever seen. I thought, "Surely there can't be a more pretty campus anywhere." When I got to the dorm, I had no idea that I was getting in this dorm because I was black, and it was a place to put me where I wouldn't be with the other students. I never was aware of that, and my parents said nothing about it. They knew, but they said nothing. It was a  2  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A little dorm called Louise Garig. If you ask most people on campus, they don't know where Louise Garig is. It's in the horseshoe area, and it was connected to a large dorm called Highland, which was connected to another large dorm called Evangeline. I forget what the other one was across the horseshoe. Instead of me going through the big door, Highland was the door that everyone went through, even if you lived in Louise Garig. But the day I moved in, the door to Louise Garig was open. The only day it had been open that I knew of was so that we could bring our things in. So I was just busy packing things in my room, and then said goodbye to my parents who looked very sad. I couldn't understand . . . why are they looking like that? I thought they'd be glad to be rid of me. Then I just went about packing my room and reading the material they had left in there for me, seeing what was the next scheduled thing that I had to do and just getting ready to be really excited about college life. DEAN: Where was your room in the dormitory? CRUMP: That's another thing. It was at a perfect spot. I didn't have to pass anybody's room to get to my room. It was at the very . . . soon as I climbed the stairs, I walked right into my door. So I didn't have to pass anyone else's door. They had to pass mine, but they didn't have to see me walking in the hall. DEAN: Was this a dorm room that had its own bathroom, or was there a shared bathroom for all the floor? How was that laid out? CRUMP: It had its own bathroom. It had a suite, but no one was in the suite. So I had my own bathroom. Oh, I was a special student. I was laid out. DEAN: Did you become aware that your housing situation was any different than anybody else's? CRUMP: Yes, yes, about halfway through the year. It took me until about halfway through when everybody had a roommate, and I kept thinking, "Surely I'm going to get one, too. She's just not here yet." Some of the girls commented, "You're lucky. I have to go down the hall to the bathroom." I'm thinking, "Yes, I am. I really like having this bathroom, you know." I met a Latin American student, and we became friends. She said that she liked talking to me because I understood her accent. I understood her English through her accent, and she said, "The other Americans say they can't understand me." She said, "And I can understand you because you speak slowly enough." I speak rather rapidly, but I think she understood me for a different reason. It couldn't have been because I spoke slowly, because normally I don't. We became friends, and she wanted to room with me. I didn't really want a roommate at that point, and I didn't tell her no. But she went over to the housing and asked. She came back and she told me, she said that they told her that it wouldn't be proper. They said, "Are you aware that she's black?" She said, "Of Course!" I felt hurt and shocked, and it was an awakening that I had. But then, it was like, well, I didn't, didn't really want a roommate, and she, and she was not really . . . well, who I  3  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A wanted as a friend. I felt like we were not well matched as friends. I liked her, but I didn't want to have her as a roommate. If I'd had her as a roommate and not a friend, it would work, but a friend and a roommate with us being unmatched as friends, I didn't think would work. I don't know if I thought it out quite that sophisticatedly then, but it soon passed because I enjoyed having a room to myself. My dad gave me a special treat and paid for linen service for me so I didn't have to worry with laundering sheets and towels and stuff. I had that every how ever often we got it, and that was fun. DEAN: Nice little luxury. CRUMP: It was. DEAN: Tell me about some of the other friends that you made. CRUMP: [105] Okay. On my floor was about two or three other rooms -- some of the neatest women probably ever at that university. I didn't know at the time, but they chose them based on their willingness to live in the dorm with a black student. I had no idea. One of them was from New York and an actress. One of them was from Panama. I don't remember the others, but they were very nice. I think the university set it up that way. I think they consciously did it. So they would come to my room and sit on the floor and talk, and I'd go to theirs. We'd answer each other's phones. Oh, there was another student on that floor who was an actress, too, and both of the actress women were the most interesting women I have ever met. I mean, they seemed older than me because they knew so much, and they were from northern cities. The North will accept her. The South won't. There were no Southerners on that floor. In fact, I'm almost just really realizing this now, but I've tried to remember that other woman's name. I used to go to both their plays because they were in plays immediately at LSU, and I used to go see them in plays. Which I loved plays, and they would get me tickets and get me in and stuff. Kay Hodges was more of the Janis Joplin type. She was always having some dramatic panic. The other one had the appearance of calm and control, but it was still in drama. They were just fascinating. I could tell that my race really was irrelevant to them, and I enjoyed them because they were . . . I was so fascinated by them than anything. I wanted to dress like the students on campus, not because they were white, but because I was on campus, and I wanted to look like everybody . . . I just wanted to blend in at that point. So I told my parents that everyone was wearing gored skirts, and penny loafers, and oxford shirts-long sleeved, and blush. They wore blush. So, my poor dad, he's so funny. He went out and bought me the best blush you can buy. I had better blush than any of them. I had Revlon. He had heard Revlon. That's it. I'll get her Revlon. So I got nice gored skirts and oxford shirts and penny loafers. I just felt like, "Oh, I know I look like an LSU student." That's all I could see is that I looked like an LSU student. So I walked with the same brisk pace that they all walked with. Because at our school everyone sort of, you sort of shuffled down the halls slightly, you know, but at LSU, everyone had a quick pace. So it was like, I can get this pace, too. I can do that.  4  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A As a result of going to the cafeteria every day, I got to . . . I didn't have private dining, however . . . I went to the communal cafeteria. DEAN: That's a very important question. CRUMP: [141] Yes, yes it was. All of the, listen to this, all of the cooks were black, of course, and I would say hello to all of them as I went through. They loved it. They loved it. At first, they were a little skeptical, come to think of it. One of them was friendly right off, and the head cook, and one down on the other end of the line was friendly, but the others were kind of standoffish waiting, you know. "If she's at LSU, she must think she's better than us." Something in there was there. It hadn't dawned on me to think of myself as better than anyone, and so I would say hello to them because they just . . . it was just, you just always said hello to other blacks, when you were . . . oppression breeds this, you know, especially when they're in a setting where there are few of you. You acknowledge that you know something here, and the hello is just part of that. Eventually, they all became friendly to me. Invariably, someone always sat with me. I was never left alone. DEAN: Really? CRUMP: They always joined me. Sure did. We had male students who worked there. The second semester, I applied for a job on campus, and that's where they put me. In that cafeteria, I loved it. What did I do over there? I think I used to check people in as they came through the line or something. The people who worked there would always sit with me. Some of the male students would sit with me. DEAN: White? CRUMP: Yes, they were all white. Everybody was white except the cooks. And so . . . DEAN: There were, that first year you were there, someplace around twenty-five black undergraduates that I'm aware of. Most of them freshmen. Most of them coming in as you were. CRUMP: [164] I saw them when I went to the Union only. None of them were in any of my classes. They were always studying higher careers than I was. I was studying office administration, studying to be a secretary. It was the only career I knew. I didn't know anything, and I had taken typing in high school. They finally introduced typing in my high school in my senior year, and I had done very well in it. So I thought, "Well this is what I'll take in college." I didn't know anything else. I mean, the other subjects didn't, I didn't know any careers linked to them, like history, English, math, science. I didn't know any careers linked to them. The only careers I knew were teaching, and secretary was kind of a new thought for black women. I didn't know any black secretaries then, but I knew that the field just seemed to be right, because I knew how to type. I went into that study. A very boring field, by the way. Some of the freshman courses were interesting, but when I actually got into the study of secretarial, it was so boring. But I didn't know, I  5  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A just thought I was lazy maybe. I did. How unfortunate. I didn't know how to think very well anyway. I didn't know that I did know how to think but just hadn't had it fostered. And so I didn't see any of the students. I remember meeting a student in the restroom one day, and she said that she heard that I was taking Spanish. I said "Yes, I am." She said, "Well, I'm having an awful time in it. Can you help me out?" And I'm thinking there's nothing you can do to help somebody in a foreign language, and there isn't. There's nothing you can help them with. They've got to do it. But anyway, we ended up talking and I invited her to my dorm room and everything, and we became friends. She told me, she said, "You know, the other students are saying you don't want to associate with them." I said, "Why do they say that? What are they talking about?" She says, "Because you never come to the Union." I said, "Well, I come back to my room between classes." She said, "Well, you know, you could at least drop by the Union." I said, "Why? Just to keep them from talking about me?" I said, "I won't do that." I said, "I'll go to the Union if I want to but not just because they're talking about me." So I went to the Union one day with her, and one of the students in there said, "Well, it's the first time I've seen you in the Union." I thought, "This is the first time I've seen you at all." You know, "What are you talking about? How do you talk to me this way?" So I didn't have much to say to her. I didn't like that, so she and I didn't become friends at all. So of the twenty-five students there, I had one friend. DEAN: Who was it? CRUMP: Her name was Yolanda Laws at the time, and she was from Baton Rouge. She drove to school everyday, and she worked part-time. DEAN: Most of these other students were from Baton Rouge, so they had probably gone to high school together they knew each other already. CRUMP: I think some of them had gone to high school together, yes. I didn't know where a lot of them were from. I really didn't get to know them, but many of them were from Baton Rouge. And I think several of them were from the same schools. Because they hung out in the Union between classes, because where else you going to go between classes. DEAN: Your coffee machine's talking back. CRUMP: [204] Yes, getting in on this. They got to know each other very well and sort of formed a camaraderie which I didn't want to do that. I didn't want to go and isolate myself into a little safe corner of let's be black together. I knew I didn't want to do that because I wanted the university experience. I came there to have the full college experience. I just felt like that's what I had dreamed about, really being fully a college student, and I didn't want to miss anything. I felt like I would have been missing something if I had hung out in safety numbers. That's another thing I learned from my dad, he never hung out in safety numbers. If he went to a large function, I remember he would come back home and say, and he'd been at some function, political function, he'd  6  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A say, "All of those fellows all sat together, but I went and sat over with the white guys." So I guess I learned not to do that thing, not to isolate myself. Even today, I just talked to my vet yesterday, and he was saying, "Well, you know, people always separated themselves in groups, you know, blacks did it." And I thought, blacks didn't do it for the same reason other groups. Other groups did it because they didn't want to associate with them. Blacks did it because no one wanted to associate with them, or they feared no one did. So that's totally different. I don't know what blacks would do if they really had been allowed to choose in this country, so you can't say. So, I knew I wanted to have a full university experience, and I did everything I could to have it. I was Catholic. So I joined the Newman Club right away, and that gave me lots of people to associate with, at least as Catholics. I had that in common with them. I was looking for what I had in common that could get me into the university experience. What I saw from the blacks, they offered me nothing to get me into the university experience because they hung out in the Union all conglomerated together and whatever they did. The girls in the dorm, I had that in common with them, I sat around and talked about the same things they did. I don't remember what it was, now, I guess everything. Mostly light conversation, mostly play and fun. And I listened to them talk about their boyfriends, I didn't have a boyfriend. I had had a boyfriend in high school, but we weren't in contact with each other. [229] He had moved to California. And I met one friend, I think I met her in the cafeteria. She came and sat with me one day. Her name was Donna Howell. I met her very early on. She was from New Mexico, and I really identified with her. We looked related, almost. She was very dark and had dark long hair. But she looked like my relatives, so that's why she looked related to me. We dressed very similar, except she had nicer clothes, but our taste was very similar. She had a low, modulated voice like mine, but she was a singer. She sang very, very well. She was in choirs. The university choir. She was Baptist, so she was in the Baptist student choir and just an incredibly pretty woman. They used to have what they used to call the Bengal Belle of the Week, and she was the first Bengal Belle of the week on campus the semester I came. DEAN: Was this something in the Reveille? CRUMP: The Reveille, yes. They would come out with the Bengal Belle of the Week. I guess we got rid of that in the sixties. You know in the late sixties. DEAN: Right. CRUMP: We're not going to be belles, southern belles, Bengal Belles, or any other kind of belles. Donna and I did everything together. I never remember her looking like someone was going to say she was with a black person. I started becoming aware of who was comfortable being where with me. There were some people who were very comfortable with me in the cafeteria, very comfortable with me in the dorm, but out on campus in the Union, they'd pass me like they'd never seen me before. So I started becoming aware that there was a difference that I had to select who were my friends. I started to learn very fast how to recognize who was my friends. Really my self-esteem  7  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A started to suffer some because some of the people that I really thought were my friends, I didn't want to think that they didn't fully embrace me as a friend. And that would hurt a lot. But it hurt in a way, unfortunately, that made me feel like there was something wrong with me, something wrong with being black, but it was never spoken. You know, people think, it's funny, whites often think that the worst thing that can happen to a black is to be yelled a racial slur at. PLEASE give me racial slurs. You know, just don't give me a friend who ignores me in public. You know, a friend who embraced me intimately and then get in public and she's never seen me before. Give me racial slurs over that. I think that that's probably true for most blacks. We don't care that Ross Perot said "You people" it doesn't mean anything, you know, that's nothing. So . . . DEAN: Was this the first time you had had to deal with this kind of racial prejudice? CRUMP: Yes, it was, it was, because when, yes, it was in that way . . . the only other racial prejudice I had dealt with was white people at home who just didn't speak to you at all, who kept their distance from you. That was it. Or you saw "White Only" signs, or that sort of thing, but directly where people just . . . I had never seen people who look at you like you didn't exist once they've known you. These people at home had done this across the board, but I had never known anyone who could sit and talk with you and the next time they see you, look right through you. Blacks don't do that. They may now, but from when I was coming up, there was no such thing. If blacks didn't like you, they wanted you to know they didn't like you, and they would give you a sign and turn their heads. You know, they would obviously let you know, "I'm angry with you. I don't like you." They made a statement out of it which is great because then you know where they're coming from. But I wasn't used to people who could look like they had not even registered your presence. I had never seen that before. That was the coldest, most painful thing that I had ever experienced. I knew that I could never learn it, and was sure that I didn't want to, but kind of wondered if I didn't need it. I finally met some friends from New Orleans, the Barberito sisters, Janel and Mary Barberito, and they were very, very nice to me and very helpful. Didn't treat me like a black friend, you know. We went everywhere together, mostly Janel and I, because Mary lived further away, I think. Mary came the second semester, I believe, but anyway. DEAN: Was Janel in your dormitory or one of your classes? How did you meet her? CRUMP: I don't remember how I met Janel, but I think she was in a nearby dorm in that horseshoe. Yes, she was. So I don't remember how I met her. DEAN: [299] Were they your first white, southern friends? CRUMP: Well, Donna Howell was. DEAN: No, she's New Mexico. She's not southern.  8  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A CRUMP: Oh, southern, yes. Yes, the Barberitos. That's right. They were from New Orleans. Then I met a friend from Mississippi named Becky, and I don't remember Becky's last name. But Becky was friends with Donna, and that's how I met Becky. When Donna told me she wanted me to meet Becky, and Becky was from Mississippi, I said, "I don't think I want to meet her, Donna." She says, "Oh, no, Becky's really nice." When I met Becky, Becky says, "Look." Becky was the first person who spoke directly to me about race. From Mississippi! She says, "I hate Mississippi and all that it stands for." I thought, "Really?" She was blonde! She wasn't very pretty, though. Becky wasn't very pretty. She was kind of gawky and tall. DEAN: Didn't qualify for southern belle? CRUMP: Right, right. I met no southern belles. I met no southern belles. They did not associate with me. They said, "Hi, how are you," type thing and kept going, or they would give me a quick smile. But there was no relationship with them, ever. They never connected with me. Can't think of one. I had Jewish friends, New Orleanians are a little different sometimes. By the way these two New Orleanians, the Barberitos, you can tell by their name, they were Italians. I didn't meet any WASPS. I didn't meet any WASPS. They definitely kept their distance, but they kept it in a way that I was almost unaware that they were keeping their distance because like I said, I wasn't used to really being around people who looked like they had never seen you. I thought if they looked like they hadn't seen me it was because they didn't for a long time, until I had people that I had been friendly with that when I saw them out changed on me. I didn't, still didn't, connect those people who were not looking at me as people who hadn't seen me. I thought they didn't see me. So I didn't even get the invisible syndrome -- which is what I think you get when people ignore you -- because I didn't know they were ignoring me. I thought they didn't see me. You know, well, there's lots of people around they just don't see me. DEAN: You really took it at face value. CRUMP: [322] Yes, I did. I didn't examine it. It was so unfamiliar to me. Then the Newman Club, everybody was nice. I don't remember knowing where everybody was from, but I remember being asked thousands of times a day, "Where are you from?" I heard that more times than I ever thought I could hear it in my life, "Where are you from?" "Oh, you're from here!" That was the reaction. "Oh, you're from Louisiana!" I finally realized it was because I lived on campus it was like, if you live on campus you must not live nearby where you can drive here, because otherwise you wouldn't live in this dorm. So they would ask me all the time, "Where are you from?" All the time. I felt like a lot of the treatment I got was because I was the only one. I felt like if there had been one other black student on campus, especially in my dorm area, I would have been forced with her, and then they would have shut us both out. Whereas when there's one, I just think it's easier. I think it's best to have a large enough group or one. Two or three is devastating to any type of mixing, so they almost, I guess, thought they were doing me a favor sometimes which is fine. Do me a favor. Be my friend. I need somebody to talk to. I used to go home about every two weeks. Some weekends I'd stay  9  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A on campus and go to the Newman Club. I used to have to work for friendship there. I had never worked for friendship before. It just sort of, you know, I had grown up with all these people. I actually had to go up and say hello to people and ask them questions. It took a little work. I didn't mind it. I saw that I had to work a little harder than other people to make friends. Some people who reached out to me in the Newman Club were doing the, "I'm going to reach out to you because you're black," and those friendships weren't friendships. I had nothing in common with these people. They said nothing I could relate to. They didn't try to get to know me. They just had me as "black." And so I didn't have much in common with them, so that didn't go very far. Finally, I would meet some people in the Newman Club that I could be friends with, that would talk to Maxine. One of them was in the choir, and I said, "Well, I used to be in the church choir at home." They said, "Well, come join us." So I joined the student center choir, and I enjoyed that because anyway I had another group to be in. I went to the LSU games, even by myself. DEAN: You did go by yourself? CRUMP: [371] Yes, sure did. I went by myself up until I think, the second semester. I had met Pat Kennison, who was from Gonzales. Was not a southern belle, by far, although her family is more the French-Cajun type. She was very different. She was boyish and laughed aloud and clowned and cut up. Then I met Marie Biendeau and her sister, I've forgotten her sister's name right now. Of course, they're Italians, too, and they were from Baton Rouge. These were my friends. The Biendeaus had a Jeep that we would go riding in, a convertible Jeep, which was fun. So I got to do those kind of wild college things. It was like, "Ooh, this is being in college!" Riding in a Jeep with the top off of it. Going to get malts, I had never gone to go get malts. My mother was always, "I don't want you hanging around the malt shop. People are going to say I'm just letting you run wild." It was like, "I'm running wild. I'm having a malt!" DEAN: Where did you go for malts? CRUMP: At the pharmacist right off of campus. It was a Rex- All drug store, it was called Tigertown Pharmacy. And I remember there seemed to have been a time where there was a question about whether I could sit at the counter, but I did. But they didn't know I was black because the Italian girls were dark, too. So they didn't know. I did a lot of things that way with my dark white friends because they were almost my color. We would go in places where they didn't know I was black, and they would say, "They don't know you're black!" So a lot of the places I went were like that. A lot. DEAN: How did you feel about that? CRUMP: I felt that if I had been discovered, I would have been embarrassed. If they had said, you know, "You can't because you're black." I just felt like I would have been embarrassed to be thrown out. DEAN: Did you think about it at the time?  10  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A CRUMP: Yes. Yes I did. Yes, but it didn't stop me from having a good time. I thought about it, and I went on and had a good time. But I was aware of it. DEAN: [407] Did you have any sense that passing that way was in any sense a betrayal of your race or that you should have been confronting this rather than just getting away with it, so to speak? Did you feel that way at all? Did that ever occur to you? CRUMP: I knew that I didn't have what it takes to confront it then. I knew that in a way I was passing, but I knew that I wasn't choosing to pass. I was letting it pass. I wasn't saying, "I'm going to go in here and pretend to be white." If anyone had asked me, I would have acknowledged being black, absolutely, I knew that. I knew I'd be honest about it, and I knew that the other blacks on campus would have scoffed at it, totally. I knew that from my friend Yolanda. That they would have said that I wanted this. But what I wanted was the full college experience, and if it meant going in those places they went, then I wanted to go in those places because they were college students, not because they were white. About two years after being there at the university, after they had a large onslaught of blacks come in, and blacks couldn't go in all those places right off campus, then the university issued a statement that said: "You will serve all LSU students, or you will move your business." Then we could go in everywhere, but I was thrown out of one place when I went with some other blacks to a place I had gone to when they didn't know. I went in that same place, and it was the Bengal, no, the Pastime. They came over immediately and said, "We don't serve coloreds here." DEAN: Was this part of a deliberate attempt to desegregate these places? CRUMP: It was. I knew what I was doing. I knew that they didn't accept blacks. DEAN: Was there a larger movement among the students to do sit-ins, to picket, to . . . CRUMP: Yes, I think there was . . . DEAN: . . . force desegregation . . . CRUMP: Yes, there was at that time. DEAN: So you were an active part of that. CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: And this would have been, what? Sixty-six, sixty-seven, someplace around there. CRUMP: [447] More like sixty-seven, yes. Yes, because it was really two years later when a lot of blacks came from Shreveport and Bogalusa where the blacks were more active in their own hometowns. Those from Baton Rouge and Plaquemine and where I 11  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A came from, we didn't come from strongly activist communities. But when they started coming in from Bogalusa and Shreveport and New Orleans, then we had activism among the blacks on campus, and we started forming organizations and making a difference. The university started listening to us a little more. DEAN: Yes, it was sixty-seven, sixty-eight that we start getting Black Power and then of course, with Martin Luther King's assassination in the spring of sixty-eight, I think it was. DEAN: Yes, and so things started to change on campus, and then I had some black friends, finally. It was difficult because I had black friends, and I still had white friends. I almost had to sort of associate with them alternately, separately. DEAN: Was there pressure from your black friends, from the black student community as a whole to be militant and not associate with . . . CRUMP: That's right. There was, but I had found that I was learning a lot from whites about how life works in this society. I knew how life worked in the black community, but I did not know how life worked in the society. I found out that there were things I had to know, things I had to be connected to if I ever wanted to go anywhere in this society. I couldn't just be black, I knew that, and just do what the black culture did because I wanted the opportunities that everybody else had. I always felt I was entitled to them because my parents said I was. They didn't say it with any if and's about it. I believed that, and it just made sense to me that I had rights to all of this, and I didn't feel like a militant for feeling that way. It just felt normal to me that I should be able to get any opportunity that anybody else could get. I didn't see enough difference. I didn't see where because I was black I shouldn't be able to do it. I just didn't get that. I didn't get it. I saw that they had the edge. They were on the inside, and I wanted to be on the inside and learn from them. I used to go to the coffee houses with them, so I was a part of both movements. I was a part of the Black Power movement, the black civil rights movement, and I was also a part of the peace movement. I had no interest in getting to know the southern belles in the sororities. That seemed to be going nowhere to me. That was another way of belonging in the society that was very big and very important, but it didn't fit me. I knew that if I had to do that to fit in, forget it. I'll dig holes and live underground first. I was totally turned off by the ribbons, and the matching socks and the plaid shorts. Well, they weren't shorts then. They were skirts. We didn't get to the pants until sixty-seven. DEAN: Women were still not allowed to wear slacks on campus until then. CRUMP: No, that's right, sixty-seven, yes. DEAN: Dress codes. CRUMP: Yes. Dress codes were a big deal.  12  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side A DEAN: Let me turn this tape over.  [End Tape 204 Side A. Begin Tape 204, Side B.] DEAN: [20] I'd like to go back later on and talk a little bit more about some of those rules for women and so forth that we all lived under in that period, but I want to pick up on something you were saying about being part of the peace movement as well as the Black Power movement. The Catholic Student Center was, and, and the priests at the, at the Catholic church or chapel, whatever it was on campus, were very active in both civil rights and the peace movements. CRUMP: Yes, there were some terrific priests there. DEAN: Can you, can you tell me a little bit about that aspect of the, of the Catholic Student Center and those priests. CRUMP: The priest that was there when I came there was your traditional Catholic priest, reminded me very much of our hometown priest. But within a year . . . DEAN: Now your hometown priest, you said, had been black, is that right? CRUMP: Yes, one of them. DEAN: Ok. CRUMP: But traditional as a priest. DEAN: Do, do you remember his name? CRUMP: The black priest? DEAN: No, the, the one on campus. CRUMP: Father Brew(?) DEAN: Father . . . CRUMP: Brew(?) DEAN: Brew(?) CRUMP: [30] I don't remember any others until Michael Cody came, and he was the most phenomenal priest I had ever met because he talked about real life practical things. 13  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B He wore cool robes. I mean, they had a flair to them. They looked like they were designed for his personality. He didn't wear the traditional thing. His was an all-in-one robe. They used to have the layers. They'd have the white thing under with a belt tied on and then the other thing over it. Well he just wore this one robe with a hood on it and a belt. It looked good on him. He was a good looking man. Young, strong neck, looked like he worked out, and you could see his neck because it wasn't all bailed up. I thought, "This is neat. This is so down." In the sixties, we were really getting into letting go of the establishment tightness. DEAN: Right. CRUMP: And so here was a priest who, who walked down off the altar and sort of swayed around when he talked and wore this real cool robe and was good-looking and young, and who said these things that with, with no fear that, that really connected with us. We got rid of the choir loft, and we started the guitar singing. We brought it down into the chapel and started singing cool songs in church, and girls became ushers and altar servers. It was wonderful. I thought, "This is the way Catholics should be." I thought, "Gee, my parents would love this." Well they . . . it was like, "Not really. Okay fine, you do your thing, this is what I like." I was having a lot of fun with it, and it seemed like I knew that anytime I went in that church that anyone I sat next to welcomed me. I knew that there was no such thing as, "I better check and see if I can sit next to this person," because it seemed like only cool people came there. They were involved in the peace movement. The Newman Center invited students from Southern to do programs and social things with them and that sort of thing. DEAN: Were there any of the faculty that were involved in, who came to the church, were involved in any of the peace activities? CRUMP: Not that I was aware of. DEAN: So it was the priest, Father Cody, and the students. CRUMP: [57] And the students. We started a coffee house. We put parachutes in the top of the hall, and we would just come over there and drink coffee and talk about life, you know, and situations and issues and the world. I would listen to all these people with these thoughts and these opinions and plans, and it was like I was in the middle of life happening. I was no longer on the outside looking in, hoping to be in. I had found a way in. Part of the was that I found to be in was to listen, to be friendly, and pick my battles. There were things I stood on. There were lots of things I let pass. It was like, "I don't know why they're saying this," because then I started to find that even the people in the peace movement had little idea of what black was like. You don't know unless you're black, and it still remains. They meant well, but they had little idea. I was very surprised at that. Then there were those that I would actually talk to who would tell me I was wrong. Whites would tell me that I was wrong about what blackness is. And I'm thinking . . . "Could I be?" Really! I would think, "Could I be?" because they seemed so smart and so knowledgeable, and they could put it in such logical terms to show me where it really  14  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B didn't make any difference, you know. People would talk about disliking a person because of the color of their skin, and I used to think, "It's more than skin color that they don't like." That may be what is the most obvious distinction, but the dislike list is a lot longer than color. But no one ever spoke it, and I used to think, "Well, maybe it's just you, Maxine, maybe you just think this." It's like, no, no, which one of them would choose my hair. None. It wasn't until years later that I asked someone that, you know. You say you'd love to be my color because I always get people who say, "Ooh, I'd love to be your color. I'd love to get a tan." I'd say, "Yes, but you wouldn't like to be black. You'd just like your white skin to be my color because you wouldn't choose my hair. Maybe some other things about me that looks black you wouldn't want it. You don't want to look black because you don't want the problem." And I understand that. So as a result, they would think that I didn't want the problem, but they were wrong. I didn't want to be white. I just wanted to have everything whites had. I was walking with a friend one day, and she told me that she would (Years later, she told me that she told this to every class she taught. She was a teacher, Miss Pat Kennison from Gonzales.) She was short, shorter than me, and had short legs. I'm not very tall, but my legs are long. We were walking. She had on heels, and I didn't. So you can imagine I was out- striding her. And she would say, "Slow down, Max." I'd slow down, and after a while, I had left her again. And she'd say, "Slow down!" After a while it happened again, and she stopped, and she says, "Max, if you don't . . .," because we were walking on Highland, where cars were passing and things, she said, "If you don't slow down, people are going to think I don't want to walk with you." She tells this, I had totally forgotten it, she said, "And you stopped and turned around and put one hand on your hip and you said, 'They might think I don't want to walk with you.'" She said, "Boy, was I humbled." So I really did see myself, I was perfectly fine with my color. [94] There were times when I had a little difficulty explaining my hair because at the time I didn't have a permanent relaxer in it. I used to get my hair hot combed, and in swimming I wore a cap. Well, all of us had to wear caps in swimming, but we still got our hair wet anyway, and afterward they had blowers in the, in the gym. The girls would all go and blow their hair under these dryers, and I would think, "This doesn't quite serve me, because when I blow mine it's going to just be wild." I would dry it some, and I found that I could get it to work some because unless I really washed it with soap it didn't lose all of its straightening. But it didn't look like theirs. I was a little conscious that they may have been criticizing my hair. No one ever seemed to, but that they may have been thinking it was odd. I remember one day I said to Pat that I needed to get my hair done, and she said . . . no, she said . . . something, it was something came up about the hair . . . and I said . . . needing to get it done or something, and she said, "Well, I've got conditioner. Doesn't it just need conditioning?" I said, "No!" I said, "I straighten my hair!" She says, "Why would you straighten your hair? It doesn't look curly to me." I said, "It's not curly. It's kinky." "You don't have kinky hair." I said, "That's because it's straightened now." They just couldn't understand that, and that was uncomfortable for me to have to explain. I didn't want to have to explain my hair. I wasn't comfortable with that. And, that sort of thing. Then I remember thinking, "Well, gee, if I was dark, I would have to explain why my makeup color was different than theirs." Because really they  15  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B could borrow my makeup, some of them, you know, and that sort of thing, and they did. I could use theirs sometimes if they weren't really fair, and so that made it easy for me, in a way. They just couldn't understand my hair. DEAN: We were all spending so much time getting ours curled. CRUMP: Yes, and I used to . . . DEAN: Or by then, I guess, maybe we were in to ironing it CRUMP: Yes, you were in to ironing it. That's right. I remember asking one white friend, "Why is it I know so much about you, and you know so little about me?" I didn't know at the time that it is the nature of oppression, you know. All blacks knew whites better that whites knew blacks. Whites had no reason to get to know blacks other than the way they needed to know. But as friends, they had never had a need to really relate oneon- one. I met a friend named Ciel the next semester, Ceil Brown, and we became good friends. Ceil asked me to spend the night at her house. It was between semesters, and my dad brought me to campus because Dad didn't know where they lived, and my dad brought me to campus and her dad picked me up. We went out to the house, and they were so impressed to show their friends that their daughter had a black friend visiting. I didn't know that was such a big deal because I had lived with Ceil on campus, but in the home it was different. To have a black friend sleeping overnight in your home was a big deal. I didn't know that. I found out then, and I kind of enjoyed that part of the specialness. In my family's home, we didn't think of it as a big deal, you know. Ceil had asked me to come stay at her house, and my parents thought, "Well, isn't that nice?" but that was about it. But her family brought in friends to introduce and they were just, "Well, hi!" you know, it's like, "Hi." Amazing to me. But I learned so much, there [at LSU], things I never, never knew that I came there to learn . . . never knew. Our dorm mother was named Mrs. MacDougal. [137] She was an old southern white lady, surely and old southern belle, but she treated me so well. As I look back, I think part of it was Miss Mac was old enough not to care about all of that. She didn't have to worry about it. She treated me just fine. And I really liked her, and all the girls liked Miss Mac. I just remember my parents liked her too because she always spoke so well of me when they came. Oh, that's what they wanted to hear. "Maxine never gives me any trouble." They felt safe having me there. And I was also so very impressed with how well LSU had prepared for their students to live compared to Southern. I had been in the dorms at Southern because I had been there for an overnight program of some type when I was in high school. The dorms, I thought, were horrendous. Just horrendous! The rooms were just so ill-prepared and ill-equipped and poorly laid out, just such ugly dorms. I didn't really want to live there. And a phone in the hall. Things like that. We had this solidly built dorm, built out of the best of things in this beautiful setting among trees and magnolias and azaleas and camellias, crepe myrtles, all of that in this one area. Wellheated, well-lighted. We didn't have air conditioning then, but it was designed for great  16  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B air flow. So I was basically very comfortable there even in the summer because of all the trees and the big windows they had. And a phone in my room. My own phone. "How can they afford this?" I wondered. So I was very, very pleased with how well- equipped we were, how fine the cafeterias were, how the meals were always exactly on time. We had everything. They served us everything. I thought the food was delicious. The other girls used to say it was horrible food. It took me about two semesters to decide cafeteria food's at least tiring, but at first it was exciting to get up in the morning and have breakfast done. It's not that I didn't have that at home, because my mother did but certainly not that kind of layout. I gained seventeen pounds my first year, eating three full meals a day with all . . . with desserts every meal or something with sugar in it every meal. But then the next year, I got it off real fast because after I started taking swimming . . . two semesters of swimming. And I got back down, I lost . . . well, no, I lost only seven of those pounds because I came in very underweight. I needed to gain about ten pounds. Then I lost seven, and I stayed at that weight. At that time, it was a beautiful weight for me, and it looked, it looked really good. Because I was working out, because I remember some of the white students used to say, "Your legs are so firm!" You know, "How do you do that?" And I said, "Well, I take swimming" and everything, but it wasn't just that . . . it was kind of genetic, you know, the firmness was. But I had never noticed that, I thought, "Geez, you all have everything, what could I have that you don't have?" Then they started noticing things that I had that they liked, like eyebrows. At that time, that needed no pencil, eyelashes that needed no mascara, things that they didn't have they started to admire on me. I thought, "This is exciting! I've got something white people want!" And I had a behind . . . DEAN: Including a perfect tan. CRUMP: Yes, they liked my perfect tan. They liked the fact that I had a behind, and they liked my eyelashes and my eyebrows, that sort of thing. Things I hadn't thought much about, they liked. Yes, it felt pretty good. DEAN: [187] Let's talk for a minute about all those - -since we're on sort of feminine topics right at the moment -- all of those rules, checking in and out and, and so forth that you had to live with. CRUMP: It's the reason that I was able to live on campus because my parents liked them. So at first, they were a blessing to me because they gave me the opportunity to live on campus. CRUMP: The house mother enforced them, but they were set by the university housing. DEAN: This was an administrative thing. CRUMP: Yes, and they got to be . . . if you signed out, if you were going to go out of town, you had to get permission from your parents, so I knew that was out. I never even asked for permission from my parents. But when my sister came two years later, she would sign out and just go. They didn't know where she was. She'd go to New Orleans or whatever. She wasn't about to ask parents, and I never even thought of that. Never even thought of breaking that rule. I thought my parents might look for me or ask . . . I never would have been comfortable being in New Orleans having just signed out regular when I didn't have . . . because I was given limited permission. There were those who had what they called general permission, and they could go out of town on the weekend, but I couldn't unless my parents signed. I think I kept limited permission that whole year. I don't know if I had it the next year when they moved me to the French House, which is another exclusive dorm. And I was given another private room. DEAN: [216] Tell me about the French House. CRUMP: It was a lovely place, just lovely, small amount of students so I got to know them all . . . that's where I met Ceil Brown. DEAN: Now, where these local students or foreign students, mostly in the French House? CRUMP: Local. I think at one time it used to be for foreign students, but it wasn't. DEAN: Was it a co-ed dorm at this point? CRUMP: No, no. There were no co-ed dorms when I was there. DEAN: I know at one point French House was co-educational and primarily international students. CRUMP: Oh, I see. But I think because this was baby boom, they had to just use every available space they could, yet I had a private room. A little cubby hole at the top of the stairs, once again. I walked those stairs and walked into my room, and it was like a little loft. It had a little portal window. The only room like it in the whole dorm because all the rooms were different in the French House. I was given this little loft, and the bathroom  18  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B was directly around the corner. I mean I'd just walk out of the room, and it was right there. I think I must have shared it. I'm not sure. DEAN: Do you remember if there were other rooms on that floor? CRUMP: Yes, there was one in the corner, but I don't remember those people. There was one other room up there, and I don't remember who stayed there. They must not have made an impression on me. DEAN: Was there still dining in the French House, or did you still go . . . ? CRUMP: No, I had to go to the cafeteria. We went to another cafeteria, then. The upperclass cafeteria. DEAN: So this was your sophomore or junior year? CRUMP: Sophomore. DEAN: Sophomore year. Did you live on campus the whole four years? CRUMP: No, I lived on campus until my sister and I moved into an apartment. DEAN: I have to take a break. CRUMP: Oh, indeed. DEAN: It's, it's the coffee . . . again. CRUMP: [241] Well, you were asking me about the strict codes, and one of the strict codes were that you had to wear dresses everywhere . . . to class. I mean, you could wear slacks in the game room. DEAN: This is in the dormitory, is it, or? CRUMP: No, at the Union. DEAN: At the Union. CRUMP: And that was, I believe, on weekends. I don't remember how we got to the game room in these slacks. I guess we could walk across campus in them, but we could not be seen out of the game room. It was like you wore pants, you went to the game room, you came home, you changed. I believe that's the way it was. But you could not wear them to class. Just out in the Union and everything. I guess I just kind of accepted it as a university rule until the students started to complain about it and how inconvenient it was for us. Then it started to make sense to me that this rule doesn't make any sense. When they decided, they had dorm meetings, which was another exciting thing about 19  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B being in the dorm because the dorm girls would get together and do things like rally around candidates at election. They would decorate at homecoming, and they would just get together and talk about things on campus and the way the university worked, and there was really not much of a forum for that if you lived off campus. DEAN: Was there a house committee or something? Did that body make any kind of rules for the dormitory? Was there any scope for that sort of thing in that system? CRUMP: I don't know that it made rules for the dormitory, but it planned things to happen at the dormitory. We agreed to participate in certain things and support certain things, but for the actual inside of the dorm, we had enough rules already -- didn't need to make any new ones. We used to have room check after closing hours. DEAN: Who did that? CRUMP: One of the students. She would be like a hall monitor or something and she would just come in and say, "Room check." She may open your door, or she just may yell in. They got to where if they knew you well enough they just yelled in and you answered back. DEAN: Making sure everybody was in. CRUMP: Yes. DEAN: Was there somebody checking you in when you came in in the evening? CRUMP: No, you just signed in, but then they did room check afterwards. But anyway, when they decided the deal about wearing the pants on campus, we all met in different dorms, and they came around and made speeches to us. I don't remember where house mothers were. I don't remember whether they agreed to let us gather in the lobby to talk about this or whether we just did it because it was students talking to us. DEAN: Were they official representatives of student government or anything like that? CRUMP: No, no. Just concerned women students. DEAN: That started raising this issue. CRUMP: That's right, just rebel-rousers, I suppose. They said, "What will the university do if we all just show up in pants at the Union tomorrow?" Oh, that sounded exciting, and so I was going to be with that group! So we did, and of course then the rule was changed instantly. We just converged on them. It was one of the best movements that happened on campus while I was there. Because many times there were movements attempted on campus that the university just put down, and I saw the power of the university then. I thought, "How can these wonderful ideas that I see these students have ever become a  20  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B part of society or ever make a rule change at this university because then the semester ends and any movement is killed." The administration knew how to wait us out. DEAN: Is there an example you can think of of one of these cases where you tried to do something and you couldn't? CRUMP: Let's see, there was something we wanted on campus, what was it that we fought for? We held a vigil. I wasn't actually in that, but there was something that students actually went and sat outside the administrative offices and things like that. The university just didn't budge and didn't have to, and at the end of the semester, it was over. It was something, some sort of activism that we wanted. It was during the sixties movement, type thing, and I'm sorry I can't remember what it was. But I know that whatever it was, the university just waited us out. Thanks to David Duke, who used to stand on the steps of the Union and hold arguments with people until crowds would gather around him, espousing his beliefs on white supremacy. It drew a lot of attention from black students arguing with him, white liberals arguing with him. The university was very embarrassed by this, and they couldn't stop him, though. David Duke knew they could do nothing about this. The Union was public, and the first amendment was on his side. So the university answered that by designating Free Speech Alley. When I told them that at Channel Nine, when I said when David Duke ran, I said, "Well, David Duke started with the reason Free Speech Alley was started." They said, "No one ever said this. I didn't know this." I said, "They weren't at the university, maybe. You're talking to someone who was there. I know he did." They checked it out and found out it was true and used that in their news story on him. The only station that did because they were the only ones who had Maxine Crump. But anyway, yes, that's how that started. I started to really like the activism, and by then I was starting to understand what activism was about. I was starting to understand what civil rights were about. I was starting to understand some women's rights, in a little way, not as strongly as I was civil rights. It seemed to be the bigger issue at the time. Then when the women's movement started, some of the men said, black men in particular, said that the women's movement was started to dismantle the civil rights movement. I don't think it's true. I think they went hand-in-hand, and they needed to stay hand-in-hand because they couldn't stay hand-inhand, both were disbanded. Even though activism made sense to me and I was a part of activism, I ran into trouble with blacks because I didn't wear dashiki. I didn't like the way they looked, so I didn't wear them. I didn't like the way they looked on me. I liked seeing other people wear them, and I liked the thought of them. But on me, I just didn't like them. DEAN: How about an Afro? CRUMP: I didn't wear an Afro because it looked like a boring haircut to me. I couldn't style it. It's just one shape, and so that's why I didn't wear that. To be honest, I also knew that I would lose some of my white friends if I wore an Afro because they would have a  21  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B hard time with it. They would have taken it as a radical statement against them. However, if I had been stronger, I would have worn it and just let them go if that was what the friendship meant. But it was like I thought I needed that connection. Then basically, though, I still was aware that I didn't like the Afro because even later, when I wasn't friends with as many whites, wasn't as in contact with them, I still didn't want to wear one. I saw it was damaging to the hair, and I already had very fine hair, easily damaged. So I didn't want to wear it for that reason, too. I didn't feel that that was how I needed to express my activism, necessarily. I thought it was fine for those who did. I was glad people felt free to wear their hair in a natural because they didn't call them Afros at first, they called them naturals. Afro was a name given to them by whites. So I never wore an Afro, but I was involved in activism. But I wanted to be in real activism. I didn't just want to be radical or militant. DEAN: Was there any particular activities that you were involved in? Incidents that you think of being involved in? CRUMP: [355] I was in Harambe, which was a black student group on campus. DEAN: How did that come about? CRUMP: It was a group of male students, and I don't remember quite how they formed. But I remember being contacted immediately. One of the students that was there was Rap Brown's brother, Lance Brown, and he was one of the originators of Harambe. DEAN: Was that a group unique to LSU or was that something . . . CRUMP: Yes, it wasn't nationwide. They formed it there, and we met in the Union in one of the, I guess, community meeting rooms. We talked about issues. I don't really remember exactly what issues we talked about. There was so much going on then. There were so many issues everywhere . . . I just don't remember. DEAN: Well, some of the things I know that came up at various times in sixty-seven, sixty-eight, was the issue of recruiting black athletes, recruiting black faculty, do you remember any specific efforts in that direction? CRUMP: They were talking about how to get the black students participating in more of the functions on campus. That was one of the discussions. It wasn't so much recruiting black students for things and recruiting black faculty, but how could we be involved in more campus functions, and that sort of thing. Because actually, we weren't involved. I had been involved in more than any of the students had been because I had been in the dorm and in the Newman Center and there early on campus and I had infiltrated, so to speak, a lot of things that they wouldn't have allowed me if I had been more obviously black and if it had been more blacks. Then I started to really realize how the other black students were being shut out and how I had now truly experienced how black students were being treated on campus fully. Then I started to become aware that I wanted to make a difference in that area. I didn't want to just enjoy the privileges and be able to get  22  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B away with the lighter color. That I wanted the university to really change, to really take responsibility for its students, all of its students and not have this separating idea. Also, the jobs on campus, certain jobs we didn't get on campus. If you were a black student, you got relegated to filing books in the back room. You never got any out-front jobs. I was in the cafeteria, and I was in the speech department, but I was always in a closed-away office. DEAN: Interesting. CRUMP: Yes, and they had some nice jobs on campus for students. There was no chance of me getting it, but I didn't know that at the time. Yes, I didn't know why I wasn't getting the good jobs on campus. I never gave it a thought until we started having Harambe and discussing those type things. DEAN: So your consciousness was being raised. CRUMP: [407] Very definitely. Finally, we elected the first black student government president, Kerry Pourciau. You'll probably interview him. As I look back, I can say he was a safe bet. He was selected by the whites to be an okay black. DEAN: In what sense was . . . CRUMP: He didn't cause any trouble. He proved that there were some better black people that were deserving of being with white people . . . barf. DEAN: What had he done to demonstrate that he was a safe . . . CRUMP: He made good grades, and he got along well with whites. He didn't say anything antagonistic. DEAN: Was he a member of Harambe or anything like that? CRUMP: Not that I recall. I don't think he was, but he was a very bright, optimistic student. He had a very positive attitude. But I think he had been programmed, like a lot of us were when we first came there, to show whites that all blacks are not bad. That's shifted for me. I recognize that I didn't have to prove anything. They needed to prove to me that all whites weren't bad, you know, if we are going to do any proving here. Who are they that I should prove anything to them, and so I felt that he had not unlearned that. He still did that, and he did it very well, to the point of acceptance, and I found out that there are several ways of getting into the system. In a way, I had participated like he did by being the right kind of black, but I wasn't aware of being it because I needed white endorsement. I was aware of it from him. I saw myself as needing to be on the inside to get the full university experience. Maybe he was doing the same thing I was doing, but it looked to me that he was trying to impress white people that this is how some blacks are, and I'm not like the others. So they said, "Great! We'll, we'll reward you. We need, we  23  Crump, Maxine - Tape 204, Side B need to run you for SGA president because we need to make a statement to the university." There were some liberal students who needed a black student to make that statement with, and they did have to choose one that whites would vote for. They were able to make it Kerry, and it was very popular at the time for the university to show itself in that way. They did, and they voted for him. I mean, they wouldn't have voted for Lance Brown. [Laughs] DEAN: [458] You were at LSU when Martin Luther King was assassinated. Tell me, do you remember about that? Hearing about it, and, and what the student response and administration response to that was? CRUMP: I think the actual date that he was killed, I wasn't on campus because I had flunked out. I think I wasn't there because I know I would remember more of what actually happened on campus. I remember sitting in the dorm, in my junior year or earlier, watching a program on tv with Martin Luther King, and I was the only one watching it. I don't remember what that occasion was, but I remember feeling a little uncomfortable, at first, and then discomfort doesn't matter here, this is important to me. People passing didn't even say hello. It was like it wasn't, it wasn't even okay to associate with me if I was going to watch that. I was just aware of almost being in a time warp with that. That stands out more to me than actually what was going on in the program, is how I was being treated for sitting in a lobby actually watching it. So in order to ignore the program there, they ignored me, too. No one even said hello. DEAN: That was too militant? CRUMP: I think it was. DEAN: Well, let's see . . . what else . . . CRUMP: Well, the class work. The class work was unlike high school in every way, and I don't know if it was that unlike it for other students. I think partly because I came from a small town, partly because I was from an all-black high school, and partly because just college is different. I didn't know which was which, but I found that the professors extremely knowledgeable and interesting, but I didn't quite understand them. DEAN: Let me just . . .  24	Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections			Louisiana State University Libraries, Special Collections ( http://www.lib.lsu.edu/special )									Physical rights are retained by the LSU Libraries.
