Transcript Hamilton 453A										Narrator: Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Interviewer: Mary Hebert Session I Transcriber: Robin Cate 21 August 1993 [Begin Tape 453, Side A. Begin Session I.] MARY HEBERT: [001] This is Mary Hebert. Today is August twenty-first, 1993, and I am going to be talking to Leo Hamilton. And we're at Hill Memorial Library on LSU's campus. Okay, first we're going to start out just me asking you just a few questions about your background, and then we'll stop the machine and make sure that the sound quality is okay. LEO HAMILTON: Okay. HEBERT: Okay, first, I'd like for you to just tell me your first name. HAMILTON: Leo. HEBERT: Your full name. Your full name. HAMILTON: My full name, Leo Charles Hamilton. HEBERT: And when and where were you born? HAMILTON: June twenty-third, 1951, in Baton Rouge. HEBERT: And what was your father's name? HAMILTON: Leo Hamilton. HEBERT: So you're Leo Hamilton, Junior? HAMILTON: No. I'm Leo Charles. My, I'm the second of six kids, and my mother thought if there was to be a junior it should be the first. And she has eight sisters and one brother, and her brother's name is Charles. So, I'm Leo Charles. HEBERT: So you're Leo Charles. HAMILTON: Right. HEBERT: And what about, what was your mother's first and maiden name? HAMILTON: Myrtle Franklin. [break in tape]  1  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HEBERT: What did your father do for a living? HAMILTON: He was a machinist. Well, he, yes, he's retired now. He was a machinist with Holsum Bakery here in Baton Rouge. HEBERT: Did your mother work? HAMILTON: Off and on. She worked as a domestic, really, for most of my life, and then she did . . . Well, all her life she wanted to be a nurse and did not get the opportunity to go to school to do so. And actually, I guess it must have been in about 1971 she decided to go to one of these schools, small business colleges around town, and now, she's a nurses' aide. She just decided it was time to do something about it, and so she's a nurses' aide. She does home health care. HEBERT: Did she not have the opportunity to go to college or to go to a nursing school? Was that . . . HAMILTON: Well, no. What happened was she got married, I guess, gosh, right after my dad got out of the service and had children and at the time could not afford it. There really weren't a whole bunch of opportunities available, and, as I mentioned earlier, there are six of us. So, with six kids, she just didn't have the time. HEBERT: That's true. HAMILTON: And at about '71, and I say '71 or '72, I can't remember exactly, but the three boys are a year apart. All of us attended LSU and my brother Gregory was in his second year here in '71 and that was the time at which she decided, my sisters are younger than us, and she just felt she had the time so she went to school part time, got her . . . I remember helping her, that's how I can remember we were in school, and I remember helping her study. So at that time, she got her degree, and now, she's a nurses' aide. HEBERT: What was their level of education, your parents? HAMILTON: High school. HEBERT: High school. HAMILTON: They both finished high school. HEBERT: Here in Baton Rouge. They're both from Baton Rouge? HAMILTON: Actually, they're from Pointe Coupee Parish. My dad's from Morganza and my mom's from Bachelor. And they did finish school here in Baton Rouge at McKinley High School. But you have to remember when they were in school there,  2  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A McKinley High School was the only black high school for the three or four-parish area. So they rode a bus from Bachelor and Morganza to school at McKinley. HEBERT: So they met at McKinley High? HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: You said you had six brothers and sisters. Well, there are six of you. HAMILTON: Right. HEBERT: Could you give me your brother and sister's names and the order of their birth? HAMILTON: Larry is the oldest. Gregory is the . . . well, I'm the second. Gregory is third and he's a year younger. Oh gosh, Glenda. Glenda is the oldest girl. And, I guess Glenda is three years younger than Gregory. And Sheila, Sheila Gayle, is two years younger than Glenda. And Sandra is three years younger than Sheila. I have to put it that way. HEBERT: And what was their level of education? You said your brothers all came to LSU, did your sisters? HAMILTON: Yes, my brother Larry started LSU, quit, after his freshman year, I guess really after the first semester of his sophomore year. Went to the Marine Corps, was in the Marine Corps for nine and a half years. Came back from the Marine Corps and graduated from Southern. My brother Gregory graduated from LSU, as I did. Glenda came to LSU, left, I guess Glenda left here after her . . . I think Glenda left after her first semester of her sophomore year and went to work, got married, and completed school, eventually, in Atlanta at Clark, a small college, in Atlanta. Sheila left LSU after her junior year and she eventually completed school in Washington. I've forgotten the name of the school, small college in Washington, D.C. And my sister Sandra left after her sophomore year, mid-semester second semester, got married and just left, and she's working now, here in Baton Rouge. HEBERT: Did your parents encourage you to come to college? Was . . . HAMILTON: Oh, yes. HEBERT: Was that expected? HAMILTON: Well, it was desired. My parents, my mother, I guess more than anyone else, always wanted us to go to college and wanted us to go to LSU. I mean you have to remember, I grew up in south Baton Rouge. I was born in south Baton Rouge about six blocks from the campus. So we grew up listening to the LSU football games on my front door step and coming to and from the campus. In between, I guess, my mom's different 3  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A jobs, one of the jobs she had was working here on the campus for a while. And, because, I said, she was a domestic, but she did some other things as well. But, she worked on the campus, and I guess there was always this thing that that school's right there. She always wanted us to go to college, and it was right there. It was always like "If you can go to LSU, why don't you go to LSU?" So we always had this thing about LSU, it was kind of like it was right there, it was a neighborhood school. So it was, and, and my dad was a big civil rights person, I mean really into this integration thing, really into getting his kids involved in it, and it was, it was a, it was a goal that his kids were not going have to go across town . . . HEBERT: To Southern? HAMILTON: Not that we have anything against Southern, but it's the idea that you had to go. And that that school is right there and they're going to go to school right there. And we were fortunate enough to be able to do that. HEBERT: Did your father take part in any of the civil rights activities in Baton Rouge, such as the bus boycott? HAMILTON: Oh yes. Dad did all, he did all that stuff. My dad did all that kind of things. He was a, a, he's kind of hot- headed and he got involved in a lot of that. Not as a leader as much as one of these people who did a lot of leg work. He marched and did that kind of stuff, but, as much as he could and keep jobs. You know, you hack people off you lose your job. So as much as he could and keep his job. And he was more involved in voting rights than anything else. HEBERT: Registering people to vote. That kind of thing. HAMILTON: Right. He really was much more concerned about that. That was a big thing with him and my mom. HEBERT: Was he, were they involved in any of the national organizations such as . . . HAMILTON: NAACP. But that's, you know, I mean, I don't think anybody of their age group who did anything wasn't involved in the NAACP. So, in that sense, yes. HEBERT: So you grew up in a home that it was expected . . . that civil rights was a part of it. HAMILTON: Well, that you get an education, that you understand what the civil rights movement was about, and that you did better than the people who came before you and that you moved outside of a segregated environment. HEBERT: Did your parents ever lose jobs because of their involvement in the movement? 4  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HAMILTON: My dad lost two. I mean, I know he lost a job at Sunbeam Bakery and one other. But like I said, my dad's got kind of a hot temper and he has a habit of stating his opinion about a lot of things. Not that I always agree with my dad, but I mean he . . . and we have arguments about a lot, so I know he lost some jobs on that but that's part of what you're doing. So they had, they were involved in it and had some struggles but they did okay. HEBERT: [115] Did you-all ever have any contact with people such as Reverend Jemison or Johnnie Jones? HAMILTON: Oh Johnnie Jones and Joe Dubb [?] and all those people because we're all in the neighborhood here in Baton Rouge and Rev. Jemison, too. But my parents, my dad probably, my dad's Baptist, and my mom's Methodist, so that has something to do with whether or not they would move in those circles with Rev. Jemison. My mom would tell you more about Reverend Haynes, who was a Methodist minister. HEBERT: Is that J. K. Haynes? No. HAMILTON: L. L. Haynes . . . HEBERT: L. L. Haynes. Okay. HAMILTON: . . . was the, was involved in those days. J. K. Haynes is education at Southern and L.E.A. and all that sort of thing. But they were religious, much more so than, and much more involved in that than any other groups. Because those groups, because those groups are more geared towards teachers and, and academicians or people who were more involved in the educational system. HEBERT: So your parents were more involved in, in, well, the religious . . . well, the not, not too much education. HAMILTON: Yes, through the church. Yes, through the church because grass roots were through the church, and they were really more grass roots than the leadership level. HEBERT: [130] Let's get back to talking about your childhood. What kind of chores were you expected to do as a child around the house? HAMILTON: Oh wow! Gee, around the house, I mean you did all the chores everybody else does around the house. You did the housework. Because our parents were working all the time. HEBERT: So the children were responsible for keeping up the house and the yard. HAMILTON: Oh, and the older kids kept the younger kids, and you kept the house up. During summers when we weren't in school and weren't able to or weren't required to do 5  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A those sort of things, my dad did other jobs, odd job type things, because he's kind of . . . My parents were from the farm, so they had sort of like other, I mentioned they do one thing but they did other things as part-time work, too, because you got six kids, you got to do a bunch of things. So, my dad used to work with construction jobs, too, like on the weekends, and I've done everything from cleaning bricks to mixing cement, carpenter's helper, you know, all kinds of manual type stuff, labor like that. My brothers and I, we, if Daddy was working on a on a project with some folks and they needed some helpers, we went out and did what was required. We carried wheelbarrows and helped people with bricks and wood and whatever the devil else they need you to do. HEBERT: Did you ever have a job on your own, like not through your father? HAMILTON: Oh yes. I guess my first job was when I was in high school, a sophomore in high school. And my brother, Larry, had the job first, then he got me the job, then Gregory got the job. But we were delivering groceries on a bike. City Grocery, on the corner of North Boulevard and 12th Street here in Baton Rouge, was one. And, oh God, let's see, I've done hamburger work, you know working . . . not, but it's not here anymore, Burger Cream Town, used to be a hamburger place in South Baton Rouge. It's been closed for years. I worked over there . . . Everything else, I would guess, would be after college. Because after I got into college, worked on the campus through student aid, and I worked in, in the library over there, in Middleton Library. Actually, my first job as a student was with the Louisiana Higher Education Facilities Commission. The commission was doing a facilities study, campus facilities studies, throughout the state of all the campuses. And what we did was measure space in all the buildings on the campus for utilization, buildings and structures, because, I mean, we even did the darn farms. But, we were doing a spatialization study. Then after that, I worked at the library, and then I had some off-campus jobs. Around-the-Corner restaurant was one. HEBERT: That just closed recently didn't it? HAMILTON: Yes, it did, just closed . . . It's the pizza place now, Pizza Hut. And I think it was something else even before that. Then, I mean, I was a bartender at the Catholic Life Center, at, you know, parties and things like that. All kinds of work things. HEBERT: So you had to work to put yourself through, to pay your way through college? HAMILTON: Oh yes! Yes. I mean I had, I did get loans but mostly it was work study, work study and loans. And, I mean, that gets you your books and it gets your tuition but . .. HEBERT: It doesn't give you any living expenses. HAMILTON: No. And I also worked at, the best job I had, I guess, when I was in college was with the Baton Rouge Consumer Protection Center. I worked at Consumer  6  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A Protection Center, I guess, from 1972 through about 1975, I think, I think it was about '75. But that was a good job, an interesting job. I liked it. And it was geared . . . At the time, I knew I was going to be going to law school. I'd already made up my mind to do so, and it made that job better because I was working in, as an investigator, resolving consumer complaints, doing studies. So it was, it was helpful, and it was more hands-on, practical-look- at problems and problem solving. So I, it was a good job, I liked that job. HEBERT: Let's go back a little and go back to your early education. Where'd you go to elementary school? HAMILTON: Buchanan Elementary. HEBERT: Buchanan? HAMILTON: Here in Baton Rouge. HEBERT: And it was segregated at the time. HAMILTON: Oh yes. HEBERT: You did, you did go to integrated high schools didn't you? HAMILTON: Yes. But I went to segregated elementary and junior high school. McKinley Junior High School, which is McKinley Middle now. And there's a middle magnet. My daughters, both, my older, my two older daughters both went to McKinley Middle. So it's interesting to me now to see that that school is a middle magnet school and is, I mean, one of the most, one the best schools in town, and one of the most desirous. I mean, it's interesting to even talk with people that I work around now who try to get their kids into McKinley Middle. When I was at McKinley Middle, I don't think anybody was fighting to get their kids in McKinley Middle. But . . . I mean, I enjoyed it. It was a good school. and then after that I went to Robert E. Lee High School. HEBERT: How large was Buchanan Elementary? HAMILTON: Oh God. HEBERT: Did it serve just South Baton Rouge? What I'm asking is . . . HAMILTON: Well, when I started Buchanan, it was the only elementary school in South Baton Rouge. By, I would say, my, oh gosh, I guess it must have been about my fourth grade year, Polk Elementary was built so there were two. But before that it was the only one. For this . . . lower part of South Baton Rouge. There's Park Elementary for the upper part of Baton Rouge, and then there was South Boulevard. But there weren't a great many, so it was a pretty big school. 7  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HEBERT: Let's talk about Lee High a little bit. Did you have to apply to go to Lee High, or had they desegregated to the point where you could just enter it? HAMILTON: [222] No. When I started Lee High School it was, what, let's see, I guess that was in '65, '66, oh gosh I've got to think now. I guess it must've been '65, '66 . . . '66'67, '67-'68, '68-'69, so it was '66-'67 school year. And actually, they had gone through their second integration plan. My brother, Larry, was at Baton Rouge High School which was in the first integration plan, and the zones changed in each plan. He was at Baton Rouge High, and graduated from Baton Rouge High. I was at Lee High because the zone changed. And it was a choice plan. You had the option of Baton Rouge High or McKinley when he was eligible to go to high school. And it was Lee High or McKinley when I was eligible to go. You had these two schools within a zone, you had a choice. And my parents opted to send us to the integrated school. HEBERT: Did you have any problem getting in? I talked to someone who applied to Baton Rouge High and they, he was, his application was turned down. HAMILTON: No, I didn't have any problems at all. And neither did Larry getting into Baton Rouge High. HEBERT: How were . . . HAMILTON: We had good grades, I don't know whether there was any problem or, we got in. It wasn't any problem in getting in. No. HEBERT: How were you treated once you got there? Was there a large group of black students? HAMILTON: Like shit. HEBERT: You were treated very badly. HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: How did that affect you? I mean, did it hurt your grades? HAMILTON: Well, it was really interesting because . . . Well, first of all, we're from an area in South Baton Rouge that it is today, much worse than it was then, but then people kind of thought it was a bad part of town. But it wasn't as really bad as it appeared. But, I, obviously there were people who were going to go the other way. Our parents were more concerned with keeping us away from an element. Given the option, "No, you're not going to go to school and hang around those people and keep them as your friends. You're going to go here." So, there was this big effort to do that. Now that created a problem, not just at the school, because, I mean, obviously, we were at a school where people didn't want us there. But, you had a problem in the 8  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A neighborhood because you didn't go to school with these people anymore, and there was a group of people, a small group of people, who resented the fact that you didn't go to school, not just that you didn't go to school with them, but you were at school with those white folk. You know. "What, you want to be white? You can't come to school with us?" So, you had to deal with these, you know, idiots at school. Then, you have to come home to deal with all these other idiots who were against you because you weren't in school with them, and you were all of a sudden trying to be white. So, it was really kind of an unusual paradox. It was an unusual situation. And I guess, oh, it was maybe my senior year in high school before I felt comfortable with that, I mean, to where it wasn't a real problem because it got to where you weren't able to relax, you couldn't go home and relax. I mean you developed three or four friends who would still be your friends, but mostly you'd have to deal with people you went to school with, who may or may not have lived in your neighborhood, immediate neighborhood. I went to school with black kids who didn't live in my immediate neighborhood. We were friends because, and associated because we all rode the bus together. But, I lived on West Johnson. They may have lived on Louise Street, which is eight or nine blocks from me, which means, you know, they're not the neighborhood. There was no one in my neighborhood who went to Lee High School, in my immediate neighborhood, the people I grew up with, played ball with, that sort of thing. So, I'd have to go outside of that immediate neighborhood to deal with friends that I could deal with everyday, or even talk with, or go, you know, that sort of thing. I mean, there were two or three guys who eventually come back and say, "Hey man, you know, we're sorry these people are doing this to you." But as a general rule most of the people in the neighborhood decided you were like, isolated, you just, because you didn't go to school with them anymore. So you couldn't hang out. So it was kind of weird. And you couldn't hang out with the white kids! HEBERT: No. HAMILTON: No. So it was kind of weird. HEBERT: Did you start, in the second integration plan, was it all through high school? Did you start out as a freshman at Lee High? HAMILTON: Actually as a sophomore. McKinley went to ninth grade, so actually I started in tenth grade and stayed at Lee High throughout. HEBERT: Now, the first integration plan was started with seniors, did it not? HAMILTON: [224] Well, it started with high school. Well, the first one, yes, started with the seniors, but as that plan developed it moved from seniors to freshmen, you know, seniors, juniors, sophomores, freshmen, but the zones changed, was the key thing. I mean, your home may have been in, like I mentioned earlier in Baton Rouge High School's, attendance zone in the first plan, when the change, the plans changed, you weren't. And so, that was a bigger problem for us because actually our zones changed  9  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A three times. I mean, once you were in you were there. They didn't say, "Oh, my goodness, you got to move now. Go to your respective school." But my brother, Gregory, would have been, I'm trying to remember which school, but he wouldn't be in Lee High zone again. And he was only a year behind me. But his attendance zone was McKinley and, God, I forget, I can't remember what it was, but it was weird, because he opted to go to McKinley. I mean all three of us went to different high schools, and one of the reasons he opted to go because it was like, "Hey, why should we all be at three different schools? No. And just stop." Then, plus, he didn't like the experience that we were going through, and he wasn't going to go through it. He just told my parents, "No." He wasn't going to do that. HEBERT: And your parents allowed him to choose McKinley? HAMILTON: Right. By that time they were at least sympathetic to the fact that it wasn't pleasant. You had to go to school everyday. So it was like hey, you know, it was like, "Okay, okay, they're not having a good time with it but they're going to stay. You can go to McKinley." HEBERT: You describe the bussing. It was segregated bussing. HAMILTON: Oh yes. HEBERT: How were you treated at school? You say you were treated very badly. What were some, can you give me some examples of things people did? HAMILTON: Oh God. Outside from being isolated, I mean, you were taunted all the time. I'll give you an example. There was a joke that white kids used to play, it used to piss me off, I mean the absolute worst one I ever . . . I'll always remember this thing because it was like the first winter we were there. And you know how cold, phlegm, they'd spit in the ceiling, and then call you over to talk so it'd drop on your head. Okay? Now I saw it done to, it never happened to me, but I saw it done to two people. It really pissed me off, okay. And I remember the first rainy day in P.E. and there was this game, battle ball, because when it was raining you're indoors and you had to be in the gym. Coach Fairchild had this game, battle ball, which apparently they'd played before. I'd never seen battle ball, but it's like dodge ball, except the idea is you get the juniors and the seniors on one team, and it's the guys, and the freshmen and the sophomores on one team. The girls are all sitting, and you push the bleachers in, the girls sit around the top of the bleachers or on the side lines. And there are nine volleyballs placed in the middle of the floor and the bleachers on one side, the freshmen and the sophomores, the bleachers on the other side, juniors and seniors. The object is to hit each other with the balls, it's like dodge ball. And obviously, you know, you scramble for the balls and you, and then, there's like a barrier, a line at which you're not supposed to cross, like that chair, between that chair and the bleachers. At some point everyone all had, one team has all the balls, and you're backed  10  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A against the bleachers, and the other guys are taking shots at you. And the deal was to get all, everybody out and save the black guys for last, you know. First of all, it's embarrassing because all these girls are up there, you know, looking at you. You couldn't, you couldn't fake getting hit or try to get hit, you know. The coach would make you go back in. "No no no, you have to go back in." So it would always be the situation where they save the black guys for last. And so there are like six or seven of us and all these guys with these balls staring down at you. You know with these volleyballs, and, you know, you're raring back to take somebody's head off. It was one the most degrading, and embarrassing and, I mean, I was the most hacked off person. I hated that game, okay. Absolutely. Never liked it, never, can't believe that they allow people to do that. I just thought that was asinine, I mean, it really pissed me off. But, I mean, just, that's nothing compared to some of the real stuff, I mean these people were assholes. Okay? I mean I'm talking, every damn minute of the day you had to deal with some idiot, you know, not just calling you "nigger" or, or, or, tripping you or pushing you or doing some crap to you, you know, and some teacher saying, "Well, well you're just going to have to take that." And you can't argue, or you can't do . . . You know, that is what pissed me off about the first year. The second year it got a little better because you'd been around them a lot, and some people felt like, "Well gee, that's wrong." You know, some, maybe five per cent. Most people just didn't care or they were against you. It's interesting to me that my ten-year class reunion, high school class reunion, I didn't want to go. My wife made me go, "Leo, come on, go." I guess in my class let's see, in my graduating class, there were, gee, I can't remember how many of us there were. But it was less than thirty, it was less than thirty, it was . . . only five of us went. I was not willing to see any of those damn people anymore. I was like, "Good, gone, screw them." It was interesting to me when I did go how little they remembered, how little they remembered how mean they were. I was really . . . It was obvious the ones who did, because of how reluctant they were to deal with, because they didn't know how you felt or did you remember, but it was an eerie uncomfortable feeling because so many people didn't remember. I mean, what, would walk up to you as if you'd been old buddies, you know. Somebody you want to punch out. HEBERT: They blocked out what they had done. HAMILTON: [304] You know, "I remember you." You know, "What are you talking to me for now? You'd better get away. Or I might just punch your ass out." I was really that upset about it. I didn't have a good ten-year one. Twenty-year class reunion was better. Primarily because, I think, everyone was more satisfied with themselves. People did remember and, I think, were more resigned to who they were and were ready to deal with who they had been. Actually, I enjoyed my twenty-year class reunion. Actually, the same five of us went too, but it was better. It was much more relaxed, and . . . Of course, over the course of the twenty years, I'd seen some of these people in and out of careers, lives, businesses, the whole thing. So, I mean, we were more at ease with each other and the ones who had been my friends, if you can say friends, because I guess I did have some friends, five or six, who I consider friends, and the ones who I think were amiable were,  11  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A were closer because they were amiable in high school, some of them I even went to college with, so we we're friends now. HEBERT: Now, these are white students? HAMILTON: Oh yes. Oh yes! HEBERT: I was confused for a second. HAMILTON: Yes. No, no, no, I mean, black students, there's a camaraderie there. I mean, even though there were some of us who had never been friends, before or after, but there's a camaraderie. You didn't have this . . . If, if, if one thing that experience did, it was make a camaraderie with people you might not otherwise have been comrades or friends, or you might not even want to be around. Because, I mean, some of those people I probably wouldn't have liked. Period. We just weren't compatible, but, you know. You're sort of forced into a position where you're all facing the same thing, and you understand each other. So, you have to develop a camaraderie, so, there was a camaraderie involved there. And . . . but I'm talking white kids when I say all these things, I mean they were they were really mean. Okay? HEBERT: Right. Well, I understood that. But I wasn't clear on the friends. Your white friends. How were . . . did you-all hang out together at school? Did you ever hang out with a few white friends? You said you had a few of them that you're still friends with now. HAMILTON: Hang out, no. But, I mean, when I say friends, there were . . . I can name them. I can name Ogden. I can name Needa . . . I was in the choir. I was in the a capella choir which develops a camaraderie. I mean, there were people that when I say friends, I mean, as much as you can be friends in high school. People that would talk with you because you were in the choir together or P.E. together that you could talk. Otherwise, they didn't talk. So, there were people who were amiable with you. I consider them at least, I mean, I'm talking about white kids here, as close as you're going to get as a friend in high school. Other people just wouldn't talk to you at all. So no, it was not a question of "hanging out," it was a question of whether they talked. HEBERT: Okay. HAMILTON: [349] Okay, it's like, you're not there, and "If I am going to talk at you, it's going to be telling you that you ought not be here and you ought to get your, get the hell on out of here." You know, so no, there wasn't any that "hang out" type thing. Oh no. Even though, I will say that as I became a senior, I did notice that say, in the freshmen class there were people who began to hang out, so you could see a change. HEBERT: So you were a senior right around like 1968? HAMILTON: Sixty-nine. HEBERT: Sixty-nine.  12  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HAMILTON: So, I could see it changing. But not my class. I mean, by the time I was a senior, I guess there were a few people who would hang out, in a sense. They'd talk to you, but that's not hanging out. HEBERT: No, that's . . . HAMILTON: They'd talk to you. They'd carry on a conversation. I mean, I'm talking about, you sit in a class and nobody talks to you, you know, or you walk the halls and nobody talks to you, or, you know, you're in P.E. . . . HEBERT: And no one talks to you. HAMILTON: They talk at you but they're not talking to you. No. HEBERT: What about the teachers? How did the teachers treat you? HAMILTON: A couple of them were horrible and obviously racist and obviously unfair. HEBERT: In giving out grades and . . . HAMILTON: Oh yes, because . . . I had a teacher give me an "F" once on a paper because she said, "You can't write that good, and I know you didn't do this. And you're going to have to do this again." And I did it again and I got a "D." Okay? So, I mean, I had teachers who did that to me, and I had teachers who would side with the students, who would almost egg on the students to do things to you. Then, you had teachers who just ignored things. Maybe one or two teachers who were, I thought, good teachers. I mean, I did have two or three good teachers who decided their job was to teach and they weren't going to care who you were. But there were others, those other kind of people involved in there. P.E. teachers, I had a couple of asinine P.E. teachers. I mean like the battle ball stuff. But I mean I'm talking teachers who would see kids do things to you and just walk on by . . . HEBERT: Ignore it. HAMILTON: And if you looked like you were getting ready to retaliate would come over and slap you across your head and say, "I'll put you out of school." That sort of stuff. I mean, so yes. You, you've got a mixture because, I mean, you got a mixture of people. But clearly, there were some people who were unfair, who were clearly unfair. But I can't say there weren't any good teachers who did their job. There were some. HEBERT: How were your grades throughout school? Were they . . . HAMILTON: My grades were okay, my grades were fine. I was an honor student.  13  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HEBERT: So, it didn't really deter from your grade point average or anything or . . . HAMILTON: No. I mean I had a, what, 3.4, 3.3 average, you know. HEBERT: That's great. HAMILTON: [390] So, I mean, I was a pretty good student. But I was always a pretty good student. I liked school. You know, I wasn't . . . and it didn't, it didn't hurt that I, I had to go home and study. What was I going to do? HEBERT: Right. Right. HAMILTON: You know, I couldn't go out and hang out. So it was easy. But my parents made us study. So, I had to study. I wasn't allowed to do anything until I studied. I'd come home, study, and then, I'd worry about going out and play ball or whatever I was going to do. Generally, if I was going to do that it meant usually I had to worry about calling Greg or Byron or somebody who was from another part of town and say, "Hey man", you know, "can you, can you, can someone come pick me up or can your dad come get me?" so I can go play ball over there with those guys. Because it was maybe my sophomore, summer between my sophomore and junior year, before I really felt comfortable playing ball in my neighborhood again. I mean, people would get rough on you. I mean they . . . I had fights in my neighborhood because I wasn't . . . HEBERT: You weren't going to the same school. HAMILTON: . . . going to school with them anymore. And all of a sudden you go to that white school, that kind of stuff. But it was really summer before I could go out there and play ball with them. Then you kind of go out and they, "Well, don't pick him. Pick me." Then you have to worry about waiting for your up because somebody's got to pick you to go up with them, you know, it's like, "Oh, shit". But, you know . . . it's part of growing up. HEBERT: Right. So what were some of your favorite subjects in school? Did you have any . . . HAMILTON: Early in school, I liked science subjects, but then, I liked history and English subjects . . . HEBERT: Did you . . . HAMILTON: . . . and social sciences. HEBERT: Did you know that you were going to go to law school when you were in high school? Had you decided that yet?  14  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A Or . . . HAMILTON: No, not really. I guess . . . I honestly didn't know what I was going to do, but my dad, when he was in the military had gotten all these books, I guess when he was in the Second World War, and he had a whole bunch of books that were in our attic, I remember. One of them was a law book, some kind of book, business law, or something. And . . . I just remember reading through it. I thought it was interesting, and we talked about it. And he really sort of brushed it aside. But . . . so it was in the back of my mind, I guess. But I really liked the sciences more then. Then, once I got to college and more involved in social consciousness, law school became more real to me than anything else. Because once I got to college, I got involved in campus activities, campus life, that kind of stuff, to the degree that I was concerned that we weren't involved in it. And . . . politics, I got more involved in that sort of thing. So, political science was my area and once in political science, law school became more of an option than anything else. By, by my sophomore year, I knew I was going to be in political science, and I knew that I wanted to do something in the social science areas. HEBERT: Let's talk about LSU now. We, we talked a little bit about why you came, and let's talk about financing. You said it was a financial hardship for you to come to LSU. Was it, did it hurt your parents, was it a financial hardship on your parents? HAMILTON: [440] Well, they couldn't afford it. HEBERT: So . . . HAMILTON: That's the first thing. I mean, obviously they couldn't afford to send us to college although they wanted us to go. My first semester here I was eligible for work study my first year. So, I did qualify to get work study and a loan. And really went through college on that. HEBERT: Was there any scholarship options? HAMILTON: No. HEBERT: No. Harambe, later on, tried to change that, didn't it? Didn't it protest against the lack of scholarships granted to minority students? HAMILTON: Yes, we did do that. It was a concern that the financial aid options were so limited and that there was really not an effort to make the financial aid options available and known. You know, it's kind of like recruitment, I mean, it is recruitment. You have to . . . at the high school level, inform kids of these things. We didn't have good guidance counselors. Well, I shouldn't say we didn't have good ones. There wasn't the effort at Lee High School to make all options known to black kids. My guidance counselor didn't make a whole bunch available to me. I went in there and I asked for things, and I got what I asked for . . .  15  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A HEBERT: Did they try . . . HAMILTON: . . . but if I didn't ask for the right thing I wasn't going to get it! HEBERT: Did they push for you to go to Southern, your guidance counselors, or did they try to discourage you from coming to LSU? HAMILTON: They didn't care one way or the other. If I didn't ask for anything, I didn't get anything. Period. And no one said . . . I mean, when I went . . . My mom and dad said, "Find out what your college options are. See what you can do in terms of getting some money to go to college because we can't afford it." They gave . . . I went in, asked my guidance counselor for information about schools, yes, Southern, and initially, she gave me Southern. I said, "Well, I'd like to see some other schools." That's when I got all the other stuff. Okay? So, I mean, I got stuff that I asked for. I can't say I didn't get stuff. HEBERT: But she automatically handed you the stuff for Southern. HAMILTON: [480] Oh, well, yes. And, I mean, I assumed that's what they all thought we were going to do, or I was going to go. But that has never really been an option for me. I honestly did not grow up with an affinity for it or a kinship to it. You have to realize, Southern is in North Baton Rouge. Within the black community there is a sort of, or was, clearly then, I don't about as much now, but there was clearly sort of a rivalry or competitiveness between North and South Baton Rouge, as is in the white community. Southern's reach was more so in the black schools, first, and I was not in a black school, and beyond the black schools, North Baton Rouge, Southern Heights, the more affluent areas of the black community and in the black middle class. Actually, Southern in the upper black middle class was not an option. Howard was the option. Howard or Morehouse. Southern really was kind of more middle, but like teachers, not doctors, not as much, lawyers, yes, but not doctors. Doctors, Meharry, Howard, that thing. But law school, Southern. And if you had any sort of options it was more one of the SWAC schools, South West Athletic Conference schools. Alcorn . . . HEBERT: Grambling. HAMILTON: Grambling, but Southern, Grambling principally. Once I wasn't in that environment anymore, you don't get the pull. You know, once I moved outside of the segregated school system, you're outside of the loop. So, I didn't have that Southern thing, or the . . . just the feel of it, because you don't get that really until high school. So I wasn't around that, you know. I didn't go to the football games, and . . . I went to the track meets because I like track, but I, it just wasn't anything that was really big for me. I had friends there, and I had relatives there. I mean, I had relatives that played on the football team and stuff like that. But it wasn't something that I went . . . My dad was not a big football person. My dad's a baseball guy, okay? He likes baseball; he didn't like football. So, I didn't go to Southern football games every weekend like lots of other kids do. I didn't develop that camaraderie or feel for the school. Like I said, in all honesty, I sat on my front steps and listened to the LSU football, I could hear the game  16  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A from my . . . HEBERT: From your house. HAMILTON: Oh, I could hear Sid Crocker from my front steps. So, I listened to games at home on Saturday nights. I'd be listening to the LSU game, they're all listening to or watching the Southern game. So, you know, I really had a closer kinship to this school. HEBERT: To LSU. HAMILTON: As my brothers and I, we all did. HEBERT: It's time for me to flip . . .  17														Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections, LOUISiana Digital Library, Baton Rouge, La. Transcript Hamilton 453B										Narrator: Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Interviewer: Mary Hebert Session I Transcriber: Robin Cate 21 August 1993 [End Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B.] HEBERT: [005] Let's . . . Can you describe your first day on campus to me, as a student? HAMILTON: Oh gosh. One thing I remember about my first day on campus was there was only one class where I wasn't the only black student. The classes were big, real big, and it was kind of cold. But it wasn't the same sort of feeling at, like in, like Lee High, at Lee High School, or in high school. Because it was so big, there wasn't the immediate feeling of this group of people on your back. I mean, it was, people were indifferent, more or less. There wasn't this feeling that every white kid walking around you was either going to beat up on you or was so overly resentful about you. I mean, it just wasn't there, it was too big. You know, high school there's this closeness, and it's really immediate. You didn't have, I mean, it didn't feel bad, I mean, I liked it, I really did. Since I had worked that summer, remember, with the Higher Education Facilities Commission, I was familiar with the campus. I mean, hell, I knew every building on the campus. So it, I didn't have any bad feelings about it at all. I felt great. I don't know if every other minority or black student had that feeling because they were coming from smaller towns, from segregated schools, you know. I had come from an integrated school environment which I thought helped, really helped. I thought it made it easier an adjustment, and I'm sure now that I recall the interactions with other students who didn't have the integrated environment or hadn't come from an integrated school environment. I wasn't intimidated by the teachers or the methodology of doing things, it just was school. I mean, I kind of liked it. HEBERT: How many black students were on campus at that time? Just a general idea. HAMILTON: Oh Jesus! HEBERT: A hundred? Two hundred? HAMILTON: Gosh, let me think by classes. Maybe two hundred. Maybe two hundred. Large freshman class. Not as large sophomore. And there were some graduate students that I, you didn't get to see. So, I'd say maybe two hundred. HEBERT: Was there a camaraderie among black students here at LSU, the same kind that had existed at Lee High?  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: Oh yes, more so. Actually, Harambe had gotten started, I think the year before, in '68, '67, and there was an effort to, to identify black students and get you aware that there was, that there were other black students, that there was a home-type thing where you could at least talk to people. I mean, obviously, the same inability to talk, to relate to people was a problem, and you wanted to be . . . School's like anything else, you want to interact with people, you want to, there's a social part of it. They were concerned that everybody understood that there was an avenue for some social interaction, and so there was an effort to identify who we were. There was even a convocation. HEBERT: Oh really? I don't remember reading about that. HAMILTON: I can't remember. I shouldn't say a convocation because convocation gives the idea that it was sponsored by the university, but it wasn't. It was sponsored by the students, by Harambe, and this group. I mean, there was a meeting, I should say, I guess is the word, because there were leaflets and "Hey, come to the meeting." That kind of thing. HEBERT: Did you . . . HAMILTON: And we all went to the meetings. HEBERT: Did you ever belong to the Martin Luther King Action Movement, or did that die out . . . HAMILTON: That was not . . . Well, let's see . . . HEBERT: It died out right around that the time you got here. HAMILTON: [042] Yes, because, in '69 Harambe was, like, taking over that. The Martin Luther King Action Movement was considered at the time a little too . . . meek. There was an effort to get more aggressive. The Martin Luther King Action . . . There were some people, because I remember the conflict. The people who were in the Martin Luther King Action Movement geared at . . . which, which, most of the times they used to meet over at the Episcopal Center and at the Catholic Center. They were not aggressive enough for the newer group of people. The thing about the Harambe group was it was a more aggressive, activist approach, looking more toward identifying with the more really radical groups in the civil rights movement at that time, identifying more with SNCC than with the NAACP. That type thing. And . . . or identifying more with the Panthers, or stuff like that, you know. It was . . . the idea was "Hey, the movement is moving this way not that way. That's pass. No, let's go this way." So Harambe was the thing. HEBERT: And Harambe was an all black group? HAMILTON: Oh yes.  2  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HEBERT: And Martin Luther King Action Movement was an integrated group, wasn't it? HAMILTON: Yes, right. HEBERT: Was there some feeling in the Martin Luther King Action Movement that the white students were taking over and, and directing it . . . HAMILTON: Well . . . I don't know if it was that they thought they were taking over. I think, and I'm thinking about people like Charles Cochraham and Johnny Gill when I think about that. I think, and Ron Johnson, those guys were of the opinion that the movement for black people has no place for white people. You can not identify this struggle, you, therefore can not participate in it. You may sympathize, fine. But you can't sympathize, and assist because once you get involved in it you want it to go your way, and it's not going to go your way. You're not going to direct. We're not going to . . . if we disagree with you it's because we know best. There was this conflict, there was this real conflict. I mean, it's easy to say, "Well, we thought they were taking over". That's not really what was going on. What was going on was a conflict in thought. I mean, you can't, and looking back at it it's easier to see. You can't allow discontent. You know, you can't allow these guys to challenge you when you put something on the table which is what happens. Because, as the movement was changing and discussions within the MLK were going on, you know, people would say, "Well, what do you think about [H.] Rap Brown or what do you think about or what do you think about . . ." shucks, . . . any one of these other guys? HEBERT: James Farmer. Those kinds of people. HAMILTON: "What about the Panthers, you know? Don't you think they were wrong to do such and such?" "Hell no, they weren't wrong!" You know, you'd always have arguments because the white group that was involved in MLK was not really the white group that was the activist, radical white group. HEBERT: Which was the Progressive Student . . . HAMILTON: Which was the peace movement white group. HEBERT: Right. HAMILTON: So, that group was going off in another direction, and you were really left with basically pacifists. And it wasn't going to work. HEBERT: The movement had moved beyond the plane of pacifism.  3  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: [078] The movement had moved beyond that. was the real problem. The real problem was this movement from pacifism to activism to real, God, I don't want to say radical movement because it was characterized as that when it really wasn't. Not here. I mean, nationally it was, but not here. It never really got to that level here. That, I think, characterized the conflict between the peace movement breaking out of the civil rights movement and the civil rights movement moving towards activism. And those coupled together destroyed, I think, the Martin Luther King Action Movement more than anything else. Because the two leaderships, those two groups just took off, I mean, it just took off. HEBERT: Speaking of Martin Luther King, I just thought back, I didn't ask you, were you involved in any of the civil rights organizations during your high school time? HAMILTON: Oh, NAACP. That sort of thing. HEBERT: Never CORE, or SNCC, or SCLC? HAMILTON: No, no, no, no, not them. Not them at all. I mean, you read about it, obviously, and you talk about it but there were no active high school chapters. There were chapters at Southern, and I know friends and had friends who were more involved because they were . . . again, in the loop. I wasn't in the loop at that time. You know, when you weren't in, in a black school, you were kind of out of the loop, and they weren't going to let you in the loop. So, you missed out on that, really, and that was an interesting kind of a concept. The interesting thing to me at that time was the movement was fighting to integrate the country and the people who were placed in the forefront of integration were getting lost, because that effort was no longer the principal goal of the new movement. And it's a tragedy. But that's really kind of what, we were kind of caught in the middle because it was almost . . . The movement was moving towards where we almost are now, towards separatism really. It was moving toward self-identity, self-improvement, even though now I think they lost the idea of self-improvement, but that's another thing. HEBERT: So this was a time when the Black Power Movement was coming out? HAMILTON: Oh yes. The Black Power Movement was, nationally, mid-'60s. Here . . . HEBERT: It happened here later. HAMILTON: I'd say it really took hold here about '67, '67, '68, really took hold here. I mean, we didn't begin to have Afros in Baton Rouge till '70s. You know shucks, I mean, we were kind of slow, you know. I mean, it's Baton Rouge. It didn't really pick on, pick up here till '67, '68, and '69, I mean it really began here. It was like, okay, this movement is going to last, it's for real. So, where I think nationally the heat of the Black Power Movement was '60s, mid '60s, '64-5-6-7-, the heat really wasn't here until '69, '70, '71, '72, that kind of strange time thing.  4  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HEBERT: Well, Baton Rouge is a little slower than the . . . HAMILTON: Oh yes, agreed. HEBERT: Did [H.] Rap Brown being from this area have any effect or any influence on the Black Power Movement here in Baton Rouge? Would he come down . . . HAMILTON: Well, I'll tell you. It didn't have any effect until those time periods that I mentioned. It's too laid back. I mean, people here, first of all you've got to realize his age group didn't buy into it. My age group did. HEBERT: So your age group did, the next generation bought in to it? HAMILTON: Yes. His age group didn't buy into it here. I mean, here. I mean, nationally, yes, but not . . . [H.] Rap Brown was, what, six years older than me. He's six, seven years older than me, so his age group didn't buy into it; my age group bought into it. So that difference in time just between the two of us in a place like Baton Rouge is a significant difference. Guys who were within his age group, a guy like Jody Bivens [?], who was big in the Baton Rouge area in the Black Power struggle, were considered crazy. "These guys are nuts, you know, wild." And . . . HEBERT: Murphy Bell . . . HAMILTON: Kind of wild. I mean, by the time he was big nationally it became obvious to Louisiana. "Oh God, he's from here." "Oh, you know, that guy's from right over there on Grant Street." You know, "Oh man", you know, that kind of thing. He was just another big mouth guy in Baton Rouge for a long time, you know. And then it dawned on the community here at the college level that this guy had credibility and that he was a part of a major movement. I mean, it just was slow getting here. HEBERT: How did certain incidents, such as the march from Bogalusa to Baton Rouge in 1967 affect you? Do you remember those kinds of things? HAMILTON: Oh sure, I remember it. I remember it . . . at the time it was great. I mean, we thought it was outstanding. The idea . . . remember the date on that? What was the date? HEBERT: It's 1967, August thirteenth, fourteenth.  5  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: Right. Okay. I was in high school and it was a big thing, a tremendously big thing. I think it did a whole lot, not just in Baton Rouge, but in the state toward making the state more involved in the national movement. But, the big national movements, big national marches, were when? HEBERT: They were earlier than this, right? HAMILTON: Right. So, again, this is all a part of the fact that the movement was slow getting here. I mean, this was eight to ten years after the movement and marches became considered viable tools nationally. So, I mean, that's a good picture of how the movement is changed. HEBERT: Why do you think it was slower here in Baton Rouge, in Louisiana? HAMILTON: [144] I think it was slower in Louisiana for a number of reasons. One is, the dichotomy of life here. Black people and white people live so closely in practical aspects. I mean, neighborhoods overlap. The "across the track" thing isn't as obvious in many places in Louisiana; it just really isn't. The ability to talk was, I think, part of the problem, and, it was a problem that it was slow. The other thing is, I honestly believe, that we're so ignorant. I mean, we're a poorlyeducated state. We're very, very ignorant. Within the black community, fear of that ignorance and what it could do, that . . . Because I've had, I had teachers talk to me about this. I've gone back and talked to them, people who taught me when I was in junior high school, who said there was a fear that pushing for integration too quickly would hurt the black community. That, yes, we knew it had to be done, but if you pushed too quickly it would hurt the community because we weren't ready. So there was, I think, a feeling among the leadership that we weren't ready. That these people, there are people within this community who understand it has to be done, but we're going to all work together toward that and, that's something you can't control. You just can't control it. I think that there were knowledgeable, concerned people who saw it and who realized it had to happen and that we were a part of it but who wanted to control it in a manner such that it created less conflict. That there was a serious effort to do that. But I think, as anything, of that magnitude, it gets uncontrollable because you can't control people's desires and, ignorant or not, television is there. I mean, you could see it. You're a part of it. Everyday, you go to these churches and you talk. Then, it just snowballed. Then, the fact that there's a college there, a college campus right there, and the fact that that college campus had students from all across the country who came in here telling us how jerks, what big jerks we were, and how slow we were, and I just think it just got out of hand. I think Southern, and tv, brought new leadership, and I think that new leadership was as big a part of it as anything else because the old leadership wanted it to be slow. HEBERT: So the old leadership, say the NAACP-type leaders and the church leaders.  6  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: Well, I mean the NAACP, the church leaders, even the white leaders who were conscious enough of the eventuality of it, who could talk to those black leaders all thought they could control it. I really believe that's what happened. I really believe there was an effort to say, "Okay, we all know this is coming, guys. What's the best way for us to do this so it creates as little turmoil as possible?" And everyone agreed, Well it's got to be gradual." I think there was positioning in such a manner that this was the best way they thought to attack. HEBERT: Who were some of these leaders? HAMILTON: And then, of course, you always had these idiots who just weren't going to see it. That's natural. HEBERT: So who were some of these leaders? Do you think of any . . . HAMILTON: I think of people like Jemison and all of those guys. J. K. [Haynes], L. L. Haynes, all those people. I think they saw this and I think that the march, oh . . . Golly, what's his name, A. Z., all of them. HEBERT: A. Z. Young. HAMILTON: I think they saw it. You know, but I think they had to be convinced that it couldn't happen that way. "That no, even though we do think it has to be gradual, you people are never going to understand it, so no. It's never going to be slow enough for youall. Slow enough for you-all is not fast enough for us." Because the heat was on these guys, I mean, the community began to put heat on. I don't think there's any question about it. The community began to say, "Wait a minute. Are you-all with us, or are you-all with them? What do you mean wait? Wait for how long? Tired of waiting." HEBERT: And the waiters, the people who wanted to wait, were called "Uncle Toms." I've read some . . . newspaper articles about things like that. HAMILTON: Exactly! Exactly! Oh yes. HEBERT: What about the national groups, such as CORE, coming in to Bogalusa, and things like that? HAMILTON: That helped. HEBERT: That helped to push . . . HAMILTON: That helped to push these guys, but I think, that's again, a product of the fact that black college students . . .  7  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HEBERT: Who were involved in these organizations. HAMILTON: Right. Because these guys had connections that were outside of this immediate leadership group. They had connections in other states, nationally. I mean, you know, you're talking about, you have a college campus here. And the consequences of having kids in college makes a difference. I mean, A. Z. probably had to deal with people coming back to Monroe wanting to know what the hell was going on, you know. "Come on Z., what are we doing?" It's it just got out of hand, I think, from their perspective. And that's the way it should be. I don't have any problem with that. HEBERT: So, let's talk about Harambe and your involvement in that organization. Let's see, what's the history of that organization? Is it a national organization, or . . .? HAMILTON: [205] No. No, it's not. Actually, it's a local, here, on the campus. The organization started about '68, '67, between '67 and '68. And was, my freshman year, the first effort to really make a black power statement, a self-consciousness statement. I mean, the really big thing about Harambe was it was part of the movement to emphasize black pride. Harambe, the term is part of an expression, a Swahili expression, "harambe uhuru,": "let's pull together." HEBERT: Okay. HAMILTON: And it was the first effort toward moving back to identifying African roots, the whole thing. It made an effort to look at a global black power struggle, because, I mean, that was the first time there was an emphasis on looking at Africa, South Africa, the problems in Rhodesia, those type things. I mean, they really were like, "Let's educate ourselves on what the struggle is and let's, now, begin to look at actively changing our plight here." So, there were efforts made to say target things that need to be done on this campus. Target people who are best able to get these things done. Let's begin looking at how we're going to do it. And let's begin looking at what's wrong. Let's begin to, instead of sitting around ignoring it or pretending it's not there, or walking around it, let's attack it head on. Like fraternities. We're tired of them, and we're tired of them being the assholes they are, you know, picking on us, using us as the brunt of their jokes, whatever. You know, let's go at them. Let's not have it anymore. I mean, it was a really activist attempt to deal with it. HEBERT: How did you go after fraternities? HAMILTON: [229] Well, first thing is, we decided to approach the administration about some of the things the fraternities were doing. Like the, the, well, the biggest thing . . . I was trying to figure out what the biggest thing. The biggest thing we were mad about at the time was this football party weekend deal where they, just, really they did some really racist kind of things in those days. I mean, they used to, like, hang black people in effigy  8  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B and stuff. And, I mean, they used to do some goddamn weird things, and even at . . . At football games, if there were black players on other teams, they'd scream, "Niggers" and hang effigies and do all kinds of wild-ass things. Okay? And not to mention, you couldn't walk by, if you were black, you couldn't walk by the fraternity house. I mean, they'd harass you, harangue you. Well, I mean, on occasions have attacked people after football games, okay? So it was like, "Look, hey, these people are going rampant over there. You-all got to do something about this foolishness." That whole confederate slavery thing, you know, attacking that kind of nonsense. I mean, believe me, these people today still talking about the flag, nothing compared to what those people used to do then, okay? Believe me. I mean, they were, they were asinine. They were . . . That situation was as bad as when I was in high school. Those people were just stupid. Drunk assholes is what they used to be. And it was just ridiculous. And then there was the concern about this not being able to participate in things, like fraternities. Black-balling. I mean, there were some black kids who wanted to be in fraternities and sororities. Not going to be. . . HEBERT: And attempted to join these. HAMILTON: Yes. And attempted to join. And were embarrassed, harassed, and the whole thing. Because, you know, during those days, everybody got rush letters. You didn't know that when you got a damn letter that you went down to rush they were going to treat you like shit! You come down there thinking you got a chance to be in a fraternity or a sorority. And these people were really asinine to these people. I mean, awful, okay? That sort of thing. It was awful, okay? I mean, they were really awful people. HEBERT: And so Harambe contacted which organizations? HAMILTON: [257] Well, we started with, we started with Dean Reddoch. Jerk! You know, and he was not sympathetic nor did he consider it a problem! I mean, he was like, well, you know, he was just really a jerk, okay. Then, we decided we needed to just go . . . I mean, going through these channels and, we decided, "Hey, stop. We just need to take this to the chancellor." We went to Chancellor Taylor, who was a nice man, really nice man, but Chancellor Taylor was not confrontational. Just wanted to believe the best way was for everybody to try to kind of work things out. Real nice man, who preferred to not be involved in any confrontation. So a lot wasn't getting done, okay. It wasn't getting done. And ultimately, I mean, we had a sit-in, I think, in his office one time, where he did assign some people to work with us and try to identify problems. Don Tarver was the guy, I think, he assigned to work with us and begin attacking some of these things. And begin looking at why black students were not allowed to participate in the social part of this campus. Because, I mean, that was . . .  9  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B In all honesty, I didn't consider it that a big a problem. Two, one, because I'd gone through this. It's like, "Hey, hang these people!" I mean, this big campus is big enough to do what I'm going to do, and if they don't get in front of me I don't give a darn. I mean, that was really my original approach to the whole thing as an individual. Secondly, the way I looked at it, if we worked hard enough at it, it was going to work despite them. I just didn't care that they were going to be jerks. I'd lived with that before. The way you deal with them is you make them out to be the jerks they are and let everybody see they're jerks. Sooner or later, it's going to work against them. But, you know, you've got more people concerned about it, you got a bigger campus, which means the problem might take longer to be recognized and solved so you have to work on it. That's when we decided, well, okay, some of these people wanted chapters from Southern to come in and begin setting up chapters here so we had to work with the administration to allow them to do that, and to become a part of the Interfraternity Council. I never joined one those things. I don't believe in fraternities, sororities. I don't believe in that. I just think that's nonsense. I think that whole idea creates atmosphere for conflicts because they create these elitist cliques. I mean, I'm sorry, but that's my feeling on it. HEBERT: But you thought if students wanted to join those fraternities then they had every right to join them. HAMILTON: Well, they ought to have a right to do it. You can have a right to do whatever the devil you want to do. No, not whatever the devil, but if you want to join . . . HEBERT: I know what you mean. HAMILTON: [291] Yes, fine. I helped them do that. I was a part of a group that said, "Okay, fine. You-all want to be in these things, fine, but I assure you they did more harm than good, in my feelings. HEBERT: These . . . How'd you get to be the president of Harambe? You ran in your sophomore year, did, were you elected your sophomore year? HAMILTON: Summer of my sophomore year. It was interesting because, in all honesty, I didn't, I didn't want to do it, initially, because I had a different approach than the other group who was in who . . . When I came into this school, as I mentioned earlier, there were like two groups of black students: those who had gone to integrated schools and those who had not. And just as I had the problem in Baton Rouge, "Those of you-all who went to white schools, you-all think you're white," there was that feeling on the campus from those students who didn't go to white school. "Well, then what the hell are you doing here now?" You know, that kind of stuff. It created a different approach toward things. I believed then, I believe now, sure there are a lot of jerks here who will never get it and there are a lot of people here who it will take a great deal to get them to see, but there are people here who will work with us. We  10  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B just, we have to find them. The other thing is, the university has to make this work, or the university is going to fall. Period. And the people in administration know that. We just have to make them understand that we are going to help it fall if you don't get involved in this, okay? So get involved in this. Their approach was, "Screw the whole thing, let's just make them understand it by beating them over the head." Well, if you beat everybody over the head too much you're never going to help, they're just always going to be against you. That was my, and it was, it was a conflict that was apparent and at that time, about to get really bad. Actually, a couple of people said, "Man, you ought to run." Because I have a big mouth. I used to get in arguments with this other group a lot. So, okay, I'll run. At the time there was also the feeling that, "Well, gee, it's all going to fall apart any damn way because this place is so awful they're going to kill us. They're going to make it fall apart." So, there was this challenge to try to make the thing work, and that's really kind of how it happened. HEBERT: Who were some of the other leaders? I've read Dale, Dale Marcelle's name. Marcelle. HAMILTON: Dale was involved. You mean in Harambe? HEBERT: In Harambe. HAMILTON: Oh gosh, let's see. Johnny Gill, Charles Cochraham . . . HEBERT: Ivory T. Walker, was he in Harambe? Or . . . it seems Ivory . . . HAMILTON: Yes. Frank Roubleau, Harold Deselle . . . HEBERT: Were there very many women in Harambe? HAMILTON: Yes, yes. Barbara Favorite, Maleka, I forgot what her name was before Maleka . . . HEBERT: What about Jacqueline Jones? HAMILTON: Yes, Jackie. Louanna Peters [?]. Last time I saw Louanna [?], she was working for a congressman, Gus Savage. Awful man, but she was working for him. Gosh, I've forgotten that other girl's name, too. Because there were really a lot of women. HEBERT: Gwendolyn, Gwendolyn, someone named Gwendolyn. I came across the name last night.  11  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: Gosh, and I just saw this lady. She's at Southern. She works at Southern now. But there were a lot of women. In fact, there were more women leaders than men, really. HEBERT: Really? There was also an effort, on the part of the women, to change dorm, housing rules and stuff on campus. Well, treatment in housing. HAMILTON: Right. Well, there was an effort to change everything. To be honest about it. HEBERT: Everything, that's right. What about the Black Cultural Center? That was a fight that Harambe won. HAMILTON: Yes. The Cultural Center idea came up during, I guess '71, '72, after, after the marches and after the, I can't remember whether it happened after the students died at Southern . . . HEBERT: Let me check. HAMILTON: But, golly. HEBERT: I think it was . . . HAMILTON: No, it was before. HEBERT: It was before. It was 1971. HAMILTON: [355] It was before. But, when we, it was after, it was after a sit-in in the Chancellor's office, that's when it was. After we did that, there was this effort, again, as I was saying, to get black people more involved in the social aspect of the campus and to get the white students and the state to recognize the contributions of black people to the state and to the university. I mean it's like, "Look, you have this Afro-, this AngloAmerican art museum over here, this natural science museum over here that extols everything except the contributions of black people. This ought to be about providing, the state and the university community, some information about the role of black people in this state." Now that was a big fight because, and I mean a big fight within the group, first, because within the group, the initial move was a BSU, a Black Student Union. That was then initial call. The initial call was, "We need a BSU" because BSU's were popular all over the country. There was a group, a small group that disagreed with that, that thought the BSUs were counter- productive because BSUs promote separatism, more so than integration. And that it doesn't, in the long run, aid in your total campus involvement, it just isolates you more. Big, big, big fight about that, but we won. The group won that wanted a center as opposed to a BSU. We put the idea to the campus, was able to get,  12  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B campus administration, was able to get assigned some property, was able to get some assistance from the architecture and, what do you call that darn school, the School of Environmental Design, to help us come up with a design for the project, an initial fund-raising project, and just get the idea off the ground. But it was always conflict, always. Because there was always the group that wanted the BSU lingering and upset, festering, because they always thought that idea didn't work. The idea was, involved white people. It involved something of an integrationist concept more akin to the old Martin Luther King Action Movement fight than what we ought to be about, which is Black Power, self-identity, black pride, the whole thing. Johnny Gill . . . Johnny Duncan started leading that group. Johnny became a real big spokesman, Johnny and Terry Bonnie, Johnny Duncan and Terry Bonnie became real big spokespersons for that group. And the younger members began to move toward that more. As anything, you know, it changes, you got transition. They just believed that the idea of integration was the wrong way to go, that the idea of desegregation was the way to go. And there's a distinct difference. Desegregation means removal of forced barriers. That's all. Integration means something much broader than that, much broader than that. It actually means working together. Some, some effort, at merging ideas, philosophies, people, and interacting. They weren't about that. I don't mean it to say that they were right or wrong as much as they didn't agree. As much as from their background and perspectives, they didn't see it accomplishing anything. They were part of a group, Johnny was a part of a group who came from an integrated environment in high school, which was very unsuccessful. Unsuccessful, not in the sense that they didn't put the bodies there, but unsuccessful in the sense that they didn't get people working together. It was violent. It was always confrontational. Those people . . . It never got beyond where my initial high school years got. I mean, I saw through my high school years it getting better. I mean, it may have gotten worse by now, I don't know. But they never saw it even getting better. I mean, they never got to where they even saw people hanging out or talking to each other. They had a lot of bitterness, a significant amount of bitterness. Their feeling was, "These people will never do right. They will never want us to succeed. They will always do their most, utmost, to keep us down." I mean, that's just their feeling. Like I said, I don't know if they're right or they're wrong, I mean, but that's . . . There's a significant . . . There was a significant amount of animosity and hate in that. I could see that group moving up. My senior after that, I mean, Johnny became president. HEBERT: That's right. HAMILTON: And I think that had a lot to do with what happened after that. HEBERT: So the Cultural Center never became a reality?  13  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HAMILTON: No, because what happened after I left was, and we had set up an initial, as I mentioned we had a fund raiser, set up an account . . . HEBERT: And you had a house corporation . . . HAMILTON: Fund-raising, and we had to set up a corporation and so forth. Too much stuff, in their vernacular, "No. White folks stuff. No." They moved back toward the idea of a BSU, and there was a building off campus. The darn union building was using it as a storage and supply place at the time. Johnny always wanted things now. He wasn't about patience. They opted to buy the building with the funds that we had and, not buy the building, but opted to swap the property on Dalrymple Drive for the property over on Highland Road and to use the monies to renovate the building. And it became a BSU. The disheartening thing to me, more than anything, more than his idea winning out over my idea, one way or the other, was that I lived, at that time, in those apartments right across the street from that place. God, I've forgotten the name of those darn things. They were townhouse apartments. Tiger Townhouse, I think? Yes. Everyday, I had to walk out there and see that building. They all gathered together one Saturday afternoon, and I watched them. They painted it red, black, and green, which was the flag of the struggle at the time. It was more green than anything, but. But it was awful. I mean, it was like, everyday I would get out of my house, every morning, and I had to walk past that damn building. I was like, "Jesus Christ!" And everyday I'd have to deal with the fact that this is not going to work. This is counter-productive. I watched it just go down, and down, and down. I say that, not, again, not meaning that I think that Johnny meant anything wrong or anything bad, because he didn't. He honestly didn't. He believed in what he was saying. And his experiences, I think, had a great deal to do with his believing that. He had no reason to believe that integration was ever going to work. He had no reason to trust white people. I mean he had no reason to do that. HEBERT: What part of the state was he from? Do you remember? HAMILTON: Bogalusa. HEBERT: Oh, okay. HAMILTON: He had no reason to believe that. There's a number of other people who have no reason to believe that. That's the tragedy of it, because any effort to make a longterm program that is student-based is difficult because you have transition. You have people in and out of school, the whole thing. It depends, so much, on support from outside. You cannot garner that kind of support if you don't, one, show responsibility, and two, make a conscious effort to show that you're prepared to cooperate with people. He never understood that. He never saw the next day. He only saw today, and that's a real problem. But he was an honest, sincere guy, in all respects. But, you know, you just have different view points.  14  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 453, Side A. Begin Tape 453, Side B HEBERT: Well, we've about finished up this tape . . . [End Tape 453, Side B. End Session I.]  15														Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections, LOUISiana Digital Library, Baton Rouge, La.	E-mail lsudiglib@lsu.edu with questions or comments.
