Transcript Hamilton 471A										Narrator: Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Interviewer: Mary Hebert Session II Transcriber: Robin Cate 11 September 1993 [Begin Tape 471, Side A. Begin Session II.] HEBERT: [002] This is Mary Hebert. It is September eleventh, 1993, and I'll be talking to Leo Hamilton and we're at Hill Memorial Library on LSU's campus. Last time we were talking about Harambe and we covered a lot of the more political aspects of it, or the social aspects of it. What about Harambe as a social organization? Was that a big role? HAMILTON: Oh sure. There really wasn't any social life outside of Harambe, the Martin Luther King Action Movement, and I would say the churches, the Episcopal Center, the Baptist Student Center, the Student Union, and maybe the Catholic Center. But a significant part of what Harambe did was social because it was so easy to make the social and the cultural aspect of it work. And so much of what we were concerned about was feeling needed, feeling wanted, feeling that you could live, you could survive here. It wasn't just a question of whether of were you going to pass the courses or hang around on the campus. I mean, people just kind of felt alone, so you had to use that to develop a social point of view. HEBERT: You would meet a lot at St. Alban's, the Episcopal Center. How'd you go about getting that to be one of the meeting places out there? HAMILTON: Actually, it just happened, originally. It just became, it was a place where people studied. Then, Father Wood, Chuck Wood, was a really, really, nice guy. And he talked to people. He took the opportunity to speak with the students and . . . Recognizing that it had become, I guess de facto, a place that people sort of hung out in, eventually, he offered us the opportunity to use one of those rooms as a meeting room. It became meeting and social, the whole thing, which was a large, multi-purpose type room, I really don't know what Chuck and those guys had used it for previously. Then, the other center would have been the Wesley Center where Will Fennan [?] was over there at the time. HEBERT: That's the Methodist . . . HAMILTON: Yes, that's the Methodist Center. I'm Methodist, so, I met Will, really through my minister. Actually, his center became a focal point because they had Sunday meals, Sunday evenings. The membership at University Methodist would sponsor these Sunday meals for the students. Great meals! I mean, these ladies would come in and fix these outstanding meals on Sunday night, and you couldn't beat it, I mean, it's free! So,  1  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A that became another area where, I mean, we went from the meals . . . Then, Will was also interested in promoting more integration and diversity on the campus. So, I think those two guys and the fact that you got the impression that their centers, the buildings, the environment, the people, were warm and you . . . It just sort of gravitated toward it. I mean, it helped that they were as warm as they were and not pushy. I mean, they really just sort of opened it up and it helped itself. So, that had a lot to do with it. Those became more places to socialize than even the Union building in those days. The Union building had more to offer but it was, it's big, it's cold, it's . . . It was not the same feel. It really wasn't. I mean, you almost had a home feel there. HEBERT: Were these ministers activist ministers in more areas than just integration? I know that a lot of the women's movement came out of St. Alban's and so they promoted social activism. HAMILTON: Oh, yes. Oh, yes. Oh, both those guys. Really nice men. Both progressive, in, what I would consider a progressive manner, in all sorts of social issues and it really made a difference, really made a difference. I don't know if that's as much reflection of the tenets of their faith or just that they were those kind of people, but they were open and warm people as much as the tenets of their faith. HEBERT: Did Harambe do other kinds of social activities, like go to football games together, or . . . HAMILTON: Well, yes, we did that. Initially, we were concerned that, the fraternities had this practice of sending pledges, or new members, to the games to cordon off the best seats. I don't know where the student section is now, but we used to go to about the forty yard line, which was great! Good seats. They'd have the seats between the forty and the end zone just cordoned off, and they were pretty rude about it. I mean, they'd get damn mean about it. They were their seats. So you always got pushed in to the upper end zone area, sometimes lower end zone, but still end zone. And there were some of us who like football. I like football, I like the sport and I like, and I'd been a fan. So it's, we made efforts to get better seats, get there early enough, challenge them for the seats, as well as after football game parties, and stuff like that. I mean, it was so important that you made people comfortable. It's very difficult to make it through the university environment if you don't have a cultural aspect or social aspect of it. It's just very difficult. It's not like you can go to class and go home. I mean, you're still here, so . . . A lot of these folks lived . . . I was fortunate. I didn't live on campus. A lot of people did live on, most people lived on campus who participated in the programs we were doing, and it was just the only avenue they had. It's kind of difficult for you to appreciate that now, because, I mean, you just walk all over the campus, you see black people everywhere and people on the teams. You know, in those days, I mean, there weren't any on the teams, okay! I mean, we were talking about Lora [Hinton] when we came in. I remember when we were making efforts to get them to recruit black athletes.  2  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A HEBERT: [089] How did you go about doing that? Would you petition the athletic department or try sit-ins? HAMILTON: Yes, well we met with . . . we even, we did that. In our initial sit-in in the Chancellor's office, that was one of our lists of demands that the university make an effort to recruit black athletes in all the sports. And we met with Carl Maddox and Charlie McClendon at the time, and they really had a pretty asinine attitude about it, I'll be honest with you. They weren't, I don't think, I won't say that they were blatantly racist about it. That may have been what they felt. What they said to us, they just felt like, "Well, gee, we don't," I mean, suppose, I mean, I'll never forget this, Carl Maddox said, "Well, hell, suppose the Jewish students said they wanted Jewish athletes. Do you think we ought to go out and recruit Jewish athletes?" It was like, "Yes! Damn it, if that's what it's going to take to make you win, first, and if it's what's going to take to make the university a more well-rounded environment, yes! I mean, you're not isolated, you're on a university campus. Yes." So, I mean, they just didn't really take a pretty, I thought, open attitude about it. I thought they were asinine. HEBERT: Did you all also push to get black basketball players? HAMILTON: Oh, athletes all across the board. I mean, we worked with Coach Brown when we came in. HEBERT: When did Collis Temple come in? HAMILTON: After Coach Brown came in. Maybe, gee, I don't know if it was first or second recruiting year for Coach Brown, but it was after Coach Brown. There just weren't any and there really wasn't any effort to recruit any. Of course, that was not just here, it was throughout the league, the Southeastern Conference. You know, I mean, I absolutely remember Adolph Rupp, I mean, we called, of course we called him Adolph Hitler Rupp. He never wanted black athletes at Kentucky. You know, Kentucky dominated the league. And he made the statement, I mean, it was, shit, in the papers. He made the statement that he didn't need them and wasn't going to recruit them. I remember the first black athlete there, recruited a guy named Paine. Tom Paine. Seven footer. And he eventually went to jail for some offense, rape or something, I can't remember what it was, something awful. But, there was several articles, newspaper and magazine stories about him. But I can imagine what he must have felt like on that campus and, God, I wish I could remember the magazine where the statement was made, but there was an article in the magazine where Rupp was quoted as saying, you know, he didn't need any black athletes, he didn't want any. And the only reason he had this guy was because he was a freak! So it's like, you know, that, you can't imagine the psychology of being on this campus or any campus where the only members of your race you see are in service capacities, in lower service capacities. You know, if the only, only black people you see are serving you food or cleaning up the building it's like, hey.  3  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A You want to believe that you belong. You want to believe that you have, and your people have more to contribute to this environment than those things. And I think that it is those things that contribute to those other people feeling like you don't belong. Because that's how you feel, you said, "Gee," the rest of the student body was like, "Well, what the hell do we got these people here for?" You always felt like an intruder. You just really always felt like an intruder. If you weren't, even if you weren't openly attacked, verbally or physically, you always felt like an intruder, or you were always made to feel like, "What are you doing here?" you know, "Why are you at our school? Why the devil are you making us go through having you around?" It was just an awful feeling. HEBERT: You mentioned the sit-in in the Chancellor's office. Was that 1970? Or '71? HAMILTON: Gosh, let's see. That would have been, I guess '71. HEBERT: And at the sit-in you went in with a demand, with a list of demands. HAMILTON: Oh yes. HEBERT: Was that the one with calling for black counselors and . . . HAMILTON: Counselors, teachers . . . HEBERT: Black history courses? Or was there a black history course at that time? HAMILTON: No, that was, that was part of the demand, too, curriculum changes. Yes, counselors for sure. HEBERT: Was this the sit-in where James Reddoch went in and demanded that you leave, well, the group leave and . . . HAMILTON: Yes. Yes. HEBERT: And called the police. Was that the one? HAMILTON: Yes, that was the one. I think that was '71, oh yes. It might have been spring '71. Something like that. Yes, he was such a jerk. HEBERT: How'd you feel to have a, the riot team called on you? HAMILTON: Well, first of all, you anticipated it. I mean, you went in anticipating that that was always a possibility, which is why we prepared by asking everyone not to be disruptive. To not, as far as possible, block egress, and get in the way of people doing what they would normally do in their workday, as possible. I mean, obviously you've got a bunch of people in the way, it's more difficult than it normally is.  4  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A But you wanted to be conscious of the fact that a certain amount of disorder subjects you to arrest and possible harm, physical harm. But at the same time, we felt like, part of what was the university's best defense was the ability to keep everything in-house. So, what we really wanted to do was to make it known outside of the campus, that there's a problem here. You almost hoped they were going to do it, and knowing Dean Reddoch, you were almost certain he was going to do it. I think we talked about this last time, one of the reasons was, I mean it was his job to be a jerk. I know that. I think he liked it, but I think it was his job. I think that knowing the kind of personality that Chancellor Taylor had, I mean, just a nice man, he would never make, be confrontational. He would never be confrontational. He would avoid it at whatever cost. He just wouldn't meet with us. I mean, I don't think it was that he didn't want to solve a problem or didn't recognize that, he just wouldn't want to be made to meet with you at that juncture or in that situation. HEBERT: Did he meet with you later on? HAMILTON: Yes, he did. He did. But he just didn't like that kind of confrontational approach. I mean, he's the kind of guy who would have preferred if you'd given him a call and had five or six other people give him a call and convince him, okay, meet with him. Which might have taken a month, or whatever the hell, but . . . No! You have to meet and you have to face these people. He was not that kind of person. I mean, I think, if you were forced into realizing, "Well, I got to meet with them. Okay, I'll meet with him." But he just would prefer not to do that. Dean Reddoch, no. He didn't mind. He was kind of a bad guy. And there was this, you know, you always have to have a hatchet man. HEBERT: And that was his job. HAMILTON: And that was his job. He was a pretty good hatchet man, I thought. I thought he relished it, you know? He was darn good at it. HEBERT: What about . . . was there a concern about housing and dorm rooms and dorm room assignments and . . . HAMILTON: Oh yes. HEBERT: I was speaking to Dee Williams a while back, and she said that they didn't have any kind of policy to keep black students together and white students together, and they would sometimes assign black and white students in the same rooms. HAMILTON: Right. Right. HEBERT: But then, the white student would always find an excuse to find another roommate.  5  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A HAMILTON: Right. It was interesting because, initially, initially the idea of integration was so imbedded in the students who came to this school, the black students who came to this school, that you were conscious of efforts to separate and you were conscious of defeating efforts to separate. And there really were concerns if that happened. I mean, if the luck of the draw, and the average, if the average process was the luck of the draw, you have no ability to choose your roommate, and you take whom you get, fine. You were concerned that the university encouraged, or supported, I won't even say encouraged, because you don't know that but supported efforts of people to segregate, to say, "Well, I'm not going to be in a room with a black person. Move me out." Then we worked toward stopping that. Gradually, though, the effort began movement toward, "Well, I'd rather be with somebody black." And it's really kind of a strange progression because, I think two things contribute to that. First of all, the people who were roomed with white students, and the effort was successful, became ostracized by some black students because, "Well, you're living with that white person, you think you're this way." Almost like what . . . HEBERT: What happened to you . . . HAMILTON: . . . I mentioned earlier. The other thing that happened was the more we became involved in the black power struggle as opposed to the civil rights movement because they're two . . . they're . . . one is the whole, one is a part of the whole. The more you became conscious of blackness and of the effort to promote it and the effort to promote pride and consciousness, black history, black culture. The more the drive was toward, "Well, wait a minute. Time out on this while we focus on this. Let's focus on this cultural awareness and this identity thing and this pride thing, and we'll work toward that later, toward this integration thing later. Perhaps integration isn't working as well because we aren't comfortable with ourselves. Let's become more comfortable with ourselves, and then we'll try to go back out here and do this again." And it's kind of a, a fine line. It's a dangerous thing to try, game to try to play, because, well, when are you going to be comfortable enough? Who's going to make a decision as to when you're comfortable enough? Who's going to wait around until you do it? Why should they wait around until you do it? You know, I, those are the kind of things you don't think about while you're working toward that. So, ultimately, I think, that's probably where the situation took sort of a dangerous turn, dangerous turn. HEBERT: Was this almost where the separatism became a part of it with the, not the black cultural center, but the black student union or the black student center? HAMILTON: Well, the whole thing. The whole thing because that was all happening around the same time, and I would think that that was all part and parcel of it. You know, actually, that had been happening nationally for five to seven years, I mean, we were slow. HEBERT: Right.  6  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A HAMILTON: So, we were seeing what was happening in other areas, picking up on it a little late, and during that time, you know, we were experiencing sort of a, God, what's the best word . . . I guess . . . so much frustration at what's happening here . . . God, I don't know what the best word is for this because it really involves Southern . . . disappointment that you could not as easily or readily go to the other side of town for a break. HEBERT: So you were almost, I don't . . . ostracized is a very harsh word, but when you went over to . . . HAMILTON: No, it's the exact word! HEBERT: It's the exact word? HAMILTON: That was exactly what happened. HEBERT: So there wasn't much communication between the black students here at LSU and Southern? HAMILTON: Well, I mean, obviously there's communication with the people you knew. I mean, certainly people went to school with, former classmates were there and you knew them, but as a general feeling, the Southern University . . . There were things going on at Southern University in terms of the overall black movement which impacted us so badly because the same forces that were moving toward identity and pride and culture and closeness and getting away from this integration thing were the same forces that were killing us, in terms of ourselves here, were killing us in terms of Southern because that made those people begin to say, "Well, whoa, those people are over there. They should be here. This should be the focal point of us developing a culture and developing awareness. And they should either come over here or not be a part of this struggle." So, it became difficult to socialize with the Southern University students, as much as anything else, when maybe even a year before, it was easier. You know, it happened so quickly. HEBERT: So this was in the early '70's. HAMILTON: Yes, yes. I mean, it was almost the same thing I went through in high school. It was, I mean, almost identical. It was like, "No. You don't belong to this movement or over here because you're over there at school with those white folks. We're going to consider you part of the enemy." And Harambe was making such an effort to work with them. I mean, we really were conscious of it and we were trying because we did think they were a part of this idea of pride and culture and . . . "Boy, there's a school right here and we need, you know, to bond with these guys and get them to help us." And our idea was we get them to help us, a joint effort, we can make these students appreciate more what we're talking about and where we're going and ultimately make this university recognize its responsibility more. We didn't give a damn about this university other than  7  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A the fact that they wanted theirs to be comparable to it, which is another whole story that I can't even get into because I think it's nonsense, but that's neither here nor there. HEBERT: But Harambe did make efforts to join together with Southern in certain aspects . . . HAMILTON: Oh, certainly. HEBERT: . . . at like, I remember reading about the fraternities and sororities and attempting to have joint ventures there and have them come over here and set up fraternities and sororities and that type of thing. HAMILTON: Yes. Yes. I mean, not to say that there were not some efforts that were successful because there were some that were successful, more so after the riots at Southern than before. The riots at Southern made a big thing. HEBERT: This was in 1971. HAMILTON: Yes. Because the riots were in '72-'73. But, after that it became more easy. Before that, it was very difficult for that, '71 through '73 or late '70 through '73 period. It was difficult. HEBERT: So the . . . HAMILTON: Resentment. There was very serious resentment. HEBERT: On the part of Southern students for LSU students? HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: Did you resent them for resenting you? HAMILTON: [314] Well, sure, you're pissed off at them, but you don't . . . I mean, we were at a disadvantage. HEBERT: There were fewer here. HAMILTON: We wanted, well, the thing was, you wanted so much to belong to some group that you almost . . . Of course, you . . . "Hey don't treat me that way!" I mean, you're angry, of course, because, damn it, "You treat me like these guys treat me. What the hell am I supposed to do?" But at the same time you really wanted them to, I mean, you really wanted them to recognize what you were going through and to, if not like you for it, respect you for it and give you your damn due. You know, give me my due. I mean, I'm over here. I mean, the struggle isn't just on the other side of town, the struggle is here! We're not running away from it; we're right here. At least recognize that. It was  8  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A kind of, I thought, stupid. It just was not very, not a very fruitful time, I thought, for us. We were just really trapped. I think that's still a problem. Not as much so, but it's still a problem. HEBERT: There's still that animosity of students . . . HAMILTON: Well, it is, there's going to be that animosity because as long as there is a Southern University, in the sense of a Southern University, that is a black school, because that's what Southern University is. I mean, as long as there is that, there is going to be conflict between black and white, between black and black, because how valid is it? More importantly, how valid is its need, is its need to the community? That's, those are very difficult questions, very difficult questions. They are going to continue to come up. In the '60s, they were real questions because it's the first time that we looked at it beyond Brown vs. the Board of Education. Brown vs. the Board of Education says separate but equal doesn't work. You can't have your . . . You can't prevent me from going to any school, going to any establishment to eat, whatever the case may be. It didn't address . . . And, beyond that, it said you have to tear the system down. It didn't address what you do with a system that was created as a segregated, as a vehicle to perpetuate segregation, but which has become so, so much a part of a culture that its people won't let it go. It didn't address that. I mean, it said, "Tear it up root and branch." Well, okay. It sounded easy when you went through elementary and secondary schools because there really wasn't a feeling of ownership as much. I mean, there was some. There was very serious resentment when these, some of these schools were torn down, or torn apart because the feeling was, "Well, why my school and not your school? Why are you going to come in here and tear my school up? This is where I've been and my family's been and . . ." But it got even worse when you're talking about a college campus because so many people had gone through it and were successful. It wasn't just a question, it wasn't just a question of it's, it's fostering segregation. It was a question of whether or not it sufficiently met its task, its goal. Its goal was to educate a certain number who were black. It did that, and those people were proud that it did that. In feeling that, they got an ownership toward it. It's a problem that's difficult to resolve now. You've got to remember, those were the first days they were talking about merging the two schools. HEBERT: In the seventies? HAMILTON: Oh yes. It was the first time they discussed the possibility of merging the two schools as a method of resolving the disparity in higher education. HEBERT: Now is this, these cases that are going before the courts right now the result of that attempt? HAMILTON: [380] Oh yes. This is when those things started. This is really when all that started. This is when those discussions became real. It's graduated to where we are now, and the answers are no less obvious, no less obvious. I know, I personally went from appreciating and recognizing a need for a Southern University and the state supporting a Southern University, as is, to where I am today where I think it's nonsense. I  9  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A just think it's nonsense. I don't disagree with those people who support the idea of historically black private colleges. I don't disagree with that, and I give money to the United Negro College Fund because the United Negro College Fund is about private schools, not state-sponsored schools, public schools. I don't think any group owns a public school. The public owns it. Period. You can't have your cake and eat it too. You know, I, the more I hear people make these arguments, the . . . A very good friend of mine, who was a professor at Southern, he and I were going through this argument, and he went through this whole thing about preserving our culture and doing this and doing that and it's our school, and I went home and, I have these books, I got him a copy of a speech given by Orville Forbush [?] to the Arkansas General Assembly, 1954, 1955, discussing the reasons he opposed integrating the University of Arkansas. Same thing. You see what I mean? It's the same thing. You know, you can't act as if this is different. It's not different, it's the same thing. You can't say, "Well, he can't say that but I can." Well, it's the same thing. But we went through that and I, it's the same problem now and the tragedy is I don't think we're any closer to resolving it, I really don't. And it's because we're talking in concepts and not practically what needs to be done, I think, but that's another whole problem. HEBERT: With the Southern riot, the riots at Southern, how did Harambe handle that? I know that there were memorial services on campus, vigils, things like that. One case in particular, it was an integrated group and Harambe left, members from Harambe left the memorial service early that evening, they didn't stay up all night, and there was a plan to march to the capital the next day. HAMILTON: Which we did. Right. HEBERT: Why leave the memorial service? Was it just to rest up for the next day or . . . HAMILTON: [431] Well, it was a couple of reasons. Actually, we were having some internal conflict at the time. That was, at the time, that it was very recently after Kent State and Jackson State. And there was this feeling that there was this great emphasis on the Kent State and the whole idea about the anti-war thing as opposed to the civil rights struggle. And there was some real friction within our ranks, and we needed to talk about it. We actually left and had a meeting because we wanted to clear some things up. And there was a feeling among some people that a march was insufficient, that, particularly a march where we were going down a path which was pretty much mapped out for us by the police and the people, because, I mean, you apply. We applied for a permit, I mean, we weren't going to go to jail. It was like, I mean, again, that was one of those things where I was in charge but there was a group of people who didn't like the way you do things, who didn't appreciate my way of doing things. You know, it's . . . it was always the difference when I was president of that organization versus the other group and one of the reasons I won versus the other group. It was always the question as to whether or not our initial and primary goal was to challenge authority and obtain our  10  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A goals in matters beyond the recognized procedure. "We're going to have a march? Who gives a damn about a permit? Let's just go do it." "Well, no. If we're going to have enough people, that people can get hurt and go to jail, we're going to get a permit, we're going to do this the right way because, hey, they have to let us do it! It's a free country. You got a right to do it. There's a procedure. Let's follow the procedure, let's do it!" That's the way I look at it. Theirs was, "No, hang the procedure. They're wrong. The whole system is wrong. I mean, once you begin adhering to one rule, you may as well follow all the rules. Damn it, let's just do it." So that's where it started, and we had these big fights about that, and . . . The march, the next day, was getting to be really rough because there were some people who, within the white group, in the anti-war group who agreed with those people . . . HEBERT: That didn't want to have . . . right HAMILTON: Gus Tabony, asshole, who was a cop. I mean, those guys, I mean we didn't even know he was a cop . . . HEBERT: Was he an instigator? HAMILTON: But I mean, he was fomenting this group. You know, he was out here like, "Oh great, yea! Hey, hey." It was like, "Hey! Whoa! You guys want to do that? I'm not going to tell you you can't, but I'm going to tell you, this group, this organization is going to do this. Now, what the hell you-all do when you get downtown is up to you, as long as you don't bring any bad faith on this group and what we're trying to do, because we applied, along with all these other people, and we're going to do it right. If there's a route, we're going to follow the devilish route." "Well, we're going to break off and people are going to follow us." "Well, then fine. Let them follow you. We're going to do this." And it was a real problem going on at that time. That's really what that was about. Not that we were upset with the idea of the vigil or . . . I mean, we had some problems. We needed to talk about. HEBERT: The way the Reveille made it sound, it was kind of, "We don't want to be here with you guys. We want to go off and do our own thing." That's how the Reveille made it sound.  11  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A HAMILTON: Well, because, as I mentioned, there were some people within those groups who were saying that. There were people within those groups at all times who did not want to either become part of a larger movement because of the fear, one, it was all white, or two, that it all of a sudden become an anti-war movement as opposed to a civil rights . . . There were always people who felt that. There was always that conflict. Yes, that conflict was one of the reasons we needed to go sit down and say, "Look, folks. Tomorrow is here. We're going to be out here marching in a couple of hours. We don't need this conflict. Let's clear it right now." HEBERT: And did that meeting work? Did you settle anything? HAMILTON: [510] Well, yes. I think it did, because I think the majority of people followed what we wanted to do and went the path we were supposed to go and accomplished what we wanted to accomplish, which was show support for those students, show that we disagreed with what the state was doing in terms of resolving those problems. That's what we wanted to do and show that we thought there were some serious civil rights issues that needed to be addressed. HEBERT: What issues were those, regarding Southern and the way the Southern system was run or the fact that the police officer shot into the crowd and killed? HAMILTON: Oh, clearly. The whole idea of resolving that conflict by putting police officers on the campus fermented that type of occurrence. I mean, I don't think there was any question that put into that environment where there was that much fear, distrust, and anarchy . . . I mean, people were running around going wild and crazy! I mean, anything could have set those people off, particularly when you're dealing with the Baton Rouge Sheriff's department, Baton Rouge City Police Department, a bunch of ignorant, racist people, okay? With guns, and more importantly, afraid, on a college campus around ten thousand black people. They're scared of them any-damn-way, I mean, and, it was just a recipe for disaster to me, on a college campus. And the idea was, you wouldn't do it here. You'd try to resolve the darn problem without having to bring guns on the campus. That was enough. The other thing was, the problems these people are talking about are real. The university does not have sufficient resources. The university is in need of money, in need of physical plants, and there was a really significant feel at that time, one of the other issues was, cultural, courses that reflected black culture. Again, you know, that was during the time when people were getting a feeling of ownership for that university. It's our school, and we can't even teach these kind of courses. Because the courses, the curriculum requirements were so rigidly controlled by the state and the state board and everything. And, you know, there were some things done wrong on the students' side, greatly, I mean, because I knew the guys who were leading this thing. They were not as wellorganized as they should have been. Their planning was poor, and their goals were not  12  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A well-defined. I mean, they were defined broadly, and I think they were able to, in defining them so broadly, generate as much support as they did. But, you know, one of the responsibilities of leadership is, okay, let's define it more clearly toward accomplishing something. They hadn't gotten to that level yet, they just hadn't. Hell, they were students, you know, young students. And . . . HEBERT: Were they students from Baton Rouge? Were they originally . . . HAMILTON: A combination of students from Baton Rouge and other, I mean, Charlene Horton was from Baton Rouge. Fred Prejean was from Lafayette. God, I've forgotten that little guy, the guy who was the main spokesperson. He was from Monroe? HEBERT: And who was he? Do you remember his name? HAMILTON: [579] I can't remember his name. I really can't. I have to ask my wife. She was at Southern at the time. But . . . it was still part, it was something we identified with because it was . . . We'd had, we'd had a march asking for the same things, some of the same things. So we recognized that what they were talking about was part and parcel of our struggle, and there were a sufficient number of white students who recognized that it was part and part of their struggle. It was a great opportunity to get everybody working for the same end, to get the march together and have everybody in support of that. You know, that, to me, was as important as them making a statement that we were going to be as radical as Berkeley or wherever the devil else they were going to . . . because we weren't. You know, come on . . . HEBERT: You're in Louisiana. HAMILTON: "You're not in Berkeley! You're in Baton Rouge! So come on, let's act like we're in Baton Rouge and do it the right way and accomplish what we're going to accomplish because if we do this properly, and we follow the darn permitted route that we're supposed to go on, it's going to be effective." "But no, the TV will catch us more if we go this way and we don't do what we're told to do and they can't stop us if there's . . ." "Well, yes, they can," but that was just ridiculous. HEBERT: Were these people PSA people, people with the Progressive Students Alliance, Ted Schirmer and that group, a lot of them? HAMILTON: Ted was part of that, too. Yes, Ted was wild. He was wild. Ted wanted to go the other way, too. I think, eventually, Ted did. Ted and Gus, and a whole bunch of  13  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A wild ass people. It was crazy. It was really crazy. I just don't think those people thought. I think they . . . You know, you wild, it's the spur of the moment, and you believe that this is the moment to capture, and the way to capture it is the more dramatic way than this other way. And I just never agreed with that. HEBERT: But you did get TV coverage and press coverage on the march from here to there? Without having a riot and getting arrested. HAMILTON: Sure. No, no. I mean, you were going to get that. The whole idea that there were going to be fifty to a hundred to two hundred people out marching about something you're going to get, especially during that time, you were going to get coverage. I mean, just make your statement and make it clearly and try to get it across to where people understand that you're sincere and honest about it. You know, you don't have to blow up the damn building. Just walk down there and make our statement. HEBERT: Did you meet with the governor or any people like that once you got to the state capitol, or . . . HAMILTON: He didn't come out. We asked that he come out, but he didn't come out. There were some . . . HEBERT: Was it Governor McKeithen then? HAMILTON: It was Edwards. HEBERT: It was Edwards. His first term? HAMILTON: It was Edwards' first term. Yes, because right after that . . . Well, I was on the darn Governor's Blue Ribbon Commission to review all that stuff. The Governor's Press Secretary, Dale, God, I've forgotten what Dale's last name was. Well, he was, took a class with me here, and he recommended that I be appointed to one of those, that Blue Ribbon Committee thing, to look at, review it, what had occurred and the whole problem of resolving these, these issues at Southern. But it was Edwards. HEBERT: Was that the committee that the Attorney General set up? HAMILTON: The Governor appointed this. HEBERT: The Governor appointed this committee. Because the Attorney General did have a committee looking into the riots and such. HAMILTON: But the Governor said that. The Governor had set that. HEBERT: What about the Black Muslims and the shootings downtown of the Black Muslims? Did Harambe take any action in that?  14  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side A HAMILTON: No. We did not. We did not take any official action in it at all. We were not . . . I can't say we weren't, well, I can say that, we were not supportive, as a group, of the Muslims. I mean, obviously, there were some people who were sympathetic to them. The Muslims as a group in Baton Rouge never really caught hold here . . . [End Tape 471, Side A. Begin Tape 471, Side B.] HEBERT: [020] So it never caught, it didn't catch hold here? This was late, this was in the seventies. A lot later than it happened in the rest of the country. HAMILTON: Right. Right. And the Muslims just were never a group that caught on really big here. Particularly in those days when there was still conflict among them, I mean, within the Muslims, as to the direction they were going to go in: the Elijah Mohammed group, the Malcolm X group, you know how it is, a whole bunch of stuff was going on at that time and it just never caught on. It just . . . I think their rhetoric was too abrasive, abusive, it just never worked well here. It's difficult in the South, I think, to use that kind of rhetoric and to talk about devils and the whole idea of this harsh separatism when everybody lives so closely around each other. You know, I mean, racism in the South is so different than it is in the Northeast. That's really more of a Northeast or even the West Coast but certainly the Northeast. It's more amenable to that, I think, than it was, certainly then, because the Muslims are doing much better now, but their rhetoric is softer now, than they were then. HEBERT: But Harambe did take, did community work, community service work. I'm thinking of the tutoring program. HAMILTON: Yes, we did. HEBERT: How did that work? How was that set up and how did it work? HAMILTON: Well, actually, we started working it through the churches. It was a principle communication source, identifying within the churches, the ministers and their youth programs and offering students who would volunteer as tutors for children within the schools, minority students who were either going, principally, for those who were going to integrated schools, majority race schools, tutorials and basics courses, and then we did Big Buddy. Actually, it was Big Brother, stuff like that,  15														Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections, LOUISiana Digital Library, Baton Rouge, La. Transcript Hamilton 471B										Narrator: Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Interviewer: Mary Hebert Session II Transcriber: Robin Cate 11 September 1993 Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Tape 471 Side B which were good programs. We didn't get, actually, we didn't get enough support for that. We should have concentrated much more so on that. HEBERT: Well, the university didn't really support that action . . . HAMILTON: No, they didn't. HEBERT: Didn't Chancellor Taylor pull some funding away from the tutoring program? I think it was some funding that the SGA gave to the tutoring program, that the Chancellor pulled out so that it couldn't be given to . . . HAMILTON: Yes. He, I don't, the thing is, I'm not sure, first, they understood what the program was about. Plus, I'm trying to remember when that was . . . HEBERT: This was 1972. I didn't write down a month. It was '72. HAMILTON: [054] They didn't really understand what the program was about. The whole thing about the controls, they didn't think there were sufficient controls on disbursement procedures and . . . We weren't as organized . . . Again, you know, I mean, technically, the mechanics, mechanically organized as we could have or should have been. That's one of those examples of their not taking the time to say, "This is a good idea. Let's show you how to do it." Rather than, "Well, you guys are doing that wrong, take the money." You know, that's sort of . . . It was all dis-communications, as much as anything else, and I think the university did us a disservice on that, even though I think we probably could have handled it a little better ourselves, too, but, they did us a disservice because they were in a better position to offer assistance than they did. HEBERT: How did these tutoring programs work? Did the kids come to places on campus or did you, did people go out to the churches to tutor them? HAMILTON: Actually, we did more going out than their coming in. We didn't have a place at the time. I mean, it wasn't like we had a, I mean . . . On a couple of occasions we'd have kids come over, really with, like, Big Brother, to the centers and sit at the  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  centers where there are some study-type areas, but, usually, we'd go to the church, and do it there. HEBERT: Do you think this role-model kind of thing's important for kids? HAMILTON: Oh, yes. HEBERT: Someone in college being a role-model. HAMILTON: Certainly. Can I have some water? HEBERT: There's a fountain [pause in tape]. . . Another community action type of a thing I noticed that Harambe did was to go in to some of the bars around campus and to desegregate them . . . HAMILTON: [077] Oh yes, that was hilarious. One of the things we were concerned about was that you could not drink or eat immediately around the campus, that they were subtly discriminating. Some overtly, but some subtly, against the students. So, I mean, white students could just go anywhere they want, but there were several places that just would not serve blacks, particularly the bars. And we did this, along with the Reveille, we were going to do a survey, and, let's see, me, Kerry Pourciau, who else was with us that night? Harold Deselle and Romel Wren were going to, and the Reveille reporters were going to follow us. Gosh, it was awful. We did several. I can't remember all of them, but I remember . . . HEBERT: The Cotton Club . . . HAMILTON: I remember the Cotton Club and the Pastime distinctly. The Cotton Club because I grew up right down the street from the Cotton Club, over on West Johnson, and I knew those people, Frankie and . . . God, I've forgotten his brother's name. I mean, I grew up around them. They knew my family, the whole thing. And I knew they discriminated. There wasn't any question about it. We went in there, and we sat the bar and they came in and sat over toward the side. We asked for a beer. Frankie looked at me and said, "I know you." "Yes, yes. I live right down the street over there." He said, "Yes, you're Hamilton. What are you doing here?" "I just want a beer. We just want to get a beer." He said, "You know you can't get a beer here." I said, "Well, what do you mean I can't get a beer? Sure I can get a beer."  2  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  He said, "You know that you have to go back there to get a beer." Because you'd have to go . . . There was this . . . You couldn't eat or drink there, but you could go to the liquor store, which was on the back, and buy beer. I said, "Well, no, we just want to sit in here and get a beer." And it just kind of went on and then he said, "I can't believe you're here." Then, it dawned on him because the camera guy got up and took a picture. It dawned on him what was going on. He said, "I can't believe you're doing this to me, Hamilton. You've lived here all your life. I mean, why would you create these problems?" I said, "Look, Frankie, I'm not trying to create any problems. The problem is you're creating problems. You're wrong. You can't discriminate." We went round and round and round and round, and he was getting upset. His brother was a real ass, was getting, and he came over and was really being obnoxious. And I think one of the guys from the law school was there. I can't remember who it was. But his brother had called the police to come throw us out. We hadn't done anything, and we were going to sit there, just . . . So the police came in and we went round and round and round and round and ultimately the police said, "Well, you know you, the man, it's his private property. He wants you to leave." I was like, "Hey! We're going to leave but you can't throw us out." Then we got in this big row with the police about, "Well, we can throw you out." I was like, "Wait, wait. Let's go." So we eventually left. The other place we had a real rough time was the Pastime . . . HEBERT: Is that the place out on Nicholson? HAMILTON: Yes. It's on the corner of Nicholson and South. We stepped in, and it's kind of, almost like the Pastime. Big Tiger place. Big, big Tiger football fans. We went in there, and it's kind of dark, pinball machines and stuff. We sat down, and the same type thing. The guy gets in the corner. I ordered a beer. Except it's a different kind of crowd in there at night, now. Kind of more rustic crowd. HEBERT: It's not the football crowd.  3  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HAMILTON: No, no, no, no, no. Certainly not the LSU crowd. I mean, it's still an LSU crowd, but not the university staff type, I mean, more of a Baton Rouge type. They were like, "No." And guy said, no, Harold asked for a beer, and the guy just looked at him and he said, "No." So he turned around and walked away. I said, "Maybe you didn't hear him. We just want a beer." He said, "You must not be from here, huh?" "Yes, I live right down Nicholson Drive right there." "I said, you must not be from here." I was like, "No. I am from here. But, you know, you can't not get me a beer." By this time, there was another guy coming from the other end of the bar, and this guy was about to come across the bar. And the Reveille guy took his picture, and they blew up. I mean, they absolutely blew up and just ranted and raved and were calling the police, and . . . we had to leave out of there. Two or three other places we tried but they weren't as . . . Eventually, well, there might have been one that gave us a beer, but the others just said no. You know, just, "No. We don't serve you." HEBERT: Was there any desire on your part to file suit against these places for not serving you? HAMILTON: Sure. We were going to do it. HEBERT: Did it happen? HAMILTON: [143] No, actually it didn't happen because we wound up working with Ben Shieber at the law school and one other professor over there to . . . and after the article came out, the Morning Advocate came out and did a survey, too, and eventually convinced them, "Hey, you-all are going to lose this lawsuit. If these people file suit against you, you better serve them. You got to change your policy. If you serve food, if you do all the other things, I'm sorry, you got to serve them!" There were several, Shieber particularly, but several members of the law school, who helped us go back and meet with those people and convinced them that their policies were wrong. If someone came in and asked, they were going to have to serve them. We went back on a couple of times, and they served us.  4  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: So there was no problem after that. HAMILTON: No. I mean, and a couple of them, even after we talked with them, they explained, "Look, I mean, even if I don't have anything against you . . . " blah, blah, blah, I mean, that's a damn lie. Of course, you don't want me in your bar. "My business is going to go off . . ." Hey! I'm sorry. I mean, the bottom line is, nobody is going to run in here to hang out with a bunch of people who don't want them in here anyway. HEBERT: Right. HAMILTON: "I mean, I'm not going to come in here every damn night. I just want to know that if I came in here I'm going to be treated properly." And, I think once everybody kind of got that across and got the rough edges out of the way, it worked out. HEBERT: You never had any desire to try to integrate the places west of the, on the west side of the river? HAMILTON: Oh, hell no! You'd get killed! HEBERT: No, I just heard Kerry Pourciau tell a story about how he went to one of those places one night. HAMILTON: Yes, he's been over there. No! No! No, no, no, no, no. Never wanted to do that! You could get hurt, obviously. The police department in Baton Rouge, the fact that these establishments that we were dealing with were closely aligned with the university, that there were people who were frequent patrons from the university, hell no, they weren't going to do that. I mean they might have gotten a bit, like the guy who was about to come over the table, he might have been an asshole for whatever, but . . . HEBERT: But you didn't think you were going to get beaten up or anything in those places . . . HAMILTON: But we didn't think that we'd get mobbed. I mean, you might have one or two people who might come across, and you'd have to deal with them. But . . . we knew the Reveille was right there, once the camera began to roll, these guys were not going to do anything. They were going to be mad, but they were going to recognize that that's the press, and they weren't going to want to be on the front page of the paper beating up a bunch of students over something like that. In Port Allen, or one of those other places over there, it'd never be in the newspaper. The camera would be smashed, and we'd be in the hospital. HEBERT: If not dead. HAMILTON: If not dead! So, no! I was, like, "I'm not going to do that. I'm not stupid!"  5  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: How did the Baton Rouge Police Department treat black students, or black people in general in Baton Rouge? HAMILTON: Oh, awful! Absolutely awful! No question about it. The Baton Rouge Police Department was awful, racist. There were no blacks on the police department. I mean, I can remember when the first guy came on the force, which would have been, '67, '68, one or two. Other than that, there were no blacks on the police department. They were very, very . . . I mean, hey, you . . . They were mean! Shucks, they'd hit you over the head or whatever the devil, and, no, you didn't want to deal with police. HEBERT: What about campus security? How did they treat black students on campus? I've read some cases where they would harass black men walking on campus at night. HAMILTON: Yes. They would harass you. I mean, "What are you doing out walking around on the campus? First of all, you're not supposed to be out here walking around." HEBERT: Even if you were a student. HAMILTON: "How do I know you're a student?" I mean, once you, you had to prove you were, and even after you proved that you were, you had to go through the hassle of them berating you or really just harassing you because you were out and about. Because, I mean, it was, and I mean, this is . . . I think, a reflection as much of the caliber of people who were on the force. I look at Campus Police in those days as being generally a place to hide for a guy who couldn't be on the Sheriff's Department, the Baton Rouge Police Department, or any other legitimate law enforcement thing. But he wanted to have a uniform and a gun, if possible. They really just weren't very good, and there was a lot of racism. HEBERT: What about the Ganch and Rando case. I don't know if I'm pronouncing it correctly. It's the, two black people who were on the Parade Grounds and one was, they were charged with indecent exposure or something like that. HAMILTON: I really don't remember. HEBERT: And arrested. There was a big protest about their arrest. HAMILTON: I really don't remember it. HEBERT: This was in the, in '72 or so. HAMILTON: I really don't remember it. I really don't. I hate getting up. Let me, I should get a glass of water. [pause in tape] HEBERT: What other kinds of community type work did Harambe do? With this was past, this was after the voter registration.  6  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HAMILTON: [214] We did do some voter registration though. We did those types of things, but mostly, really, what we were concerned about were education type issues, children. Focusing on, like the Big Brother type programs. Getting young black kids off the streets and focused toward black pride and that whole awareness thing. We worked with the NAACP on some voter registration projects. We didn't, we tried to steer clear of partisan politics other than working with the whole idea of getting more blacks elected to office. HEBERT: So you'd support black candidates if they were running for office? Or . . . HAMILTON: Sometimes. Sometimes. But, I mean, we would not get in-, I mean we didn't want to get involved in picking candidates over, one over the other. We wanted to get involved in getting black people running and getting black people elected, but we wouldn't want to say we're going to support Joe Blow over the other guy. We didn't want to do that. It just, it's a bad way to do it. You just get, you lose control over it, first of all, and you make those decisions on issues that may or may not be supportable. It's just a real problem to do that, so we tried not to do that. HEBERT: Let's talk about campus again. What about David Duke on campus? What was he like? We talked a little about him last time . . . HAMILTON: He was a real jerk. But, I mean . . . HEBERT: Did you ever listen to him at Free Speech Alley and participate in that? HAMILTON: Discussions and arguments and . . . HEBERT: When he was there. HAMILTON: [241] I didn't. Certainly other people did. But, I didn't. I believed then, and I believe now, that you're not going to change him. There are those people who are going to agree with him, and there's not much you're going to do about them other than prove they're wrong and just be who you are. I never thought he was very effective. I mean, if I thought he was effective, I probably would have done more. I just never thought he was very effective. The times were against him, we felt. So he was not . . . In fact, he's much more effective today than he was then. Much more so. People laughed at him. I mean, he was a jerk and he ran around saying things that were offensive but . . . HEBERT: His following wasn't very large. HAMILTON: But his following wasn't very big, so you never felt he was a major concern. HEBERT: You were still here when Kerry Pourciau ran for SGA President.  7  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HAMILTON: Oh, for sure. HEBERT: Did Harambe support him and push for his election? HAMILTON: Yes, yes. HEBERT: Was, did you believe that he could win the election or was it just, we're going to run, Kerry's going to run and . . . HAMILTON: [260] Well, actually, the, I believed he could win, clearly. I thought he could win because the timing was right and because Kerry had aligned himself with a number of, a sufficient number of organizations. We knew it would be tough. Actually, his most difficult group to get support from was Harambe. Again, because during that period the group was going through that transition where he was viewed as being more white than black or not in support of the black agenda, the black pride agenda. He was more of an integrationist when the movement was pushing toward this cultural identity separatist thing, beginnings. There were some people who were younger, I mean, we were in our, what, senior, junior/senior class, and you're talking about the freshman/sophomore classes of blacks who would prefer to just not vote for him at all. So really we had a hard time. We supported him, and we worked our tails off to get black people to not be apathetic and to, one, realize he could win, and two, it's not enough to say that he doesn't agree with you. It's just not enough. So, yes, we worked for him and supported him. He was a friend; he's still a friend. HEBERT: Who were some of the white students or professors that were opposed to having black students on campus? Were there any leaders that were just completely opposed to having black students on campus and treated . . . HAMILTON: [285] I don't think there was any university official who ever officially took a position that they didn't want black students on campus and worked toward getting them off. Not during my tenure. Not that I met or anything like that. There were people who you felt uncomfortable around and who you thought preferred you . . . I mean, I thought Dean Reddoch could give a damn about black people being on campus. But, I don't think there was ever an effort by him or anyone else to ensure that there would be no black people on campus, or that they failed. You know, I don't think that. HEBERT: So you never had a professor that you felt was . . . HAMILTON: Oh, not like in, not like in high school. No. Not like in high school. I had professors who I felt . . . I felt, through what they did and how they acted, believed I could never make it. Believed I probably couldn't compete and shouldn't be allowed to compete, but they didn't do anything that openly . . . I don't think there was anyone who was unprofessional enough to not do his job.  8  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  I think that what we experienced and what I had people discuss with me, more than anything else, was the unwillingness to help or the or the or the belief that this person would do something more for a white student than they'd do for a black student. But I don't think anyone had an example of a teacher who was unprofessional enough at the college level to openly discriminate against them. I really don't think that occurred. It didn't occur to me. It did not occur to me. HEBERT: Who was your favorite teacher while you were here? Did you have one professor that stood out above the others? HAMILTON: I had a couple of them that I really liked. Jim Bowlner was really one of my favorite professors. HEBERT: Why was that? HAMILTON: [315] Political Science? Because I thought he was a good professor and he was he was entertaining. He was very, very good, and very entertaining. Dr. Timmons, I never had him as a teacher, but I was on a number of committees and things with him, and I just thought he was one of the finest people I ever worked around. Oh gosh, Dr. Eubanks . . . HEBERT: Did Harambe have a faculty advisor type person? Did you have to have an alignment with a faculty member? Or . . . HAMILTON: Well, there weren't any black faculty members. HEBERT: So there was no . . . HAMILTON: So, no. HEBERT: No sponsor or that kind of thing? HAMILTON: No. Not at all. There was no faculty member. No one wanted to adopt us. Now we were assigned to work with, after we had all those meetings with the chancellor's office, we were eventually assigned to work with Don Tarver on a number of things. But we never had a faculty advisor in those days because who wanted to be the faculty advisor for Harambe? You know, there wasn't any black faculty, so no! HEBERT: Did you-all get a black history course or any of those things that you asked for? HAMILTON: Eventually. God, I can't remember the man's name. Yes, we did, before I left the campus. There were two courses and I cannot remember the professor's name. I really can't. Gosh.  9  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: Did Harambe become the Black Action Movement later on? Did it die out? HAMILTON: [341] It died out, I know that. But, you know, after I got to law school, I wasn't as active in it. I don't know what . . . In fact, I wasn't active at all after I went to law school. I didn't have time for it. So I really don't know what happened. And like most things, I'm sure it died out as much because of leadership changes as much as the fact that the need changed and other organizations developed. The idea that there were black fraternities and sororities. You know, all those things made a difference. HEBERT: Do you think changing attitudes, societal, society's attitudes, also, contributed to that where black students were more accepted on campus as time went on? HAMILTON: I'd like to think that. Although I work now with, I have worked with Leadership LSU for three of the last four years, where the black students really talk about the same problems we talked about. Very much so. Still . . . I was surprised. HEBERT: Well, I know, I walk down through the quad sometimes at lunch time and the groups aren't integrated; there are groups of black kids together and groups of white kids together and there's never any mingling. HAMILTON: Yes, I was really surprised. HEBERT: That surprises me, too. HAMILTON: [360] Yes, I was absolutely surprised at the things people were telling me they were concerned about or the racism that they saw. In fact, it sounded worse. It sounded worse. It sounded as if it were more subtle and alarming to me, really. It was much more alarming because at least when I was in school, even though I thought it was blatant in a number of ways, for some people, I didn't ever have the feeling, I didn't ever have the feeling that the majority wanted it to be that way. You had the feeling that the majority were merely indifferent and were going to allow these other people to do things to you. You never had the feeling that the majority were against you. They just didn't give a damn about you, and wished you weren't there so these other people can be jerks. You get, in talking to these people, you get the impression that the group of jerks is bigger than it was then. I mean, really, it's, amazingly as that sounds, what we were upset about was, as much as anything else, never feeling a part of it. People never let you feel a part of it. That meant the people who openly hated you or the people who didn't. They just wished you weren't there. Here, when I talk to these people, it's like there's a real hate, or a real dislike. It was interesting to me. It was disappointing, but it was really interesting to me that that's the way it was.  10  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: Have you kept active with the Alumni Association and those kinds of things? HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: Are you part of the Tureaud chapter? HAMILTON: Oh yes, I was president one year. HEBERT: Oh really. Now after you got your bachelor's degree from LSU, you went on to law school here. Was there any thought of you going to somewhere else, another law school, or did you want to go to LSU Law School? HAMILTON: I wanted to go to school here. I'm, sorry, I'm a Tiger fan. HEBERT: You're a Tiger fan. HAMILTON: [401] I like it. I wanted to stay in Louisiana, and I thought the best place to practice, to go to law school if you're going to stay in the state is LSU. HEBERT: What was law school like? HAMILTON: Awful. I liked college until law school. HEBERT: Well, it's kind of like graduate school, I think it's just constant work. HAMILTON: It's awful. HEBERT: Do you think getting a degree from LSU has helped you, in your career? HAMILTON: Yes. Sure. I really do. I mean, I don't think that it can be dismissed that if you're in the state, and you're going to be successful in the state that you have some connexity with this university. I believe that. I think that's a contributing factor. I mean, not that you can't be, but it certainly helps. HEBERT: So, I read somewhere that during the '80s you were the assistant secretary to the La. Department of Labor? HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: Who did you work for in that capacity? HAMILTON: Phyllis Mouton was the secretary, Roemer was the governor. HEBERT: How did you get that position? Was it appointed?  11  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HAMILTON: Yes. I know enough people and worked for the State Legislature for a number of years and worked for a labor organization, American Federation of State, County, and Municipal Employees for five and a half years, and concentrated on labor law, that's my area. HEBERT: Did you always work for the firm that you work for now? HAMILTON: Three years. HEBERT: Okay. So did you start out with a firm or did you start out on your own? HAMILTON: [428] Actually I started out with the state. Started with the State Senate. Then . . . No, actually I started with AFSCME, I started with them, then went to the State Senate, practiced with a smaller firm, Blache, Perkins, and Koch, K-O-C- H, and then moved to Breazeale, Sachse, and Wilson. HEBERT: Did you feel you were a path breaker, or you were breaking new ground, going out and working as a black attorney in this city or . . . HAMILTON: God, I never felt that way. I mean, people tell you that. I never felt that way. I, and I don't think that there are only a couple, well, I can't say I don't think, there were a couple of times that I did something with the intent of . . . not necessarily being the first black but being, having black people involved in something. There were a couple of times I did something with that in mind, that black people should be involved in this area, and damn it, I'm going to do it. HEBERT: Such as? HAMILTON: The labor movement. When I worked for AFSCME, I mean, I was always interested. My dad was a labor union member and I was interested in the labor movement, but I was always concerned that the labor movement is, and was then, and to a certain degree now, very racist, and very concerned with wanting to get in the labor movement and move black people to the front, to the forefront. That's why I went to work with AFSCME. HEBERT: How is that, A-S . . . HAMILTON: A-F-S-C-M-E. The American Federation of State, County, and Municipal Employees. It's one of the largest labor unions in the country. I liked the idea that it was public sector, that it was basically white and blue collar, not just the blue collar. It's much more difficult at the blue collar level. And I liked the history of that union, from what I had read. The history of the union was really interesting. In Louisiana, there had been significant efforts to integrate the public sector. Suits had to be filed, and what I had learned about that union was that as that union became, as the public sector became more integrated, and the union became more integrated, it became less representative. That is, it became less responsive to those people. And I wanted to be a part of making it more  12  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  responsive, and I recognized that the labor movement was a significant part of the politics of the state at the time, particularly the Democratic Party. I really felt like, "Hey, we were not, as a race, taking advantage of that and not making the labor movement become more responsive." I wanted to do something about it. So that was a conscious effort. In moving from the Department of Labor to the practice with Blache, Perkins, and Koch, we were making a conscious effort to have an integrated law firm. We had Johnny Koch, who was a very good friend. Well, he was a very good friend of all of ours, and Johnny and I have been friends for years. We went to school together. But C. J. Blache is from New Orleans, Tom Perkins from New Orleans, but both had gone to college out-of-state. Tommy had gone to Harvard. Blache, C. J. had gone to LeMoyne, and we were conscious . . . So we had all gone to integrated colleges and universities. We were conscious of wanting to integrate. And they'd gone to Loyola Law School, so we'd all gone to . . . in integrated environments. We were conscious of the problems in the legal profession and the real segregation within the legal profession and the result, the resulting loss of opportunity for minority attorneys. We knew Johnny and Johnny . . . We had worked together on some things, it was like, "Hey! Let's have an integrated law firm." And we did. So we integrated a law firm. We not only were integrated but we were going to use the integrated environment to work in different areas because, principally, black attorneys were, or still are, either criminal lawyers or plaintiff's attorneys, personal injury, plaintiff's attorneys. None of us liked that. I mean, first of all, none of us wanted to do either of those things. None of us wanted to be criminal lawyers and none of us wanted to do plaintiff, personal injury work. C. J. really, is a lobbyist. Tommy likes defense work and commercial regulations. You know, I like labor work. You know, Johnny likes lobbying and commercial regulations. So, it was an opportunity to have a firm which was integrated that would move into other areas. We were, I think, successful for a short period of time. We wanted to take the next step, which was moving into a larger firm, and integrating a larger firm in Baton Rouge. Breazeale, Sachse, and Wilson is the oldest firm in town, sixty years old. They were prepared to make that move, too. The new leadership of that firm, the managing partners, wanted to move into integrating the legal profession, as well as wanting to move into several areas of the law that we had expertise in. "It was like, okay." And it worked well. It worked where the personalities were such that we were, we have been able to make it work so far, so we joined that firm. HEBERT: So the two combined. Or you, your firm combined with . . . HAMILTON: Yes. Right. Well, I mean . . . HEBERT: Or joined . . . HAMILTON: They bought us out is what they did!  13  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: They bought us out. HAMILTON: I mean, a merger of sorts, but, yes. So it works, it worked in that sense, yes. But, when you talk about path- . . . I mean, other than doing that . . . HEBERT: And that was a conscious effort, too. HAMILTON: [555] Yes, yes. I guess, maybe even going to LSU. But, I mean, I went to, I was a, I went to Lee High School because my parents made me do it. I went to LSU as much because I grew up in the shadow of the school as much as because I wanted to go integrate the school. I mean, that was on my mind, sure. But . . . HEBERT: And when you went to law school, that, it was just you wanted to go to LSU Law School or was it . . . HAMILTON: I wanted to go to LSU Law School, and I wanted to go to an integrated law school. And . . . HEBERT: And the law school had been integrated for thirty years by that time. HAMILTON: It had been integrated, yes, no matter how slight that may have been. HEBERT: How many other black students were in law school at the time you were there? HAMILTON: There were . . . four? Maybe five. HEBERT: Was it the same kind of . . . HAMILTON: Tyson, Pattan, David, David Prejean. God, I've forgotten the other guy's name. Billy . . ., [and] Cannon. Seven. Because there was one other person I can't think of the name. Seven of them in the whole school. There were eight in my class and of my class, three of us, maybe . . . HEBERT: Was it the same kind of camaraderie between the black law students as there had been among . . . HAMILTON: Yes. HEBERT: Did you feel more accepted in law school? HAMILTON: No. HEBERT: No. It was the same kind of feeling that you had?  14  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HAMILTON: Yes. Exactly. Maybe more so. Maybe more so. Yes, I think more so. But, certainly, no feeling of acceptance. Not at all. So it was the same game. HEBERT: Are there any law school professors that stand out in your mind as being outstanding or . . . HAMILTON: [598] I suppose, my most, the law school professor I liked the most would be Ben Shieber. I liked him personally. I liked, he's in an area of the law that I like, so, it helped. HEBERT: You had contact with him before going to law school. HAMILTON: Yes, and I knew him before going to law school. In terms of people who I thought were just excellent teachers? Oh, several excellent, excellent teachers. People I liked, Paul Beyer [?] I like. But I think Austin Johnson, I think, even though I think he has a personality problem, but I think he's, clearly, a brilliant man. Mr. Litvinoff, Dr., Professor Litvinoff. Gosh, I, you know, you can't think of all the names. Pugh, who I really enjoyed. He was a delightful man, as well as a good professor. Mr. Pascal, Robert Pascal, Professor Pascal, who I thought was a brilliant person but a real weird guy. HEBERT: I had a friend who had him . . . HAMILTON: My very first day in law school I had, my very first day, very first class, Professor Pascal, and he's quite a character. Quite a character. But I thought he was a brilliant man. And, clearly, thought that he was a professional, that it didn't matter whether you were black, white, or green, you know, he was going to handle you the same way. No question about it. I didn't get the impression that there were, any of my teachers that treated me differently because I was black. HEBERT: It was more the students who were the ones who kept the distance. HAMILTON: Right. Yes. Right. Much, much more so the students. But I never had the impression that any of my professors, and that's not to say they were or they weren't, I don't know. That's the good thing. I thought they were professional. And I thought they did their jobs as well as they could. HEBERT: Is there anything that you wish LSU would have given to you that it didn't? That, as a whole, during your whole career here, that it didn't? HAMILTON: Gosh, that's hard to say. Because . . . I mean, when you say, "That LSU had given you," I suspect you mean the LSU experience. HEBERT: The LSU experience, that's exactly what I mean. HAMILTON: And the LSU experience, it gave me a great deal.  15  Hamilton, Leo - Tape 471, Side B  HEBERT: That was my next question. HAMILTON: I mean . . . It gave me a great deal of frustration, but, at the same time, it gave me a great deal, feeling of accomplishment. Disappointment, but, at the same time I had a marvelous time. HEBERT: I have to . . .  16														Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections, LOUISiana Digital Library, Baton Rouge, La. Transcript Hamilton 472A										Narrator: Leo Hamilton 4700.0322 Interviewer: Mary Hebert Session II Transcriber: Robin Cate 11 September 1993 [End Tape 471, Side B. Begin Tape 472, Side A.] HEBERT: Okay. So you had a marvelous time at LSU? HAMILTON: I mean, I had a marvelous time, despite the problems. HEBERT: Well, last time you mentioned that it wasn't all bad, and . . . HAMILTON: Well, it wasn't. I mean, I had a marvelous time. I mean, clearly, I had some bad times, but I met some interesting and wonderful people. People who are my friends today. I learned a great deal about life and about myself, which I think the college experience is supposed to give you. I mean, I'm more concerned that the university, from where I sit today, has not progressed far enough beyond where it was then, than I am that I did not get enough from the university experience. I got a significant amount from it and, I mean, I'd do it again. No question about it. HEBERT: Well, that's about all I had to cover. Is there anything that I've left out that you'd like to talk about? Have I left out anything about your LSU experience, or . . . HAMILTON: No. HEBERT: Okay. HAMILTON: No. [End Tape 472, Side A. End Session II.]														Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections, LOUISiana Digital Library, Baton Rouge, La.	E-mail lsudiglib@lsu.edu with questions or comments. Leo Hamilton oral history interview, 1993	Hamilton, Leo C.	1993			Hebert, Mary J.	Louisiana State University (Baton Rouge, La.)--Students.; Southern University and A & M College--Students.; African American children--Education--Louisiana--Baton Rouge.; Segregation in education--Louisiana--Baton Rouge.; Discrimination in education--Louisiana--Baton Rouge.; African American college students--Louisiana--Baton Rouge--Political activity.; Civil rights movements--United States.; Baton Rouge (La.)--Race relations.	Hamilton discusses his family, his segregated early education, his attendance at an integrated Robert E. Lee High School, race relations in Baton Rouge, the civil rights movement, activism among black students at LSU, the relationship between black students at LSU and Southern University, and his legal practice.	Interview conducted by Mary J. Hebert.	en		Special Delivery: A Showcase of LSU Libraries Special Collections	T. Harry Williams Center for Oral History	T.
